The Linguistic Roots of Al-Ariyah and Its Legal Implications
Explains etymology and placement of the book on loans

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three proposed etymologies of 'ariyah, identify the correct one according to Hanafi scholars, and explain why this derivation affects whether a loan of consumables is considered ariyah or qard.

Core Idea
The word 'ariyah derives from alternation (ta'awur), not shame – this determines when a loan becomes qard.

Before diving into the legal rulings of lending (al-ariyah), the Hanafi jurists first examine the word itself. Why is it placed after deposits? What does its root mean? The answers shape key fiqh distinctions, especially the rule that lending consumable goods like wheat is actually a qard (loan for consumption), not a true ariyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the placement reasoning
The text explains that the Book of Ariyah is placed after Wadi'ah because ariyah involves both tamlik (ownership of benefit) and ida' (deposit), making it a compound concept. The simpler concept (wadi'ah) precedes the compound.
2
Examine the first etymology (from 'ar 'shame')
Some claim the word derives from 'ar (shame), implying borrowing is disgraceful. The text rejects this on two grounds: morphological (the root letter is waw, not ya) and Prophetic practice (the Prophet himself borrowed).
3
Examine the accepted etymology (from ta'awur 'alternation')
The correct derivation is from ta'awur, meaning alternation. The word indicates that the benefit of the item alternates between the lender and borrower: the borrower uses it, then returns it to the lender.
This is the third proposed etymology in the text.
4
Apply the etymology to fiqh
Because ta'awur implies the return of the same specific item, if the item is consumed (like food or money), the benefit cannot be returned in kind. Therefore, such transactions are not true ariyah but rather qard (loan of fungibles), where the borrower guarantees return of similar items.
5
Note the refutation of the fourth etymology
A claim that the word is derived from 'ariyah (a specific case) is refuted by al-Mutarriz and others using morphological evidence from verb derivations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العارية
قوله : كتاب العارية ؛ ذكرها بعد الوديعة ؛ لاشتراكهما في الأمانة ، و أخرها ؛ لأن فيها تمليكاً وإيداعاً، فتكون من الوديعة بمنزلة المفرد من المركب، والمركب مؤخّر عن المفرد لتأخر الكلِّ عن الجزء ذاتاً ، والعارية بالتشديد، وقد تخفف.
قيل : هي منسوبة إلى العار ؛ لأنَّ طلبها عيب وعار، ويردّ عليه أنَّ العاريائي، والعارية واويّة على ما صرحوا به ، وأيضاً ثبت أنَّ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم باشر الاستعارة، فلو كان العار في طلبها لما باشر.
وقيل : منسوبة إلى العارة اسم من الإعارة.
وقيل : أن الياء فيه ليس بمعنى النسبة كالكرسي ، وهي مشتقة من التعاور، وهو التناوب، كأنه يجعل للغير نوبة في الانتفاع بملكه ، على أن تعود النوبة إليه بالاسترداد متى شاء ؛ ولهذا كانت الإعارة في المكيل والموزون قرضاً ؛ لأنه لا ينتفع بها إلا باستهلاك العين، فلا يعود النوبة إليه في ملك العين لتكون عارية حقيقة، وإنما تعود النوبة إليها في مثلها، وإنما يملك الانتفاع بها على أن تكون مضموناً عليها، فتكون قرضاً.
وقيل : مأخوذة من العرية، وهي القضية المخصوصة بالأعيان، ومستعملة في تلك المنافع ، وردّه المطرزي (١) ، وغيره بالمشتقات : استعاره منه فأعــاره ، واستعاره الشيء على حذف من (٢).
(The Book of Al-Ariyah) His statement: The Book of Al-Ariyah; he mentioned it after Al-Wadi'ah due to their sharing in trust, and he delayed it because it involves both ownership and deposit, so it is to Al-Wadi'ah as the simple [word] to the compound, and the compound is delayed after the simple due to the whole being later than the part in essence. Al-Ariyah is with tashdid (double 'r') and may be with takhfif (single 'r'). It is said: it is derived from 'ar (shame) because requesting it is a defect and shame. But this is refuted because [the word] is ya'i (with ya) while al-ariyah is wawiy (with waw) as they clarified. Also, it is established that the Messenger of Allah practiced borrowing, so if there were shame in requesting it, he would not have done it. It is said: it is derived from al-'arah, a noun from al-i'arah. It is said: the ya in it is not for nisbah (relation) like kursiyy; it is derived from al-ta'awur, which is alternation, as if he makes for the other a turn in benefiting from his property, on condition that the turn returns to him by retrieval whenever he wishes. Hence, lending in weighed and measured items is qard (loan for consumption) because he cannot benefit from them except by consuming the thing itself, so the turn does not return to him in ownership of the thing itself to be a true ariyah; rather, the turn returns to him in its like, and he only owns the benefit on condition that it is guaranteed, thus it is qard. It is said: it is taken from al-'ariyah, which is the specific case of assets, and used in those benefits. But al-Mutarriz and others refuted it with derivatives: 'he borrowed from him' so 'he lent him', and 'he borrowed the thing' with the omission of min.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and structural introduction to the Book of Al-Ariyah in the Hanafi fiqh manual. The author first explains why the topic of loans (al-ariyah) is placed after deposits (al-wadi'ah): both involve trust (amanah), but loans additionally involve ownership (tamlik) of the usufruct, making it a compound of deposit and ownership. The simple comes before the compound. Then he examines the etymology of the word 'ariyah. The first proposed derivation from 'ar (shame) is rejected on two grounds: the word's root letters (it is wawiy, whereas shame is ya'i) and because the Prophet himself borrowed. Second, it is derived from i'arah (lending). Third, it is derived from ta'awur (alternation), reflecting that the benefit alternates between lender and borrower. This third view is taken as the basis for the rule that lending consumable items (like wheat) is actually qard (loan) not ariyah, because the benefit cannot be returned in the same form. Finally, a derivation from 'ariyah (a specific case) is refuted by al-Mutarriz using morphological evidence. The discussion establishes the precise technical meaning before the legal definition.

Key Terms
عارية (ʿāriyah): A loan of a tangible item for temporary benefit without compensation.
إعارة (iʿārah): The act of lending; the verbal noun from a'ara.
تعاور (taʿāwur): Alternation; the root meaning of 'ariyah indicating that benefit alternates between lender and borrower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the book of al-ariyah placed after al-wadi'ah according to the text?
A) Because both are trusts.
B) Because ariyah involves both tamlik and ida', so it is compound and thus delayed.
C) Because ariyah is simpler than wadi'ah.
D) Because wadi'ah is more important.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic derivation of 'ariyah from ta'awur (alternation) directly impacts the fiqh classification: loans of non-consumables are ariyah, while loans of consumables become qard.

Legal Definition and Proofs of Al-Ariyah
Establishes legal definition, proofs, and the tamlik vs ibahah dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ariyah, cite its three proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma), and distinguish the Hanafi view of tamlik manfa'ah from the alternative view of ibahah.

Core Idea
Ariyah is ownership of benefit (tamlik manfa'ah) without compensation, not mere permission.

This lesson examines the legal concept of ariyah (loan) in the Hanafi school. We begin with the precise definition, then trace its proofs from the primary sources, and finally analyze the key dispute over whether ariyah transfers ownership of the benefit or merely grants permission to use. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the lender-borrower relationship and liability rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its exclusions
Read the definition: 'tamlik manfa'ah bi la badal' (ownership of benefit without compensation). Then note the four things excluded: qard, sale, gift, and lease. Understand why each is excluded based on the presence or absence of compensation and transfer of the 'ayn.
The definition is precise; each word has a purpose.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
Examine the verse 'wa yamna'oona al-ma'un' (107:7). Understand that 'ma'un' is interpreted as items commonly lent. The verse censures preventing such loans, implying lending is praiseworthy.
The dalala (indication) is zanni (interpretive) even though the text is qat'i al-thubut.
3
Analyze the Sunnah proofs
Consider the two hadiths: (1) The Prophet borrowed a horse from Abu Talhah, and (2) He borrowed armor from Safwan ibn Umayyah. Both establish the permissibility and praiseworthiness of lending.
Both are authentic and indicate the Prophet's practice.
4
Note consensus and the dispute on recommendation vs. obligation
The ummah agrees on permissibility. The majority hold it is mustahabb (recommended), while some say wajib. This does not affect the definition or validity.
The moral merit of lending is highlighted by the concept of deputizing for Allah in answering the distressed.
5
Distinguish the Hanafi tamlik view from the ibahah view
The Hanafi school holds that ariyah is tamlik manfa'ah (ownership of benefit). Al-Karkhi (Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i hold it is ibahah (permission). Trace the linguistic argument: the word 'ista'ara' suggests tamlik. Understand the practical consequence: if tamlik, the borrower has a stronger right and may be liable for destruction even without transgression; if ibahah, liability only with transgression.
The mukhtalaf fih is the very nature of the contract, not merely a secondary ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الشرع ما ذكره المصنف له بقوله : هي تمليك ... الخ.
ومشروعيتها تثبت بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع :
أما الكتاب : فقوله : وَيَمْنَعُونَ الْمَاعُونَ ) (۳)، والماعون ما يتعاورونه في العادة ، وقيل : الزكاة، فقد ذم الله تعالى على منع الماعون الذي هو عدم إعارته ، فتكون إعارته محمودة.

وأما السنّة : فما رواه البخاري عن أنس بن مالك : أنه قال : «كان فزع بالمدينة فاستعار النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فرساً من أبي طلحة يقال له : المندوب فركبه فلما رجع قال : ما رأينا من شيء وإن وجدناه البحراً» (١) ، أو ما أخرجه أبو داود له من أنه : «استعار في غزوة حنين دروعاً من صفوان بن أمية»(۳).

وأما الإجماع : فإنَّ الأمة اجتمعت على جوازها، وإنما اختلفوا في كونها مستحبّة، وهو قول الأكثر ، أو واجبة وهو قول البعض.

ومن محاسنها : النيابة عن الله عز وجل في إجابة المضطر ؛ لأنها لا تكون إلا لمحتاج كالقرض ، فالمستعير مضطر ، وقال سبحانه جل شأنه : أَمَنْ يُجِيبُ الْمُضْطَرَّ إِذَا دَعَاهُ ) (۳) ، والمعير قد أعانه، فكأنه ناب عن الله تعالى في إعانته ، وإن كان فعل المعير من الله تعالى فلا ينوبه في الحقيقة، ففاعلها قد تخلّق بهذا الخلق ، وورد : تخلقوا بأخلاق الله. وركنها : الإيجاب والقبول.
وشرطها : القبض، كما صرح به في «المضمرات».

[١] قوله : هي تمليك منفعة ... الخ ؛ يعني أن العارية تمليك منفعة من عين مع بقائها بلا عوض، فاحترز بـه عـن قـرض نحو الدراهم ، وعن البيع والهبة والإجارة ، فإنَّ في قرض الدراهم ونحوها لا يبقى العين على حالها ، وفي البيع : لا يكون تمليك المنفعة بل تمليك العين بعوض، وفي الهبة بلا عوض ، وفي الإجارة يكون تمليك المنفعة بعوض وهو الأجرة.

[٢] قوله : فإن اللفظ ... الخ ؛ يعني وجه اعتبار التمليك في تعريف العارية إشعار اللفظ بالتمليك، وفيه تعريض على البعض، وهو الكرخي له فإنّه يقول : هي الانتفاع بملك الغير، وبه قال الشافعي له.
And in law, it is what the author mentioned for it by his saying: 'It is tamlik... etc.' Its legitimacy is established by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book: His statement: 'And they prevent al-ma'un' (107:7). Al-ma'un is what they customarily lend to each other; some say it is zakat. So Allah censures preventing ma'un, which is not lending it, so lending it is praiseworthy. As for the Sunnah: what al-Bukhari narrated from Anas ibn Malik: 'There was a fright in Madinah, so the Prophet borrowed a horse from Abu Talhah called al-Mandub, rode it, and when he returned said: We saw nothing, and we found it like a sea.' Or what Abu Dawud narrated from him: 'He borrowed armor from Safwan ibn Umayyah during the Battle of Hunayn.' As for consensus: the ummah has agreed on its permissibility, though they differed on whether it is recommended (the majority view) or obligatory (the view of some). Among its virtues: deputizing for Allah in answering the distressed, for it is only given to a needy person like a loan; the borrower is distressed, and Allah says: 'Who answers the distressed when he calls?' (27:62). The lender has helped him, as if he deputized for Allah in aiding him, though the lender's act is from Allah so he does not really deputize; so the doer has taken on this trait, and it is reported: 'Adopt the traits of Allah.' Its pillar is offer and acceptance. Its condition is possession, as stated in al-Mudmarat. [1] His saying: 'It is tamlik manfa'ah... etc.' means that ariyah is ownership of benefit from a thing while it remains, without compensation. So he distinguished it from qard of dirhams etc., from sale, gift, and lease. In qard of dirhams and the like, the thing does not remain as it is; in sale, it is not ownership of benefit but ownership of the thing with compensation; in gift, without compensation; in lease, it is ownership of benefit with compensation, which is the rent. [2] His saying: 'For the word indicates tamlik... etc.' means the reason for considering tamlik in the definition of ariyah is that the word suggests tamlik. In this there is an allusion to some, namely al-Karkhi, who says: 'It is using another's property,' and al-Shafi'i said the same.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is the core legal definition of ariyah (loan) according to the Hanafi school. The author first presents the definition: 'It is tamlik manfa'ah bi la badal' (ownership of benefit without compensation). Then he proves its legitimacy from three sources: the Quran (the verse on ma'un), the Sunnah (the Prophet borrowing a horse and armor), and consensus (ijma on its permissibility, though some consider it recommended and others obligatory). He then mentions a moral merit: lending to the needy is like answering the distressed, a divine attribute. The pillars (rukn) are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qubul), and the condition (shart) is possession (qabd). The commentary that follows (marked [1] and [2]) explains the definition in detail. First, it clarifies that the benefit is from a specific thing that remains after use, distinguishing it from qard (consumption loan), sale (tamlik of the thing), gift (tamlik of things without compensation), and lease (tamlik of benefit with compensation). Second, it addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school and with al-Shafi'i: al-Karkhi (a Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i define ariyah as ibahah (mere permission to use) rather than tamlik ownership. The commentator argues that the word 'ista'ara' (borrow) itself implies tamlik. This is a key intra- and inter-madhhab difference; the relied-upon Hanafi position is tamlik.

Key Terms
تمليك منفعة (tamlik manfaʿah): Ownership of the benefit/usufruct of a thing without transferring ownership of the thing itself.
إباحة (ibāḥah): Permission to use; the view of al-Karkhi and al-Shafi'i that ariyah does not transfer ownership of the benefit but merely allows use.
قبض (qabḍ): Taking possession; a condition for the validity of the ariyah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the definition of ariyah?
A) Permission to use another's property.
B) Ownership of the item itself.
C) Ownership of benefit without compensation.
D) Rental of benefit with compensation.

Key Takeaway
Ariyah in the Hanafi school is defined as ownership of the benefit of a thing without compensation, and its legitimacy is established by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

The Nature of 'Ariyah: Tamlik vs Ibahah
Defines 'ariyah, provides proofs, and refutes ibahah view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'ariyah, explain the four objections of the ibahah school, and articulate the Hanafi responses to each objection.

Core Idea
The Hanafi 'ariyah is a transfer of usufruct, not mere permission—this determines liability and contractual rights.

The contract of 'ariyah (gratuitous loan for use) is widely practiced, but its legal nature has been disputed. Is the borrower merely permitted to use the item, or does he actually own the usufruct? The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, holds the latter. This lesson unpacks the definition, the proofs, and the rebuttal to the view that 'ariyah is only ibahah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi definition
Note the exact wording: 'It is transfer of usufruct without compensation' (هي تمليك منفعة بلا بدل). Understand that this distinguishes 'ariyah from sale (transfer of ownership with compensation), gift (transfer without compensation), and lease (transfer of usufruct with compensation).
The word 'ariyah itself implies a gift.
2
Identify the opposing view and its arguments
Al-Karkhi (Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i hold that 'ariyah is ibahah (mere permission). Their four arguments: (1) valid with word 'ibahah', (2) ignorance of duration tolerated, (3) invalidated by prohibition, (4) borrower cannot sublease.
These objections challenge the tamlik characterization.
3
Follow each objection and the Hanafi response
Work through each of the four objections individually. For each, read the opposing dalil and then the Hanafi counter-argument from the text.
Pay attention to the key concepts: metaphorical use of 'ibahah', non-bindingness, and harm prevention.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under tamlik, the borrower owns the usufruct and is liable for damage; under ibahah, he only has permission and may escape liability. Also, subleasing is prohibited in Hanafi to protect the lender.
This difference matters in dispute resolution.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi position
The Hanafi position (tamlik) is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi). The text's author affirms it through the four responses.
Al-Karkhi's view is a minority within the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي تمليك منفعة بلا بدل ، فإِنَّ اللفظ يُنْبِئُ عن التمليك
وأما السنّة : فما رواه البخاري عن أنس بن مالك : أنه قال : «كان فزع بالمدينة فاستعار النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فرساً من أبي طلحة يقال له : المندوب فركبه فلما رجع قال : ما رأينا من شيء وإن وجدناه البحراً» (١) ، أو ما أخرجه أبو داود له من أنه : «استعار في غزوة حنين دروعاً من صفوان بن أمية» (۳).
وأما الإجماع : فإنَّ الأمة اجتمعت على جوازها، وإنما اختلفوا في كونها مستحبّة، وهو قول الأكثر ، أو واجبة وهو قول البعض.
ومن محاسنها : النيابة عن الله عز وجل في إجابة المضطر ؛ لأنها لا تكون إلا لمحتاج كالقرض ، فالمستعير مضطر ، وقال سبحانه جل شأنه : {أَمَّن يُجِيبُ الْمُضْطَرَّ إِذَا دَعَاهُ} (۳) ، والمعير قد أعانه، فكأنه ناب عن ا الله تعالى في إعانته ، وإن كان فعل المعير من الله تعالى فلا ينوبه في الحقيقة، ففاعلها قد تخلّق بهذا الخلق ، وورد : تخلقوا بأخلاق الله. وركنها : الإيجاب والقبول.
وشرطها : القبض، كما صرح به في «المضمرات».
[١] قوله : هي تمليك منفعة ... الخ ؛ يعني أن العارية تمليك منفعة من عين مع بقائها بلا عوض، فاحترز بـه عـن قـرض نحو الدراهم ، وعن البيع والهبة والإجارة ، فإنَّ في قرض الدراهم ونحوها لا يبقى العين على حالها ، وفي البيع : لا يكون تمليك المنفعة بل تمليك العين بعوض، وفي الهبة بلا عوض ، وفي الإجارة يكون تمليك المنفعة بعوض وهو الأجرة.
إباحة
[٢] قوله : فإن اللفظ ... الخ ؛ يعني وجه اعتبار التمليك في تعريف العارية إشعار اللفظ بالتمليك، وفيه تعريض على البعض، وهو الكرخي له فإنّه يقول : هي الانتفاع بملك الغير، وبه قال الشافعي له.
فإن العرية : العطية
ووجه قوله : أن المعير إذا قال للمستعير: أبحتُ لك هذا الثوب مثلاً، تكون عارية، والتمليك لا ينعقد بلفظ الإباحة. ٢. وأنه لا يشترط في العارية إعلام مقدار المنفعة، ببيان المدة، والمنافع لا تصير معلومة إلا بذكر المدة، والتمليك لا يصح مع الجهالة كما ترى في الإجارة.
. وأن الإعارة تبطل بالنهي ، فلو كانت تمليكاً لما بطلت بالنهي كالهبة والإجارة.
ع . وأنَّ المستعير لا يملك الإجارة من غيره ومن ملك شيئاً بغير عوض يملك تمليكه من غيره بعوض وبغير عوض كالموهوب له.
وحاصل ما استدللنا به : أن لفظ العارية منبئ عن التمليك ؛ لكونها مشتقة من العربية بمعنى العطية؛ ولذا تنعقد العارية بلفظ التمليك ؛ فلذا قلنا : بأنها تمليك منفعة بلا بدل.
والجواب عن الوجه الأول : أنّ الإباحة قد تستعار للتمليك كما في الإجارة، ألا ترى أنها تنعقد بلفظ الإباحة، والحال أنها تمليك.
وعن الوجه الثاني : إنَّ الجهالة ليست بمانعة مطلقة، بل إذا كانت مفضية إلى النزاع، وهذه الجهالة ليست كذلك ؛ لعدم اللزوم، فلا تكون ضائرة؛ ولأن الملك لا يثبت في العارية إلا بالقبض، وهو الانتفاع ، وعند الانتفاع لا وجود للجهالة. وعن الوجه الثالث : أن عمل النهي ليس باعتبار أنه ليس في العارية تمليك، بل من حيث أنه بالنهي يمنع المستعير عن تحصيل المنافع التي لم يتملكها بعد، وله ذلك لكونها عقداً غير لازم، فكان له الرجوع أي وقت شاء. وعن الوجه الرابع : أنَّ المستعير لا يملك الإجارة لدفع زيادة الضرر بالمعير، والسر فيه : أن الإجارة إنّما وضعت في الشرع لازمة، وفي ذلك سد باب الاسترداد، فيتضرر به المعير.
It is transfer of usufruct without compensation. So the word indicates transfer. As for the Sunnah: what al-Bukhari narrated from Anas ibn Malik that he said: 'There was a panic in Medina, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) borrowed a horse from Abu Talha called al-Mandub, rode it, and when he returned he said: We have seen nothing, and we found it to be a sea (of speed).' Or what Abu Dawood narrated from him: 'He borrowed armor from Safwan ibn Umayyah during the Battle of Hunayn.' As for consensus: the ummah agreed on its permissibility, but they differed on whether it is recommended (the majority view) or obligatory (the view of some). Among its beautiful aspects: representation of Allah in answering the distressed; because it is not given except to someone in need, like a loan, so the borrower is distressed, and Allah said: 'Who answers the distressed when he calls upon Him?' And the lender has helped him, so he is as if he represented Allah in helping him, although the lender's act is from Allah so he does not truly represent Him. So the doer has adopted this trait, and it is reported: 'Adopt the traits of Allah.' Its pillar: offer and acceptance. Its condition: possession, as stated in al-Mudmarat. Footnote [1]: His saying: 'It is transfer of usufruct...' means that 'ariyah is transfer of usufruct from an entity with its remaining without compensation. So it distinguishes from a loan of dirhams etc., from sale, gift, and lease, because in loan of dirhams etc. the entity does not remain as it is, in sale there is not transfer of usufruct but transfer of ownership with compensation, in gift without compensation, and in lease transfer of usufruct with compensation which is the rent. Ibahah. Footnote [2]: His saying: 'So the word...' means the aspect of considering transfer in the definition of 'ariyah is the word's implication of transfer, and in it is an indirect criticism of some, namely al-Karkhi, who says it is using the property of another, and al-Shafi'i agrees. For 'ariyah is a gift. The proof of his (al-Karkhi's) statement: that if the lender says to the borrower: 'I permit you this garment,' it is a loan, and transfer does not take place with the word of permission. Second, that in 'ariyah it is not required to specify the amount of usufruct by stating the duration, and usufructs only become known by stating the duration, and transfer is not valid with ignorance as you see in lease. Third, that 'ariyah is invalidated by prohibition, so if it were transfer it would not be invalidated by prohibition like gift and lease. Fourth, that the borrower does not have the right to lease it to someone else, and whoever owns something without compensation has the right to transfer it to others with or without compensation like the donee. The conclusion of what we have argued: that the word 'ariyah indicates transfer because it is derived from 'ariyah meaning gift; therefore 'ariyah is concluded with the word of transfer; so we say it is transfer of usufruct without compensation. The answer to the first point: permission may be borrowed for transfer as in lease; do you not see that it is concluded with the word of permission while it is transfer? To the second point: ignorance is not an absolute preventer, but only when it leads to dispute, and this ignorance is not like that due to non-bindingness, so it is not harmful; and because ownership is only established in 'ariyah by possession, which is usufruct, and at the time of usufruct there is no ignorance. To the third point: the effect of prohibition is not because there is no transfer in 'ariyah, but because by prohibition the lender prevents the borrower from obtaining the usufructs he has not yet owned, and he has the right to do so because it is a non-binding contract, so he may revoke at any time. To the fourth point: the borrower does not have the right to lease to avoid increased harm to the lender; the reason is that lease is established in the Shari'ah as binding, and in that is closure of the door of revocation, causing harm to the lender.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the definition of 'ariyah (loan for use). The author begins by stating the Hanafi definition: 'It is transfer of usufruct without compensation' (تمليك منفعة بلا بدل). This definition is derived from the word itself, which implies transfer. The author then provides evidence from the Quran (the verse about answering the distressed), the Sunnah (the Prophet's borrowing a horse and armor), and the consensus of the ummah on its permissibility. The hadiths are authentic: the horse incident from Bukhari and the armor from Abu Dawood. The author notes a secondary dispute: whether 'ariyah is recommended (mustahabb, the majority view) or obligatory (wajib, some scholars). This, however, is not the main dispute.

The central dispute is about the legal nature of 'ariyah: is it a transfer of ownership of the usufruct (tamlik manfa'ah) as the Hanafis hold, or is it merely a permission (ibahah) to use the property, as held by al-Karkhi (a Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i? The author explains that the Hanafi position is based on the linguistic derivation: 'ariyah comes from 'ariyah meaning 'gift', which implies transfer. Then he lists four objections from the ibahah school:

1. The lender may use the word 'permission' (abahatu) and the contract is valid, but transfer cannot be done with the word 'permission'.
2. In 'ariyah, there is ignorance (jahalah) regarding the duration and extent of usufruct, and transfer cannot be valid with ignorance.
3. 'Ariyah is invalidated by the lender's prohibition (al-nahy), whereas transfer contracts like gift and lease are not.
4. The borrower cannot lease the item to a third party, but if he owned the usufruct, he should be able to transfer it.

The author then provides the Hanafi responses:

1. The word 'permission' can be used metaphorically for transfer, as it is in lease (ijarah), which is also concluded with the word 'permission' but is undoubtedly a transfer.
2. Ignorance is not an absolute impediment; it only invalidates when it leads to dispute. Since 'ariyah is non-binding (ghayr lazim), the borrower can return the item, so ignorance does not cause dispute. Moreover, ownership is only established by possession (qabd), which is actual use, and at that time there is no ignorance.
3. The prohibition takes effect because 'ariyah is non-binding; the lender may revoke at any time before the borrower takes possession. This does not negate transfer; rather, the transfer is incomplete until possession, and the prohibition prevents the borrower from obtaining usufructs he has not yet owned.
4. The borrower cannot lease to avoid increased harm to the lender; lease is binding and would close the door of revocation, causing harm to the lender. This is a special consideration (istihsan) to protect the lender.

Thus, the Hanafi school maintains that 'ariyah is a transfer of usufruct, not mere permission, and this view is the mufta bihi (relied upon) in the madhhab.

Key Terms
عارية ('ariyah): A contract where the owner transfers the usufruct of a specific item to another without compensation, the item remaining intact.
تمليك منفعة (tamlik manfa'ah): Transfer of ownership of the usufruct (right to use) of a property, as opposed to transfer of the property itself.
إباحة (ibahah): Permission to use another's property without transfer of ownership of the usufruct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of 'ariyah (loan for use)?
A) It is a permission (ibahah) to use the property without transfer of ownership.
B) It is a transfer of ownership of the usufruct (tamlik manfa'ah) without compensation.
C) It is a transfer of ownership of the item itself without compensation.
D) It is a binding lease (ijarah) without rent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'ariyah as tamlik manfa'ah (transfer of usufruct) and refutes the ibahah (mere permission) view through linguistic derivation, non-bindingness, and harm prevention.

The Nature of 'Ariyah: Tamlik vs Ibahah
Dispute over whether 'Ariyah is a transfer or mere permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that 'Ariyah is tamlik al-manfa'ah, explain the four arguments and their rebuttals, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of this dispute.

Core Idea
'Ariyah transfers benefit ownership, not mere permission – the word itself means gift.

This lesson explores a fundamental dispute: is a loan for use ('Ariyah) a true transfer of the right to benefit (tamlik al-manfa'ah), or just permission to use (ibahah)? The Hanafi school defends tamlik with four arguments derived from language, contract structure, revocation, and sub-leasing. We will walk through each argument and the Hanafi response to the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by clearly stating what the Hanafi school holds: 'Ariyah is tamlik al-manfa'ah bi la badal – a transfer of ownership of the benefit without compensation.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the four arguments for tamlik
From the passage, list the four proofs: (1) derivation from 'ariyah (gift), (2) no duration specification needed, (3) voidable by prohibition, (4) borrower cannot sub-let.
Each argument directly counters an opposing objection.
3
Understand the opposing view
The opposing school (likely Shafi'i) considers 'Ariyah as ibahah – mere permission, not ownership. They argue that tamlik requires clear terms, which are absent.
The opposing dalil is implicit: 'ibahah' wording suffices, ignorance of duration, etc.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttals
For each of the four objections, the passage provides a response: ibahah can be metaphorical; ignorance is harmless in non-binding contracts; prohibition is preventive not revocatory; sub-letting is blocked to protect lender's interest.
Focus on the logic of each response.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under tamlik, the borrower owns the benefit and use is legitimate until the lender revokes; under ibahah, the permission is revocable instantly without any ownership.
This affects whether the borrower can claim damages for early revocation.

Because 'Ariyah (commodatum) is derived from 'Ariyah meaning gift. 1. The reasoning for his statement: that if the lender says to the borrower: 'I permit you this garment, for example,' it would be an 'Ariyah, but transfer of ownership (tamlik) is not concluded with the word 'ibahah (permission). 2. And that in 'Ariyah it is not required to specify the amount of benefit by stating the duration, and benefits cannot be known except by mentioning duration, and tamlik is not valid with ignorance (jihalah) as you see in Ijarah. 3. And that the borrower does not have the right to lease to another, whereas one who owns something without compensation has the right to transfer ownership to another with or without compensation, like the recipient of a gift. The response to the first point: that ibahah may be used metaphorically for tamlik as in Ijarah; do you not see that it is concluded with the word ibahah, yet it is tamlik? And to the third point: the effect of prohibition is not because there is no tamlik in 'Ariyah, but rather because by prohibition the lender prevents the borrower from obtaining benefits that he has not yet owned; and he has that right because it is a non-binding contract, so he may revoke at any time. And to the fourth point: the borrower does not have the right to lease in order to prevent increased harm to the lender; and the reason is that Ijarah is established in the Shari'ah as binding, and that closes the door of revocation, thereby harming the lender.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of the contract of 'Ariyah (loan for use/commodatum). The author presents four rational arguments to prove that 'Ariyah is a transfer of ownership (tamlik) of benefit without compensation, contrary to the view of some who consider it mere permission (ibahah). The arguments are: 1) If it were permission, the word 'ibahah would suffice, but tamlik requires specific wording; 2) In 'Ariyah, specifying duration is not required, unlike ijarah, but tamlik cannot be ambiguous; 3) 'Ariyah is voidable by the lender's prohibition, whereas ownership is not revocable; 4) The borrower cannot sub-let, whereas an owner can. The author then responds to each objection raised by the opposing school, concluding that the term 'Ariyah itself (derived from 'ariyah meaning gift) indicates tamlik. The responses clarify that ibahah can be used for tamlik (as in ijarah), that ignorance is not fatal here because the contract is non-binding and possession occurs at use, that prohibition works as a prevention of future benefit acquisition, not revocation of ownership, and that sub-letting is prohibited to protect the lender from a binding contract. Thus, the Hanafi school maintains that 'Ariyah is tamlik al-manfa'ah bi la badal.

Key Terms
تمليك المنفعة (tamlik al-manfa'ah): Transfer of ownership of the benefit (usufruct) of an asset, without transferring ownership of the asset itself.
إباحة (ibahah): Permission to use something without transferring ownership of the benefit.
عارية ('Ariyah): A contract where a lender gives an object to a borrower for use without any compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the lender explicitly prohibits it.
D) Because the borrower did not pay for the use.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies 'Ariyah as tamlik al-manfa'ah (transfer of benefit ownership) without compensation, distinguishing it from mere permission by linguistic and rational arguments.

The Four Types of Tamlik and Valid Wording for 'Ariyah
Classification of tamlik types and wording for 'Ariyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize the four types of tamlik (ownership transfer) in Hanafi fiqh, and state which wording is valid for the contract of 'Ariyah.

Core Idea
Ownership transfer is of four types: sale, gift, ijarah, and 'Ariyah – distinguished by object/benefit and with/without compensation.

This lesson lays out the foundational classification of all ownership transfers in Islamic law, addressing a key objection about benefits being non-enduring. The Hanafi school affirms that benefits can be owned just like objects, and then clarifies the valid explicit and metaphorical wording for the contract of 'Ariyah (gratuitous loan of benefit).

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
Read the passage and list the four types of tamlik based on two dichotomies: object vs benefit, and with compensation vs without.
Memorize this classification as it is fundamental to contract law.
2
Understand the objection about benefits
Note the implied objection: benefits are accidents (a'rad) that do not endure, so how can they be owned?
This is a common philosophical challenge to the concept of leasing and lending.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author responds by analogy: just as objects are owned in two ways (sale/gift), benefits are owned in two ways (ijarah/'Ariyah), serving human needs.
The common factor (jami') is human need (hajat al-nas).
4
Recognize valid wording for 'Ariyah
Identify that words like 'a'rtuka and manah'tuka are explicit (sarih), and even metaphors like 'at'amtu-ka ardi are valid as conventional metaphors.
The definition of sarih includes both literal and conventional metaphorical usage.

And benefits are capable of being subject to tamlik, like a bequest of a slave's service. And according to some: it is permission to benefit from another's property. Know that tamlikat are of four types: Tamlik of an object ('ayn) with compensation is sale; without compensation is gift; tamlik of benefit with compensation is ijarah; and without compensation is 'Ariyah. (And it is valid with the words 'a'rtuka' and 'manah'tuka'). The language in the chapter of 'awr, and 'ariyah is deficient, and the weak letter is in its lam, as the linguists mention in the chapter of 'arw. End. His statement: 'And benefits...' etc. is a response to an implied question: do benefits, being non-enduring accidents, qualify for tamlik? The response: benefits may be owned like objects, and tamlik is of two types: with compensation and without. Objects accept both types: with compensation like sale, without like gift. Similarly, benefits accept both types: ijarah and 'Ariyah. The common factor is the fulfillment of people's needs, just as they need both types of tamlik for objects, they need them for benefits. His statement: 'And it is valid with a'rtuka' because it is explicit in the contract of 'Ariyah. Al-Tahtawi reports from Qazi Zadah: 'The explicit (sarih) according to usul scholars is what reveals the intended meaning by itself, including the true not-abandoned meaning, and the conventional metaphor.' Commentary
This passage provides a concise classification of all ownership transfers (tamlikat) in Islamic law: sale (object with compensation), gift (object without compensation), ijarah (benefit with compensation), and 'Ariyah (benefit without compensation). The author first addresses an objection that benefits, being transient accidents, cannot be owned. He responds by analogy: just as objects are owned, benefits can also be owned, and both categories have the same two subtypes (with/without compensation). The underlying rationale is the universal human need for both types of transactions. The passage then confirms that the contract of 'Ariyah is validly concluded with the words 'a'rtuka (I lend you) and manah'tuka (I grant you), which are considered explicit (sarih) in 'Ariyah. Quoting al-Tahtawi and Qazi Zadah, the author explains that 'sarih' includes both the literal meaning and conventional metaphors. Thus, words like 'at'amtu-ka ardi' (I fed you my land) are also valid as metaphorical expressions.

Key Terms
تمليك العين (tamlik al-'ayn): Transfer of ownership of the object itself.
تمليك المنفعة (tamlik al-manfa'ah): Transfer of ownership of the benefit/usufruct only.
صريح (sarih): Explicit wording that clearly indicates the intended contract, including both literal and conventional metaphorical expressions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Tamlik of benefit without object (i'arah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of ownership transfers is binary on two axes: object/benefit and with/without compensation, yielding four contracts; and 'Ariyah is valid with both explicit and conventionally metaphorical wording.

Types of Tamlik and Validity of Expressions for 'Ariyah
Defines four types of tamlik and validates explicit phrases for 'ariyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of tamlik and state the Hanafi position that 'ariyah is a valid transfer of ownership of benefit, supported by qiyas.

Core Idea
Tamlik is fourfold: substance/benefit × compensation/none; 'ariyah is tamlik al-manfa'ah bila 'iwad.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a fundamental contractual taxonomy. It defines the four types of ownership transfer (tamlik) based on the object (substance vs. benefit) and presence of compensation. Moreover, it validates explicit phrases for the loan contract ('ariyah) and defends the legal capacity of benefits to be owned, responding to a potential objection that benefits are transient accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of tamlik
Read the passage where the author lists tamlik al-'ayn bi 'iwad (sale), tamlik al-'ayn bila 'iwad (gift), tamlik al-manfa'ah bi 'iwad (hire), and tamlik al-manfa'ah bila 'iwad ('ariyah). Note the two dimensions: object and compensation.
2
Recognize the objection and response
The passage anticipates an objection: benefits (manafi') are transient accidents, so they cannot be owned. The author responds by stating that benefits are indeed capable of being owned, just like substances. This is supported by a qiyas (analogy).
The jami' (common link) is the fulfillment of people's need (daf' hajat al-nas).
3
Trace the qiyas proof
The proof is a rational analogy: as people need both compensated and uncompensated transfers of substances (sale and gift), so too they need such transfers of benefits (hire and loan). The 'illa is the need itself, which justifies recognizing ownership of benefits.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) used in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Evaluate the explicit terms
The passage validates 'a'ratuka' (I gave you on loan) and 'manaḥtuka' (I gave you a milch animal) as explicit (sarih) for 'ariyah. It distinguishes these from metaphors like 'at'amtu ardi' (I fed my land). Understand the linguistic basis: 'manh' originally meant giving a milch animal for milk and return, making it apt for 'ariyah.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah states that 'manh' is carried on tamlik of benefits unless gift is intended.
5
Understand the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that 'ariyah is a tamlik of benefit, not mere ibahah (permission). Expressions like 'manaḥtuka' are valid for 'ariyah when hibah is not intended. This is based on the Zahir al-Riwayah and upheld by later authorities.
Shafi'is hold 'ariyah is ibahah; Hanafis disagree.

And it is valid with 'I gave you on loan' and 'I gave you a milch animal'. And benefits are capable of being owned, like the bequest of the service of a slave. And according to some, it is a permission to benefit from the property of another. Know that transfers of ownership are four types: 1. Transfer of the substance with compensation is sale. 2. Without compensation is gift. 3. Transfer of benefit with compensation is hire. 4. Without compensation is loan for use. (And it is valid with 'I gave you on loan' and 'I gave you a milch animal'). The language is in the chapter of 'ayr, and 'ariyah is defective, and the weak letter is in its lam, and so the lexicographers mentioned it in the chapter of 'arw. End. His statement: 'and benefits... etc.' is the answer to an implied question: the benefits are accidents that do not last, so they are not capable of being owned. The answer: benefits accept ownership like substances, and ownership is of two types: one with compensation, the other without compensation. Substances accept both types: ownership with compensation like sale, and without compensation like gift and the like. Similarly, benefits accept these two types like hire and loan. The common link between them is the fulfillment of people's need, just as they need both types of ownership in substances, so they need that in benefits. His statement: 'and it is valid with a'ratuka' because it is explicit in the chapter of the contract of 'ariyah. Al-Tahtawi narrated from Qadi Zada: 'Explicit' according to the scholars of usul is that whose intended meaning is apparent in itself, encompassing the non-abandoned literal and the conventional metaphorical. End. So the first is 'a'ratuka', and the second is 'at'amtu ardi'. The origin of manh is that one gives a she-camel or a sheep to drink its milk, then it is returned, so the original meaning was considered, so it is carried upon 'ariyah. And 'at'amtu ardi'. His statement: 'The origin of manh... etc.' said in al-Misbah: al-Manhah - with kasra - originally is the sheep or she-camel that its owner gives to a man to drink its milk, then returns it when the milk is cut off. This is its origin, then it became widely used for every gift. And manaḥtuhu manhan is from the patterns of nafa'a and daraba: I gave him, and the noun is al-manihah. End. And in al-Sirah: manha wa adana manhatun bi al-kasri wa hiya manihatun sutur bih bihashti shir al-ghulam yakun kasi ra. Abu 'Ubayda said: The Arabs have four names which they place in the position of 'ariyah: manihah, 'ariyah, iqfar, and akhbal. End. So it is not hidden from you that its original meaning is more appropriate for 'ariyah, so it is appropriate to carry it upon it at absolute usage as well, due to what is established in usul that if the literal meaning is not intended, as the commentator indicated with his statement 'fa ruwiya... etc.' And it is stated explicitly in al-Hidayah that carrying manh upon transfer of benefits is permissible when gift is not intended. Al-Zayla'i said: If he intends gift by it, it gives ownership of the substance; otherwise it remains upon its original meaning. End. And in al-Kafi: If it is added to what cannot be benefited from while its substance remains, it is a gift; and if it is added to what can be benefited from while its substance remains, it is 'ariyah. End. And Allah knows best. His statement: 'and I fed you my land'; al-'Ayni said in al-Binayah: When 'food' is added to what is eaten, ownership of the substance is intended; when added to what is not eaten, like land, eating its produce is intended: applying the name of the locus to the state. End.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah defines the types of tamlik (transfer of ownership) and establishes the validity of certain expressions for the contract of 'ariyah (loan for use). The author begins by stating that the contract of 'ariyah is valid with the explicit phrases 'a'ratuka' (I gave you on loan) and 'manaḥtuka' (I gave you a milch animal). He then addresses a potential objection: benefits (manafi') are accidents that do not last, so how can they be owned? The answer is that benefits are indeed capable of being owned, just like physical substances (a'yan). The proof is a rational analogy (qiyas): just as people need both compensated and uncompensated transfers of substances (sale and gift), they also need similar transfers of benefits (hire and loan). The common link is the fulfillment of human need. The passage then explains that 'a'ratuka' is explicit (sarih) for 'ariyah because it is a clear word from the chapter of 'ariyah, not a metaphor. The word 'manaḥtuka' originates from giving a milch animal to benefit from its milk and then return it. This original meaning makes it suitable for 'ariyah when the intent is not a permanent gift. The word 'at'amtu' (I fed) is discussed as a metaphorical expression: if said of something edible, it means a gift of the substance; if said of land, it means permission to eat its produce, thus it is 'ariyah of the land. The commentary cites various sources: al-Tahtawi, Qadi Zada, al-Misbah, al-Sirah, Abu 'Ubayda, al-Hidayah, al-Zayla'i, al-Kafi, and al-'Ayni. The Hanafi position is that 'ariyah is a tamlik of benefit, not mere ibahah (permission), as some hold.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; here categorized into four types based on object (substance vs benefit) and compensation.
عارية ('ariyah): Loan for use: transfer of ownership of benefit without compensation, with the substance returned.
منفعة (manfa'ah): Benefit or usufruct; considered an owned property (mal) in Hanafi law, capable of being transferred.
صريح (sarih): Explicit term: a word whose intended meaning is clear by itself, including both the non-abandoned literal and conventional metaphorical usage.
منح (manh): Originally giving a milch animal for milk, then generalized to any gift; here used metaphorically for 'ariyah when the substance is returned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of 'ariyah?
A) A revocable permission to use (ibahah).
B) A transfer of ownership of the substance (tamlik al-'ayn).
C) A transfer of ownership of the benefit (tamlik al-manfa'ah) without compensation.
D) A sale of the benefit for a deferred price.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies 'ariyah as a binding transfer of ownership of benefit, validated by explicit terms and justified by the universal need for temporary use of property.

Interpreting Ambiguous Phrases: 'Ariyah or Hibah?
Commentary on definitions and rulings of 'ariyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that ambiguous phrases relating to usufruct default to 'ariyah (loan of usufruct) rather than hibah (gift), unless a contrary intention is proven.

Core Idea
When in doubt, assume 'ariyah — the lender retains ownership, the borrower gets only the benefit.

This lesson dives into the Hanafi approach to interpreting phrases that could imply either a gift of ownership or a simple loan of usufruct. The commentary from 'Umda al-Ri'aya shows how the nature of the object and the original meaning of words guide the default ruling. Understanding this principle helps distinguish valid lending from unintended gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
Pick out the exact wording from the source text, e.g., 'أطعمتك أرضي' (I fed you my land).
The phrase must be one that could be understood as either a transfer of ownership or of mere usufruct.
2
Determine if the object can be consumed
Check whether the object (e.g., land, animal, slave) can be eaten or used up without its substance remaining.
If the object is not consumable, it cannot be a literal gift of eating; thus the phrase points to usufruct.
3
Apply the default rule
If the object is not consumable and no contrary intention is expressed, interpret the phrase as 'ariyah (loan of usufruct).
This default is based on the original meaning of the words and the legal principle that you do not attribute a higher transfer (hibah) without evidence.
4
Consider the lender's right to revoke
Recognize that in 'ariyah, the lender may demand the item back at any time, even if a term was mentioned, based on the hadith 'المنحة مردودة والعارية مؤداة'.
This is a key practical consequence: the borrower cannot refuse to return the item.

Book of 'Ariyah: Its definition and rulings. 'And I fed you my land.' The origin of al-manihah is that one gives a she-camel or sheep for drinking its milk, then it is returned. So the original meaning was considered, and it is treated as 'ariyah. 'And I fed you my land' (footnote: ...). Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary (hashiya) on the ruling of 'ariyah (loan of usufruct). It addresses how to interpret certain ambiguous phrases that could imply either a gift of ownership (hibah) or a loan of usufruct ('ariyah). The author begins by discussing the word 'manihah' (giving a milk animal), which originally meant lending the milk, not the animal itself. Therefore, it is classified as 'ariyah, not hibah. The same principle applies to the phrase 'I fed you my land': because land cannot be eaten, it means giving the produce (usufruct), not ownership of the land. Similarly, 'I mounted you on an animal' and 'I served you my slave' are considered 'ariyah unless the intention of gift is explicit. The phrase 'my house is for you as residence' is interpreted as 'ariyah because the word 'sukna' (residence) clarifies that only the benefit is intended. The ruling concludes that the lender can take back the borrowed item at any time, even if a time was specified, based on the hadith 'al-manihah is returned, and the borrowed item is repaid.' The hadith is the primary proof for this right of the lender to reclaim.

Key Terms
عارية ('āriyah): A loan of usufruct where the lender retains ownership of the item's substance and permits the borrower to use it without compensation.
منحة (manīḥah): A gift of a milk animal for drinking its milk, then returning it. Originally a form of 'ariyah, it is treated as such unless intended as a gift of ownership.
هبة (hibah): A gift of ownership, transferring the substance (raqabah) of the item to the recipient.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a gift (hibah) of the land.
B) It is a loan of usufruct ('ariyah) of the land's produce.
C) It is invalid because land cannot be eaten.
D) It is a sale (bay') of the produce.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous phrases about using an item are presumed to be 'ariyah (loan of usufruct) unless a transfer of ownership is explicitly intended.

The Borrower’s Non-Liability Without Transgression
Explains the principle of non-liability in borrowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a borrower is not liable for destruction of a borrowed item without transgression and explain the lender’s right to revoke permission for future benefits.

Core Idea
Borrower is not liable without transgression; lender may revoke future use but not harm.

This passage opens the chapter on borrowing (ʿāriyah) in the Hanafi tradition. The primary ruling is that the borrower is not liable for destruction of the item unless he commits a transgression (taʿaddī). The commentary also clarifies that the lender has the right to revoke permission for future benefits, as benefits accrue moment by moment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
What is the borrower’s liability for destruction? The text states 'lā yuḍammin' – he is not liable.
The default position in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand 'generalization after specification'
The commentary notes that the general command to return the item is specified by the fact that benefits occur gradually and are not owned in advance.
This explains why the lender can revoke future benefits.
3
Note the lender’s authority
The lender has the authority to revoke permission for benefits that have not yet occurred.
This is because he never owned those benefits fully.
4
Recognize this as the default rule
This ruling applies except in cases of manifest harm, which is discussed in the next lesson.
The exception does not change the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The book of borrowing: its definition and rulings. And he is not liable. 'And he is not liable' is effective, and it is clear that it contains generalization after specification because a gift is a specific borrowing, as you know from the expression of 'al-Misbah'. So it adds an extra emphasis that the borrowed item deserves return, because benefits occur gradually, and ownership is established according to their occurrence, so returning relates to benefits that have not yet occurred, thus it is a refusal to grant ownership, and he has authority over that. Excepted from this is when there is manifest harm to the borrower in his return, for then the borrowing becomes void, and the item remains with an equivalent rent. In the Fatawa of Qadi Khan: A man borrowed a slave-woman from a man to nurse his son, and she nursed him, and when the boy would not take anything except her milk, the lender said: Return my servant to me. Abu Yusuf said: He does not have that, and he has an equivalent rent for his servant until the boy is weaned. Likewise, if a man borrowed a horse from a man to fight upon it, and he lent it to him for four months, then he met him after two months in Muslim lands, and he wanted to take it, he may do so. But if he met him in the land of disbelief in a place where he cannot afford hire or purchase, the borrower may not hand it over to him, because this is manifest harm, and the borrower owes an equivalent rent for the horse from the place where its owner demanded it to the nearest place where he finds hire or purchase. End. And he said in 'al-Ashbah': Regarding when one borrows land for cultivation and plants it, it is not taken from him until he harvests, and if he did not set a time, it is left with an equivalent rent.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on 'ariyah (borrowing/lending). It is a ruling statement: the borrower is not liable for destruction of the borrowed item without transgression. The commentary elaborates that the borrowed item must be returned, and the lender has the right to revoke permission for future benefits. However, an exception is made when immediate return would cause manifest harm (darar bayyin) to the borrower. In such cases, the borrower may keep the item until the harm ceases, but must pay fair compensation (ujrat al-mithl). Three illustrative cases from Qadhi Khan's Fatawa are given: (1) a slave-woman borrowed for nursing: if the child will only accept her milk, the lender cannot demand return until weaning, and the borrower pays rent; (2) a horse borrowed for war: if the borrower is in enemy territory without means to replace the horse, he may keep it until safety, again paying an equivalent rent; (3) land borrowed for cultivation: if already planted, it cannot be taken until harvest, and if no time was fixed, it remains with rent. These cases show that the Hanafi school protects the borrower from severe hardship while ensuring the lender is not deprived of compensation.

Key Terms
عارية (ariyah): A contract where possession of an item is given free of charge for use, with the item itself to be returned.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or destruction of the borrowed item.
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): Fair market rental value for the item during the period of use after the borrowing is voided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the default Hanafi ruling regarding a borrower’s liability for destruction of a borrowed item?
A) The borrower is always liable.
B) The borrower is liable only if he commits a transgression.
C) The borrower is never liable.
D) The borrower is liable if the item is destroyed by natural causes.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the borrower of an item is not liable for its destruction unless he acts with transgression (taʿaddī).

Exception for Manifest Harm: Retention and Compensation
Details the exception for manifest harm and compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a borrower may retain a borrowed item despite the lender’s demand and calculate the required compensation (ujrat al-mithl).

Core Idea
Manifest harm to borrower overrides lender’s right to reclaim; borrower pays fair rent.

The general rule of non-liability is qualified by an important exception: if returning the borrowed item would cause manifest harm (ḍarar bayyin) to the borrower, the borrower may keep it until the harm ceases. However, the borrower must pay an equivalent rent (ujrat al-mithl) for the period of continued use. This lesson examines the conditions and consequences of this exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception
The passage states: 'Yustathnā min dhālika mā idhā kāna fī rujūʿihi ḍarar bayyin bil-mustaʿīr' – an exception is made when return causes manifest harm to the borrower.
This overrides the lender's general right to reclaim.
2
Define 'manifest harm'
Manifest harm (ḍarar bayyin) is harm that is clear and substantial, not trivial. The harm must be to the borrower, not just inconvenience.
Examples include severe dependency on the item.
3
Understand the consequence
When manifest harm exists, the borrowing becomes void (tabṭul al-iʿārah) and the borrower may keep the item, but must pay ujrat al-mithl (equivalent rent).
The borrower does not get free use; compensation is required.
4
Apply to scenarios
The next lesson will explore specific cases; here we focus on the general rule.
This principle is applied in diverse situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Applied Cases: Nursing Slave-Woman, War Horse, and Cultivation Land
Provides juristic examples of the manifest harm exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the exception for manifest harm to concrete scenarios and determine the appropriate compensation (ujrat al-mithl).

Core Idea
Manifest harm is context-dependent; cases show when retention and rent apply.

The Fatawa of Qadi Khan provides three illustrative cases that bring the exception to life. In each, the borrower has made the item so integral to his situation that immediate return would cause severe hardship. The ruling in each case is consistent: the borrower may keep the item but must pay an equivalent rent.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the nursing slave-woman case
The child becomes exclusively dependent on the borrowed nurse's milk. Abu Yusuf rules the lender cannot reclaim her until weaning; the borrower pays ujrat al-mithl.
The harm is to the child's health, which is manifest.
2
Analyze the war horse case
If the borrower is in enemy territory without means to replace the horse, he may keep it until reaching a safe place. He pays rent from demand point to nearest replacement point.
Location and availability of alternatives matter.
3
Analyze the cultivation land case
If land is borrowed for cultivation and already planted, it cannot be taken until harvest. If no time was set, the borrower pays rent.
The borrower's investment (crops) creates manifest harm.
4
Identify the common thread
In all cases, the borrower has made the item integral to his situation; immediate return would cause severe loss. The borrower always pays ujrat al-mithl.
This shows the consistency of the Hanafi approach.

Source Text

Borrower's Liability in Ariyah: A Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on borrower's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on borrower liability, identify the Shafi'i objection and its proof, and explain the Hanafi rebuttal.

Core Idea
The borrower is not liable for destruction from normal use, because permission negates the force needed for compensation.

This passage presents the classic inter-madhhab dispute on whether a borrower must compensate if the borrowed item is destroyed. The Hanafi school holds that permission to use negates liability, while al-Shafi'i distinguishes between normal and abnormal use. Understanding their proofs is key to mastering the fiqh of contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding the borrower's liability for destruction. Note the nuance from al-Zayla'i regarding destruction outside of use.
The Hanafi position is that the borrower is not liable without transgression (ta'addi), but if the item perishes outside the state of use, some Hanafis (like al-Zayla'i) hold him liable.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Shafi'i is the primary opponent. State his view: if the item perishes from normal use, no liability; if from other causes, liability. Also note the views of Ahmad and Malik.
Mention that Ibn 'Abbas, Abu Hurairah, 'Ata', and Ishaq share al-Shafi'i's view.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i proof: the hadith of Safwan where the Prophet said 'bal 'ariyah madminah' and the analogy to possession for self-benefit (e.g., buyer in a negotiation).
The Shafi'i argument is that the borrower takes possession for his own benefit without entitlement, so he is liable.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal uses the hadith of Abu Umamah 'al-ariyah mu'addah' (the loan is to be returned), analogies to lessee and gift, and a critique of the Safwan hadith: the Prophet took the armor under necessity and Safwan was a harbi.
The Hanafi argument emphasizes that permission negates the force (jabr) required for compensation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, the borrower pays nothing for destruction from normal use; under Shafi'i, he pays unless the destruction is from the very act of normal use.
This difference affects daily transactions like borrowing tools, vehicles, or books.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العارية تعريفها وأحكامها

يضمن إذا هلكت بلا تعد منه مطلقاً، وهكذا في «الهداية»(۱)، وقال الزيلعي في «التبيين»: إنّه يضمن عنده إذا هلكت في غير حال الاستعمال. وقال العَيْنِي في «الرمز (۳) : وبه قال أحمد له ، وعن أحمد : لو شرط الضمان يضمن، وإلا لا، وقال مالك ل ل الله : ما يخفى هلاكه كالثياب والأثمان يضمن ، وإلا لا. انتهى.

وقال الشَّمني في «كمال الدراية» : وقال الشافعي الله : إن هلكت من الاستعمال المعتاد لا يضمن، وإن هلكت من غيره يضمن ، وهو قول ابن عباس وأبي هريرة وعطاء وإسحاق . انتهى.

وللشافعي ما روى أبو داود والنسائي عن صفوان بن أمية له : «أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم استعار منه دروعاً يوم خيبر فقال : أغصباً يا محمد ، قال : بل عارية مضمونة»(4)؛ ولأنّ المستعير قبض مال غيره لنفسه لا عن استحقاق، فكان مضموناً عليه كالمقبوض على سوم الشراء. بخلاف المودع فإنّه لم يقبض الوديعة لنفسه بل لنفع مالكها، وهو الحفظ ؛ ولذا لا يلزمه مؤنة الردّ ، وبخلاف المستأجر ، فإن قبضه عن استحقاق ؛ ولذا يجبر المؤجر على تسليم الدار، ولا يملك الاسترداد حتى تمضي مدة الإجارة. وبخلاف الموصى له بخدمة العبد ؛ فإنَّ العبد لا يصير مضموناً عليه، وإن قبضه لنفسه ؛ لأنّ قبضه مستحق ؛ ولهذا يجبر الورثة على تسليم العبد إليه. ولنا : ما رواه أبو داود والترمذي ، وقال : حديث حسن، عن أبي أمامة له قال (۱) سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : «الله قد أعطى كل ذي حق حقه، فلا وصية لوارث ...» إلى أن قال : «العارية مؤداة، والمنحة مردودة»(۱). ولأنَّ المستعير قبض العين للانتفاع بها بأذن صحيح من مالكها فلا يكون مضمونة عليه كالمستأجر ؛ ولأن وجوب الضمان للجبران ، وهو لا يتحقق إلا بتفويت شيء على المالك جبراً، وبالإذن الصحيح من المالكِ ينعدم الجبر ؛ ولأنَّ عقد العارية عقد تبرع فلا يكون فيه ضمان كالهبة. وجواب التعارض أنه له أخذ دروع صفوان له بغير رضاه ؛ ولذا قال : أغصباً يا محمد ؛ لأنه كان محتاجاً إلى السلاح ، فكان الأخذ له حلالاً، ولكن بشرط الضمان كأخذ طعام الغير في حال المخمصة. وقيل : المراد ضمان الردّ ، وقيل : كان هذا منه و اشتراطاً للضمان على نفسه، وعندنا المستعير لا يضمن بالشرط ، ولكن صفوان الله كان يومئذ حربياً، ويجوز بين المسلم والحربي من الشرائط ما لا يجوز بين المسلمين. ذكره السمني)، وغيره.
Chapter of Ariyah: its definition and rulings. He is liable if it perishes without transgression from him absolutely, and thus in al-Hidayah. Al-Zayla'i said in al-Tabyin: He is liable in his view if it perishes outside the state of use. Al-'Ayni said in al-Ramz: Ahmad said the same, and from Ahmad: if he stipulates liability he is liable, otherwise no. Malik said: what perishes inconspicuously like clothes and valuables, he is liable, otherwise no. End. Al-Shamni said in Kamal al-Dirayah: Al-Shafi'i said: if it perishes from normal use, he is not liable; if from other than that, he is liable, and this is the saying of Ibn 'Abbas, Abu Hurairah, 'Ata', and Ishaq. End. For al-Shafi'i is what Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i narrated from Safwan ibn Umayyah: The Prophet borrowed armor from him on the day of Khaybar and he said: 'Is it plunder, O Muhammad?' He said: 'Rather it is a guaranteed loan.' Because the borrower takes possession of another's property for himself without entitlement, so it is guaranteed, like property taken on the basis of purchase negotiation. Unlike the depositary, for he did not take possession of the deposit for himself but for the benefit of its owner, which is safekeeping; therefore he is not obliged to bear the expense of return. And unlike the lessee, for his possession is based on entitlement; therefore the lessor is compelled to hand over the house and cannot reclaim until the lease period expires. And unlike the legatee of a slave's service, for the slave does not become guaranteed by him even if he takes possession for himself, because his possession is entitled; therefore the heirs are compelled to hand over the slave to him. Our proof: what Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi narrated, and he said it is a good hadith, from Abu Umamah: He said: I heard the Messenger of Allah say: 'Allah has given every rightful person his right, so no bequest to an heir...' until he said: 'The loan is to be returned, and the gift is to be given back.' And because the borrower took possession of the item to benefit from it with valid permission from its owner, so it is not guaranteed on him, like the lessee; and because the obligation of liability is for compensation, and that is only realized by destroying something of the owner forcefully, and with valid permission from the owner, force is absent; and because the contract of ariyah is a contract of gratuitous benefit, so there is no liability in it, like a gift. Rebuttal of the conflict: He took Safwan's armor without his consent; that's why he said: 'Is it plunder, O Muhammad?' Because he was in need of weapons, so taking it was lawful for him, but with the condition of liability, like taking another's food in a famine. It is said: The meaning is liability for return. It is said: This was from him as a stipulation of liability on himself, and in our view the borrower does not become liable by stipulation, but Safwan was at that time a harbi (non-Muslim from enemy territory), and conditions are allowed between a Muslim and a harbi that are not allowed between Muslims. Mentioned by al-Samni and others.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning whether the borrower (musta'ir) is liable for the destruction of the borrowed item. The Hanafi position, as stated in al-Hidayah, is that the borrower is liable if the item is destroyed without any transgression (ta'addi) on his part, but this is immediately qualified by al-Zayla'i who distinguishes between destruction during use and outside use. The text then presents the views of Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Malik, and al-Shafi'i. Al-Shafi'i distinguishes between normal use and other causes: no liability for normal use, liability otherwise. This view is attributed to several Companions and early scholars. The Shafi'i proof is the hadith of Safwan where the Prophet said 'bal 'ariyah madminah' (rather it is a guaranteed loan) and a legal analogy: the borrower takes possession for his own benefit without entitlement, so he is liable like the buyer in a negotiation (sawm al-shira'). The Hanafi response is based on the hadith of Abu Umamah: 'al-'ariyah mu'adda' (the loan is to be returned), implying no liability beyond return. The Hanafi argument is: the borrower takes possession with valid permission for benefit, so he is like a lessee (who is not liable). Liability is for compensation, which requires force (jabr), but permission negates force. Also, ariyah is a gratuitous contract like a gift, which has no liability. The apparent conflict with the Safwan hadith is resolved by noting that the Prophet took the armor without Safwan's consent due to necessity, under a condition of liability, and Safwan was a harbi at the time, allowing conditions not permissible between Muslims. The author follows the order of presenting the dispute, then the proofs, and finally the Hanafi rebuttal.

Key Terms
العارية (al-ariyah): A contract where the owner allows another to use an item for free with the obligation to return it.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or destruction of property.
مستعير (musta'ir): The person who borrows an item for use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, when is the borrower liable for the destruction of a borrowed item?
A) Always, regardless of circumstances.
B) Only if there is transgression or the item is destroyed outside of normal use.
C) Only if a condition of liability was stipulated.
D) Never, because ariyah is a gratuitous contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's leniency in ariyah liability stems from the principle that permission negates the force (jabr) required for compensation.

Borrower Cannot Lease the Borrowed Item
Ruling with a rational proof that the borrower may not sub-lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits a borrower from subleasing a borrowed item using the aqli proof based on the binding nature of contracts.

Core Idea
A borrower cannot lease a borrowed item because lending is non-binding but leasing is binding—creating a binding obligation from a non-binding right is invalid.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh: a borrower (musta'ir) is not permitted to lease (sublease) the borrowed item. The ruling is supported by a rational argument that contrasts the non-binding nature of borrowing (ariyah) with the binding nature of leasing (ijarah). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of permissible use of borrowed property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that the borrower may not lease the borrowed item. Confirm the act (subleasing by borrower) and the ruling (impermissible/haram).
2
Understand the nature of the two contracts
Recognize that ariyah is ghayr lazim (non-binding, revocable at any time) while ijarah is lazim (binding, fixed term). This is the key distinction for the proof.
Ariyah is a gratuitous loan; ijarah is a rental contract.
3
Follow the reductio argument
If the borrower could lease, the resulting lease would either be binding or non-binding. Both options lead to contradiction: non-binding contradicts the essence of ijarah, and binding obligates the borrower beyond his authority.
The argument is a rational demonstration (dalil aqli) using impossibility of alternatives.
4
Conclude the prohibition
Therefore, the borrower cannot lease the borrowed item because he lacks the capacity to create a binding lease over property he does not own and which he holds on a non-binding basis.
This ruling holds even if the owner gives permission, per the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا تؤجر ؛ يعني ولا يجوز للمستعير أن يؤاجر ما استعاره.

قوله : لأن الشيء... الخ؛ تقريره : أن العارية غير لازمة في الأصل، والإجارة لازمة، فلو ملك المستعير أن يؤاجر العارية لوقعت إجارته إما لازمةً أو غير لازمة. فإن وقعت غير لازمة يلزم عدم لزوم الإجارة، وهو خلاف موضوعها، وإن وقعت لازمة يلزم إلزام المستعير بما هو متبرع فيه وهو غير لازم عليه، فلا يملكه.
His statement: 'And he does not lease' means it is not permissible for the borrower to lease what he borrowed. His statement: 'Because the thing... etc.'; its explanation: that ariyah is non-binding in origin, and lease is binding, so if the borrower were allowed to lease the ariyah, his lease would either be binding or non-binding. If it occurs as non-binding, then it would require the non-bindingness of the lease, which contradicts its essence; and if it occurs as binding, then it would require obligating the borrower to something he is gratuitously involved in and which is not binding on him, so he does not have the capacity.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a rational proof. The author states the ruling: the borrower (musta'ir) is not permitted to lease (i.e., sublease) the borrowed item. The proof is based on the nature of the two contracts. Ariyah (borrowing) is a non-binding (ghayr lazim) contract, meaning the borrower may return the item at any time, and the lender may reclaim it. Leasing (ijarah), on the other hand, is a binding (lazim) contract that obligates both parties for a fixed period. If the borrower were allowed to lease the borrowed item, the resulting lease would either be binding or non-binding. If it were non-binding, then it would not have the characteristic of a lease, which is meant to be binding. If it were binding, then the borrower would be assuming a binding obligation based on a non-binding right (the right to use the borrowed item), which he does not have the authority to do. Therefore, the borrower cannot enter into a lease agreement for the borrowed item, as he cannot impose a binding obligation on the owner's property beyond the original permission.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): A contract of lease where the lessor gives possession of an item for use in exchange for rent, and it is binding.
لزوم (luzum): The quality of being binding; a contract is lazim if neither party can unilaterally cancel it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, why cannot the borrower lease the borrowed item?
A) Because lease is a binding contract and borrowing is non-binding, and the borrower cannot create a binding obligation from a non-binding right.
B) Because the owner has not given permission for subleasing.
C) Because the borrowed item is not owned by the borrower.
D) Because the borrower must return the item at any time.

Key Takeaway
The borrower cannot lease the borrowed item because a binding lease cannot be built upon a non-binding right of use.
