Prohibition of Leasing a Borrowed Item (Ariyah)
Ruling with proof from hadith and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a borrower cannot lease a borrowed item, explain the proofs from hadith and qiyas, and address the apparent contradiction from the hadith of Safwan.

Core Idea
A borrower cannot lease a borrowed item because the loan is non-binding but a lease is binding—the lesser cannot encompass the greater.

In this lesson, we examine the classical Hanafi ruling that the borrower (musta'ir) is prohibited from leasing the borrowed item. The commentator provides a textual proof from the hadith 'al-ariyah mu'addah' and a rational proof based on the nature of contracts. We will also explore the refutation of an objection from the incident of Safwan.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the act (borrower leasing the borrowed item) and its hukm (haram).
2
Examine the Textual Proof (Hadith)
Analyze the hadith 'العارية مؤداة' and how it implies the prohibition by requiring preservation for return.
3
Analyze the Rational Proof (Qiyas)
Understand the analogy: loan is non-binding, lease is binding; a non-binding contract cannot generate a binding one.
4
Address the Counter-argument (Hadith of Safwan)
Explain why the Prophet's taking of armor without permission was exceptional (dire need, harbi status).
5
Conclude with the Mufta Bihi
Restate the operative ruling and its basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا تؤجر) : لأنَّ الشَّيء لا يستتبع ما فوقه سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : «الله قد أعطى كل ذي حق حقه، فلا وصية لوارث ...» إلى أن قال : «العارية مؤداة، والمنحة مردودة»(١). ولأنَّ المستعير قبض العين للانتفاع بها بأذن صحيح من مالكها فلا يكون مضمونة عليه كالمستأجر ; ولأن وجوب الضمان للجبران ، وهو لا يتحقق إلا بتفويت شيء على المالك جبراً، وبالإذن الصحيح من المالكِ ينعدم الجبر ; ولأنَّ عقد العارية عقد تبرع فلا يكون فيه ضمان كالهبة. وجواب التعارض أنه له أخذ دروع صفوان له بغير رضاه ; ولذا قال : أغصباً يا محمد ; لأنه كان محتاجاً إلى السلاح , فكان الأخذ له حلالاً، ولكن بشرط الضمان كأخذ طعام الغير في حال المخمصة. وقيل : المراد ضمان الردّ , وقيل : كان هذا منه و اشتراطاً للضمان على نفسه، وعندنا المستعير لا يضمن بالشرط , ولكن صفوان الله كان يومئذ حربياً، ويجوز بين المسلم والحربي من الشرائط ما لا يجوز بين المسلمين. ذكره السمني)، وغيره. 11 ] قوله : ولا تؤجر ; يعني ولا يجوز للمستعير أن يؤاجر ما استعاره. And it is not to be leased: because a thing does not necessitate what is above it. I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: 'Allah has given every rightful person his right, so no bequest to an heir...' until he said: 'The borrowed item is to be returned, and the gift is to be repaid.' And because the borrower took possession of the object for use with valid permission from its owner, so it is not guaranteed upon him like the lessee; and because the obligation of guarantee is for compensation, and it is only realized by causing a loss to the owner by force, and with valid permission from the owner, force is absent; and because the contract of loan is a gratuitous contract, so there is no guarantee in it like a gift. And the answer to the contradiction (in the hadith of Safwan) is that he (the Prophet) took the armor of Safwan without his consent; therefore he (Safwan) said: 'Is it a seizure, O Muhammad?' because he (the Prophet) was in need of weapons, so taking it was lawful for him, but with the condition of guarantee, like taking another's food in case of famine. And it was said: what is meant is guarantee of return, and it was said: this was from him and a stipulation of guarantee upon himself. And with us, the borrower does not guarantee by stipulation, but Safwan (may Allah be pleased with him) was at that time a harbi (enemy combatant), and between a Muslim and a harbi, conditions are permissible that are not permissible between Muslims. Mentioned by al-Samani and others. 11. His saying: 'And it is not leased' means it is not permissible for the borrower to lease what he has borrowed. (2) His saying: 'Because the thing does not necessitate what is above it' etc. Its explanation: The loan is originally non-binding, and the lease is binding. So if the borrower had the right to lease the borrowed item, his lease would occur either bindingly or non-bindingly. If it occurs non-bindingly, it would entail the non-bindingness of the lease, which is contrary to its nature; and if it occurs bindingly, it would entail the bindingness of the loan, which is contrary to its nature. That is because if the lease becomes binding, the loan becomes binding due to the impossibility of revoking it. So the conclusion is that loan is inferior to lease, and a thing does not necessitate what is above it.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a typical Hanafi sharh on the rule that the borrower (musta'ir) cannot lease the borrowed item. The commentator begins by stating the ruling and providing multiple proofs. The primary textual proof is the hadith 'al-ariyah mu'addah' which obligates the return of the borrowed item, implying that any act that prevents return (like leasing) is prohibited. The rational proof (qiyas) is that the loan contract is non-binding (ghayr lazim) while the lease contract is binding (lazim); a non-binding contract cannot encompass a binding one, just as a thing cannot contain what is above it. The commentator then addresses a potential objection from the famous hadith of Safwan, where the Prophet took armor without permission and stated it was not a seizure. Some might think this contradicts the rule that permission-based possession is not guaranteed. The Hanafi response is that the Prophet's taking was conditional on guarantee (like in dire necessity), and that Safwan was a harbi (enemy combatant) at the time, so special conditions apply between Muslims and non-Muslim combatants. The commentator also clarifies that the borrower does not become liable by mere stipulation; liability is only through usurpation. The overall ruling is clear: the borrower cannot lease, and if he does, he becomes a usurper.

Key Terms
عارية (ariyah): A loan of a usable item that is to be returned after use.
مستعير (musta'ir): The person who borrows the item.
إجارة (ijara): A lease contract that transfers usufruct for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because leasing would transfer ownership to the lessee.
B) Because the loan contract is non-binding while the lease is binding, so the loan cannot encompass the lease.
C) Because the lender can revoke the loan at any time.
D) Because the borrower does not have valid possession.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling is clear: a borrower cannot lease a borrowed item because the loan is a non-binding contract, while a lease is binding—the lesser cannot contain the greater.

Liability When a Borrower Leases and the Item is Damaged
Rulings on liability when borrower leases borrowed item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the lender's options when a borrower leases a borrowed item that is later damaged, and apply the rules of recourse and charity for the rent.

Core Idea
The borrower who leases becomes a usurper; the lender may hold either the borrower or the lessee liable, and the rent must be given in charity.

This passage addresses a common scenario: someone borrows an item and then rents it out to a third party without the lender's permission. If the item is damaged, who is liable? The text provides a detailed answer based on the principles of usurpation (ghasb) and deception (ghurur). Understanding this ruling helps clarify the strict liability of a borrower who exceeds his authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lender's choice
The lender has the option to hold either the borrower or the lessee liable. The borrower becomes a usurper by leasing without permission; the lessee took possession without the owner's consent.
2
Consequences if lender holds borrower liable
If the lender chooses to hold the borrower liable, the borrower cannot later recover anything from the lessee. The borrower must also give the rent he received in charity because he is treated like a usurper.
3
Consequences if lender holds lessee liable
If the lender holds the lessee liable, the lessee can seek recourse (رجوع) from the borrower (his lessor) provided the lessee was unaware that the item was merely a loan. This is to prevent harm from deception (غرور).
If the lessee knew the item was on loan, he has no recourse because there was no deception.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on charity
Imam Abu Yusuf differs on whether the usurper (and by analogy the borrower) must give the rent in charity. He holds that the usurper owns the rent and may keep it. The relied-upon position in the text is that charity is required.
This dispute originates in the Book of Usurpation (كتاب الغصب).
5
Apply the qiyas on usurpation
The borrower is analogized to a usurper: both use another's property without right. The ruling for a usurper (must give rent in charity, strict liability) is applied to the borrower.
This is a qiyas jali (clear analogy) based on the common cause of unauthorized use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : ضمنه المعير; أي ضمن المعير المستعير أو المستأجر، فالمعير مخير إن شاءَ ضمن المستعير; لأنه صار غاصباً بتعديه , فإنَّ العارية إذا لم تتناول الإجارة كان غصباً، وإن شاءَ ضمَّنَ المستأجر ; لأنه قبضه بغير إذن المالك لنفسه. فإن ضَمَّنَ المستعير لا يرجع بما غرَّمه على أحد ; أي على المستأجر، والأولى أن يقال هكذا، إذ لا فائدة في التعميم ; لأنه ظهر بالضمان أنَّ المستعير أجر ملك نفسه، ويتصدق بالأجرة ; لأنه صار بمنزلة الغاصب. والغاصب إذا أجر يملك الأجرة، ويتصدق بها ; لأنها حصلت بسبب خبيث , استعمال مال الغير، وكان سبيله التصدقُ خلافاً لأبي يوسف , وقد مر ذكره في «كتاب الغصب». وه وإن ضمن المعير المستاجر يرجعُ المستأجرُ على المؤجر إذا لم يعلم أنه كان عارية في يد المستعير، دفعاً لضر الغرور عن المستأجر , بخلاف ما إذا علم المستأجر بكونها عارية في يد المؤجر حيث لم يرجع عليه ; لأنه لم يوجد منه الغرور. (٢) قوله : ويرجع ...الخ ; يعني ويرجعُ المستأجرُ على مؤجره، وهو المستعير إن لم يعلم المستأجر أنه عارية في يد المؤجر، ولا يرجعُ عليه إلا للغرور، بخلاف ما إذا علم
[1] His saying: 'The lender guarantees it' means the lender guarantees the borrower or the lessee. So the lender has the choice: if he wishes, he holds the borrower liable because he became a usurper by his transgression; for when the loan does not include leasing, it is usurpation. And if he wishes, he holds the lessee liable because he took possession without the owner's permission for himself. Then if he holds the borrower liable, the borrower cannot recourse to anyone, i.e., to the lessee. It is better to say this, because there is no benefit in generalization; for it becomes apparent by the guarantee that the borrower leased his own property, and he gives the rent in charity because he is like a usurper. And the usurper, if he leases, he owns the rent but gives it in charity because it was acquired through an evil means, the use of another's property, and its path is to give it in charity, contrary to Abu Yusuf, and it has been mentioned in the Book of Usurpation. And if the lender holds the lessee liable, the lessee recourses to the lender if he did not know that it was a loan in the hand of the borrower, to repel the harm of deception from the lessee, contrary to if the lessee knew that it was a loan in the hand of the lender, where he does not recourse because there was no deception from him. (2) His saying: 'And he recourses...' etc. means the lessee recourses to his lessor, who is the borrower, if the lessee did not know that it was a loan in the hand of the lessor, and he only recourses to him due to deception, contrary to if he knew...
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage addresses the consequences if the borrower (musta'ir) leases the borrowed item and it is damaged. The commentary explains that the lender (mu'ir) has a choice in whom to hold liable: the borrower, because he became a usurper by leasing beyond his authority, or the lessee (musta'jir), because he took possession without the owner's permission. If the lender holds the borrower liable, the borrower cannot seek reimbursement from the lessee; instead, the borrower must give the rent he received in charity, as it is illicit profit akin to a usurper's earnings. This ruling is based on the principle that the borrower's act makes him like a usurper. If the lender chooses to hold the lessee liable, the lessee may recourse to the borrower (his lessor) if he was unaware that the item was merely a loan to the borrower. This recourse is based on the principle of preventing harm from deception (ghurur). If the lessee knew the item was on loan, he has no recourse.

Key Terms
معير (mu'ir): The lender of the item in the loan contract.
مستأجر (musta'jir): The lessee who rents the item from the borrower.
غاصب (ghasib): A usurper who takes or uses another's property without right.
الغرور (al-ghurur): Deception that misleads a person into a contract; here, it triggers the lessee's right of recourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The lender can only hold the lessee (musta'jir) liable.
C) The lender may choose to hold either the borrower or the lessee liable.
D) The lender cannot hold anyone liable because the damage was accidental.

Key Takeaway
When a borrower exceeds his authority by leasing, he becomes a usurper; the lender may hold him or the lessee liable, and the borrower must donate the rent to charity.

Ruling on the Borrower Lending the Borrowed Item
Expands ruling on lending by borrower and lessee, contrasting Shafi'i view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions under which a borrower may lend a borrowed item and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The borrower may lend a borrowed item because 'ariyah is ownership of usufruct, not mere permission.

This lesson explores a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on the nature of borrowing ('ariyah). While the Shafi'is view it as mere permission that cannot be transferred, the Hanafis treat it as ownership of usufructs, allowing the borrower to lend the item under certain conditions. The source passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on the standard Hanafi position with practical scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the borrower (musta'ir) lending the borrowed item to a third party. Determine the overall hukm: permissible (ja'iz) in the Hanafi school under conditions.
2
Analyze the two conditions
The matn gives two scenarios: (1) if the original lender did not specify a beneficiary, the borrower may lend regardless of whether the use differs; (2) if the lender specified a beneficiary, the borrower may lend only if the use does not differ.
Example: riding vs carrying on a beast.
3
Extend to the lessee case
The same ruling applies to the lessee (musta'jir) under a lease contract, because ijarah also involves ownership of usufructs (tamlik al-manafi'), just like 'ariyah, except that ijarah is for a price.
Lessor = mu'jir, lessee = musta'jir.
4
Examine the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i school argues that the borrower cannot lend because 'ariyah is mere permission to benefit (ibahah al-intifa'), and the one permitted cannot permit others.
Shafi'i reasoning: usufructs are non-existent and cannot be owned.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that if 'ariyah were mere permission, the borrower would not be liable for damages (daman), but he is liable, which proves that it is a transfer of ownership. This is the key proof (dalil) for the Hanafi position.
The liability (daman) indicates that the borrower has ownership of the usufruct.

Source Text

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
ويعار ما اختلف استعماله أو لا إن لم يعين منتفعاً ، وما لا يختلف إن عين، وكذا المؤجر
ويعار ما اختلف استعماله أو لا إن لم يعين منتفعاً ، وما لا يختلف إن عين) : أي إن أعار شيئا ولم يعين من ينتفع به، فللمستعير أن يعيره سواء اختلف استعماله كركوب الدابة ، أو لم يختلف كالحمل على الدابة، وإن عينَ مَن ينتفع به ، فإن لم يختلف استعماله يعيره، وإن اختلف لا.
(وكذا المؤجر ) : أي إذا أجر شيئاً ، فإن لم يُعَيِّن مَن ينتفع به فللمستأجر أن يعيره سواء اختلف استعماله أو لا، وإن عيّن يُعِيرُ ما لا يختلف استعماله لا ما

ففي الوجه الأول: للمستعير أن ينتفع به في أي نوع شاء عملاً بالإطلاق. وفي الوجه الثاني : ليس له أن يجاوز فيما سمّاه من الوقت والمنفعة، عملاً بالتقييد إلا إذا كان خلافاً إلى مثل ذلك ، كما إذا استعار دابة ليحمل عليها عشرة أقفزة حنطة مثلاً، فحمل عليها حنطة غيره، فلا ضمان عليه ؛ لأنَّ حنطته وحنطة غيره في الضرر سواء.
أو إلى خير من منه ، كما إذا استعار دابة ليحمل عليها عشرة أقفزة من الحنطة، فحمل عليها عشرة أقفزة من الشعير، فلا ضمان عليه أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ من البين أنَّ الشعير أخفٌ وزناً من الحنطة ؛ لكونها أصلب من الشعير.
وفي الوجه الثالث والرابع : ليس له أن يتعدى ما سماه من الوقت أو النوع. [٢]قوله : وكذا المؤجر ؛ - بفتح الجيم - ؛ أي حكمه حكم المعار إن لم يعين
فمن استعار دابة، أو استأجر مطلقاً يحمل ويعير له
لأن العارية عنده إباحة الانتفاع ، والمباح له لا يملك الإباحة ، وعندنا : هي تمليك المنافع ، فالمستعيرُ لَمَّا مَلَكَ المنافع كان له أن يملكها غيره، فمن استعار دابة، أو استأجر مطلقاً يحملُ ويعير له ) : أي للحمل
الموجب المنتفع به للمستأجر أن يعيره سواءً اختلف استعماله أو لا، وإن عين لا يعير إلا ما يختلف استعماله ؛ لأنَّ الإجارة تمليك المنافع كالإعارة إلا أن الإجارة ببدل ، والإعارة بلا بدل.
[ 1 ] قوله : لأن العارية عنده إباحة الانتفاع ؛ لأنَّ المنافع غير قابلة للملك ؛ لكونها معدومة، وإنما جعلناها موجودة في الإجارة ؛ لضرورة دفع حاجة الناس، وقد اندفعت بالإباحة هاهنا ، فلا يصار إلى التمليك.
And it is lent [the item] whose use differs or not if the beneficiary is not specified, and that which does not differ if specified. Similarly the lessor. (Meaning: if he lent something and did not specify who benefits from it, then the borrower may lend it whether its use differs like riding the animal or not like carrying on the animal. And if he specified the beneficiary, then if the use does not differ he may lend it, and if it differs he may not. Similarly the lessor: meaning if he leased something and did not specify who benefits, then the lessee may lend it whether the use differs or not; if he specified, he may lend that whose use does not differ, not that which differs. According to al-Shafi'i, the borrower may not lend [the borrowed item] because the borrowed item is permission to benefit, and what is permitted to him he cannot give permission to another. And according to us [Hanafis], it is ownership of usufructs, so when the borrower owns the usufructs, he has the right to make another own them. So whoever borrows a beast or leases absolutely may carry and lend it.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn on the ruling of lending a borrowed or leased item to another. The matn states two conditions: (1) If the original lender did not specify who will benefit from the item, then the borrower may lend it to anyone, regardless of whether the intended use of the item differs (e.g., riding vs. carrying a load) or not. (2) If the original lender specified a beneficiary, then the borrower may lend it only if the use does not differ; if the use differs, he may not lend it. The same ruling applies to the lessee (musta'jir) under a lease contract. The commentator explains that if the lessor did not specify the beneficiary, the lessee can lend it freely. If the lessor specified the beneficiary, then the lessee can lend only if the use does not differ. The passage then contrasts the Shafi'i school, which holds that the borrower cannot lend the item at all because 'ariyah is merely permission to benefit (ibahah al-intifa'), and one who has permission cannot grant permission to another. The Hanafi school holds that 'ariyah is ownership of usufructs (tamlik al-manafi'), so the borrower owns the right to use and can transfer that ownership to someone else, akin to lease (ijarah) which also involves transfer of ownership of usufructs in exchange for rent. The commentator further distinguishes between various types of 'ariyah: absolute in time and use, restricted in both, restricted in one and absolute in the other, etc.

Key Terms
الإعارة (al-i'ārah): Giving something to another to benefit from its use without compensation.
المستعير (al-musta'īr): The person who receives the item on loan (the borrower).
المؤجر (al-mu'jir): The person who gives something on lease (the lessor).
إباحة الانتفاع (ibāḥat al-intifā'): Permission to use the item, without transferring ownership of the usufruct.
تمليك المنافع (tamlik al-manāfi'): Transfer of ownership of the usufructs of an item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when may the borrower of an item lend it to another person if the original lender specified a beneficiary?
A) He may lend it in any case.
B) He may lend it only if the usage of the item does not differ from what was stipulated.
C) He may lend it only if the usage differs.
D) He may never lend it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows a borrower to lend a borrowed item if the original lender did not specify a beneficiary, or if the beneficiary was specified but the use does not differ.

Borrower’s Deposit: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Mufta Bihi
Intra-Hanafi dispute on borrower's deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the borrower's deposit and state the mufta bihi position with its conditions.

Core Idea
The borrower may deposit the borrowed item unless the loan was time-bound and the term expired.

When a person borrows an item (ariyah), does he have the right to deposit it with someone else for safekeeping? Early Hanafi scholars differed. Some, like al-Karkhi, held it is never allowed, while the majority permitted it. This lesson examines the arguments, the key distinction that resolves the conflict, and the final fatwa position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Pinpoint the minority view (al-Karkhi, al-Baqallani) that prohibits deposit absolutely, and the majority view (Iraqi scholars, Abu al-Layth, Burhan al-A'imma) that permits it.
2
Analyze the minority’s proof
Study the case from al-Jami‘: if the borrower sends the item to the owner via a messenger and it is lost, the borrower is liable. The minority takes this as evidence that any deposit by the borrower is invalid.
They also argue that deposit is a disposition in the substance without the owner’s permission, which is not allowed.
3
Analyze the majority’s proof
Understand the majority’s reasoning: deposit is a lesser disposition than lending (i'arah); since the borrower already has the right to use the item (a greater right), he may also deposit it (a lesser right). This is an argument a fortiori (awla).
4
Distinguish the Jami‘ case
The majority reconcile the conflicting case by interpreting it: it applies only when the 'ariyah was time-bound and the term has expired. After expiry, the borrower becomes a transgressor (muta'addi), so any deposit is invalid.
Thus the prohibition is limited to the case of transgression, not absolute.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
Later authorities like al-Marghinani, al-Zayla‘i, and the Sirajiyyah confirm that the fatwa is on the majority view: deposit is permissible as long as the borrower is not a transgressor.
The borrower must not have overstepped the owner's limits (e.g., by keeping the item past the agreed term).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في إيداع المستعير، قال بعض المشايخ: ليس له أن يودع مطلقاً، منهم: الكرخي ، واستدلوا عليه بمسألة ذكرها في «الجامع»: أنّ المستعير إذا بعث العارية إلى صاحبها على يد أجنبي فهلكت في يد الرسول ضَمِنَ المستعير العارية، وليس ذلك إلا إبداعاً منه.
:
له
قال الباقلاني : هذا القولُ أصح ؛ لأنَّ الإيداع تصرف في ملك الغير، وهو العين بغير إذنه قصداً، فلا يجوز بخلاف الإعارة ؛ لأنه تصرُّف في المنفعة قصداً وتسليم العين من ضروراته، فافترقا. وأكثرهم على أنه يجوز منهم : مشايخ العراق، وأبو الليث، والشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل، والصدر الكبير برهان الأئمة ؛ لأنَّ الإيداع دون الإعارة، والعين وديعة عند المستعير في العارية، فإذا ملك الأعلى فالأولى أن يملك الأدنى، قال ظهير الدين المرغيناني : وعليه الفتوى، ذكره الزيلعي) ، وغيره.
وجعل الفتوى على هذا في «السراجيّة» أيضاً، ومسألة «الجامع» محمولة على ما إذا كانت العارية مؤقتة فمضت مدتها ثمّ بعثَها مع الأجنبي؛ لأنه بإمساكها بعد مضي المدة يصير متعدّياً، حتى إذا هلكت في يده يضمن، فكذا إذا تركها في يد الأجنبي.
And they differed regarding the deposit of the borrower. Some scholars said: he may not deposit absolutely, among them al-Karkhi, and they argued based on a case mentioned in al-Jami': that if the borrower sent the borrowed item to its owner via a third party and it perished in the hand of the messenger, the borrower is liable for the borrowed item, and that is nothing but a deposit from him. Al-Baqallani said: this opinion is more correct because deposit is a disposition in the property of another, the very item, without his permission intentionally, so it is not permissible, unlike lending because it is a disposition in the usufruct intentionally and handing over the item is a necessary consequence, so they differ. The majority are of the view that it is permissible, among them the scholars of Iraq, Abu al-Layth, the Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, and the great chief Burhan al-A'imma, because deposit is lesser than lending, and the item is a trust (wadi'ah) with the borrower in 'ariyah, so if he owns the higher (i'arah) then it is more appropriate that he owns the lower (iida'). al-Baqallani said: this opinion is more correct. Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani said: and on this is the fatwa, mentioned by al-Zayla'i and others. And the fatwa was made on this in al-Sirajiyyah as well, and the case from al-Jami' is interpreted as when the 'ariyah was time-bound and its duration had passed, then he sent it with the stranger, because by retaining it after the term he becomes a transgressor, so that if it perishes in his hand he is liable, and likewise if he leaves it in the hand of a stranger.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether the borrower (musta'ir) may deposit the borrowed item (wadi'ah) with a third party. Some early scholars, including al-Karkhi, held that the borrower cannot deposit at all. Their evidence is a case from al-Jami': if the borrower sends the borrowed item to its owner via a messenger and the item is lost, the borrower is liable, which they interpret as an invalid deposit. Al-Baqallani supported this view, arguing that deposit is a disposition in the very substance of the item without the owner's permission, whereas lending is only a disposition in the usufruct. However, the majority of Hanafi scholars, including the scholars of Iraq, Abu al-Layth, Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, and Burhan al-A'imma, permitted deposit. Their reasoning: deposit is a lesser form of disposition than lending; since the item is already a trust (amana) in the hands of the borrower under 'ariyah, and the borrower has the right to lend (which is a higher disposition), it follows that he can deposit (a lower disposition) by analogy a fortiori. Later authorities like al-Marghinani confirmed that the fatwa is on the majority view. The case from al-Jami' is distinguished: it applies when the 'ariyah is time-bound and the term has expired, making the borrower a transgressor (mut'addi), so deposit is invalid. This ruling is consistent with the principle that the borrower may only deposit if he has the full right to use the item without restriction.

Key Terms
الإيداع (al-īdā'): Depositing an item with another for safekeeping (wadi'ah).
المستعير (al-musta'īr): The borrower of an item in 'ariyah.
مؤقتة (mu'aqqatah): Time-bound; a loan or lease with a specified duration.
متعدّي (muta'addī): Transgressor; one who oversteps the limits set by the owner, becoming liable for damages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, can the borrower (musta'ir) deposit the borrowed item with a third party?
A) No, never.
B) Yes, absolutely.
C) Yes, as long as the 'ariyah is not time-bound or the term has not expired.
D) Only if the third party is the original owner.

Key Takeaway
The borrower may deposit the borrowed item unless the loan is time-bound and the term has expired, in which case deposit is invalid and he becomes liable.

Definition of 'Ariyah and Its Legal Nature: Ibahah vs. Tamlik
Defines 'Ariyah and contrasts Hanafi and opposing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of 'Ariyah as tamlik al-manafi' and contrast it with the opposing view of ibahah al-intifa', and state the practical consequence regarding the borrower's right to lend.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, 'Ariyah transfers ownership of benefits, not mere permission; thus the borrower may lend the item.

This lesson introduces the legal nature of 'Ariyah (loan) in Hanafi fiqh and the key inter-madhhab dispute. Understanding whether 'Ariyah is mere permission to use or a transfer of benefit ownership directly affects whether the borrower can lend the item to a third party. We will dissect the positions, evidence, and practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that Hanafis define 'Ariyah as tamlik al-manafi' (ownership of benefits). This means the borrower owns the benefits and may transfer them.
2
Identify the opponent's view
The opposing school (al-Shafi'i) holds that 'Ariyah is ibahah al-intifa' (permission to use). The borrower only has permission, not ownership.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the opponent's reasoning: benefits are non-existent (ma'dum) at the time of contract, so they cannot be owned; permission suffices.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis rebut by analogy to ijarah, which is also tamlik al-manafi', despite benefits being non-existent. The difference is the consideration, but ownership of benefits is established in both.
The Hanafi response shows consistency across contracts.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: according to Hanafis, the borrower may lend the item to a third party; according to the opponent, he cannot.
This difference has practical implications in daily lending.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العارية تعريفها وأحكامها
فمن استعار دابة، أو استأجر مطلقاً يحمل ويعير له
لأن العارية عنده إباحة الانتفاع ، والمباح له لا يملك الإباحة ، وعندنا : هي تمليك المنافع ، فالمستعيرُ لَمَّا مَلَكَ المنافع كان له أن يملكها غيره، فمن استعار دابة، أو استأجر مطلقاً يحملُ ويعير له ) : أي للحمل
،
الموجب المنتفع به للمستأجر أن يعيره سواءً اختلف استعماله أو لا، وإن عين لا يعير إلا ما يختلف استعماله ؛ لأنَّ الإجارة تمليك المنافع كالإعارة إلا أن الإجارة ببدل ،
والإعارة بلا بدل.
Book of 'Ariyah: its definition and rulings. Whoever borrows an animal, or hires it absolutely, may (use it for) carrying and lend it to others. Because according to (the opponent), 'Ariyah is the permission (ibahah) of use, and the one permitted does not own the permission to permit others. According to us (Hanafis): it is the ownership (tamlik) of benefits. So when the borrower owns the benefits, he may own them to others. So whoever borrows an animal, or hires absolutely, may carry and lend it (to others) — i.e., for carrying. The contracting party who benefits from the hire may lend it whether the use differs or not. If the use is specified, he may only lend what differs in use. Because ijarah is the ownership of benefits like 'ariyah, except that ijarah is for a consideration and 'ariyah is without consideration.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage opens the book on 'Ariyah (loans) by defining its legal nature. The author states that a borrower or hirer with an absolute (unrestricted) permission may use the item for carrying and may also lend it to others. The underlying dispute is then explained: According to the opposing school (likely al-Shafi'i), 'Ariyah is merely a permission (ibahah) to use the benefit, not a transfer of ownership. The permitted person cannot permit others. According to the Hanafi school, 'Ariyah is a transfer of ownership (tamlik) of the benefits. Therefore, the borrower owns the benefits and can transfer that ownership to another. This justifies why a borrower or hirer may lend the item if the contract is absolute. If the contract specifies a particular use, the borrower cannot lend it for a different use that causes more damage, but he may lend it for a use that is similar or less harmful. The commentator then reinforces that ijarah and 'ariyah both involve tamlik al-manafi', with the difference that ijarah involves a consideration while 'ariyah does not.

Key Terms
عارية ('ariyah): A contract where the owner gives possession of an item to another for use without compensation.
إباحة الانتفاع (ibahah al-intifa'): Permission to use without ownership of benefit.
تمليك المنافع (tamlik al-manafi'): Transfer of ownership of benefits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of 'Ariyah (loan)?
A) It is mere permission (ibahah) to use the benefit.
B) It is the ownership (tamlik) of the benefits of the item.
C) It is a sale of the item itself.
D) It is a gift of the item.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'Ariyah as tamlik al-manafi' (ownership of benefits), which gives the borrower the right to transfer the benefit to others, unlike the opposing view of ibahah al-intifa'.

Dispute on Borrower Depositing Borrowed Item
Internal Hanafi dispute on borrower's right to deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a borrower depositing a borrowed item and explain the reasoning of both sides.

Core Idea
The borrower may deposit the borrowed item because deposit is a lesser act than lending; permission for the greater implies permission for the lesser.

When you borrow an item, you gain lawful possession. But can you then deposit it with someone else for safekeeping? The Hanafi school has an internal debate. This lesson walks through the arguments and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in dispute
The issue is whether a borrower (musta'ir) may deposit the borrowed item with a third party as a wadi'ah.
2
State the minority position and their proof
Scholars like al-Karkhi and al-Baqillani say it is not allowed. They argue that deposit is a disposal of the physical item ('ayn) without explicit permission, and cite a case from al-Jami' where a borrower who sent the item via a messenger was held liable.
3
State the majority position and their proof
The majority (Iraqi scholars, Abu al-Layth, etc.) allow it. They reason through qiyas: since the borrower may lend the item (disposal of benefits), then depositing (a lesser disposal of the 'ayn) is also allowed.
4
Reinterpret the conflicting case
The case from al-Jami' is reinterpreted as applying only when the loan was time-bound and the term has expired, after which the borrower is a transgressor. Thus it does not refute general permissibility.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi ruling
The later authorities, such as al-Marghinani, place the fatwa on permissibility. The majority view is the operative ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : يحمل ويعير له ؛ واختلفوا في إيداع المستعير، قال بعض المشايخ: ليس له أن يودع مطلقاً، منهم: الكرخي ، واستدلوا عليه بمسألة ذكرها في «الجامع»: أنّ المستعير إذا بعث العارية إلى صاحبها على يد أجنبي فهلكت في يد الرسول ضَمِنَ المستعير العارية، وليس ذلك إلا إبداعاً منه.
له
قال الباقلاني : هذا القولُ أصح ؛ لأنَّ الإيداع تصرف في ملك الغير، وهو العين بغير إذنه قصداً، فلا يجوز بخلاف الإعارة ؛ لأنه تصرُّف في المنفعة قصداً وتسليم العين من ضروراته، فافترقا. وأكثرهم على أنه يجوز منهم : مشايخ العراق، وأبو الليث، والشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل، والصدر الكبير برهان الأئمة ؛ لأنَّ الإيداع دون الإعارة، والعين وديعة عند المستعير في العارية، فإذا ملك الأعلى فالأولى أن يملك الأدنى، قال ظهير الدين المرغيناني : وعليه الفتوى، ذكره الزيلعي) ، وغيره.
وجعل الفتوى على هذا في «السراجيّة» أيضاً، ومسألة «الجامع» محمولة على ما إذا كانت العارية مؤقتة فمضت مدتها ثمّ بعثَها مع الأجنبي؛ لأنه بإمساكها بعد مضي المدة يصير متعدّياً، حتى إذا هلكت في يده يضمن، فكذا إذا تركها في يد الأجنبي.
His saying: 'He may carry and lend it to him.' They differed regarding the borrower's depositing (of the borrowed item). Some scholars, including al-Karkhi, said: He may not deposit it at all. They argued based on a case mentioned in al-Jami': that if the borrower sends the borrowed item to its owner via a third party and it perishes in the hand of the messenger, the borrower is liable for the borrowed item. That is nothing but a deposit (wadi'ah) from him. al-Baqillani said: This opinion is more correct because deposit is a disposal in the property of another, i.e., the very item, without his permission intentionally, so it is not permissible, unlike lending ('ariyah), because lending is a disposal in the benefit intentionally and handing over the item is a necessary consequence, so they differ. The majority, including the scholars of Iraq, Abu al-Layth, the great Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, and the great master Burhan al-A'imma, are of the opinion that it is permissible, because deposit is lesser than lending, and the item is a trust (wadi'ah) in the hands of the borrower in 'ariyah. So if he owns the higher (lending), it is even more appropriate that he owns the lower (deposit). Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani said: And upon this is the fatwa, as mentioned by al-Zayla'i and others. The fatwa is also placed on this in al-Sirajiyyah. And the case from al-Jami' is interpreted as being when the 'ariyah was for a specific time, and that time expired, then he sent it with a stranger; because by keeping it after the expiry of the term he becomes a transgressor, so if it perishes in his hand, he is liable, and similarly if he leaves it in the hand of a stranger.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage addresses a secondary but important point: may the borrower (or hirer) deposit the borrowed item with a third party? The author presents the disagreement among Hanafi scholars. The source text (matn) says that the borrower may lend it to others, but what about depositing? Some early scholars, including Abu Bakr al-Karkhi, argued against it based on a case from al-Jami' al-Saghir of Imam Muhammad: if the borrower sends the borrowed animal to its owner via a messenger and it dies en route, the borrower is liable. They interpreted this as the borrower making a deposit, which led to liability. Al-Baqillani supported this view, reasoning that deposit involves disposal of the physical item (al-'ayn) itself without explicit permission, whereas lending is disposal of benefits, so they are different. However, the majority of later Hanafi scholars, including the Iraqi mashayikh, Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi, and others, permitted it. Their reasoning is that deposit is a lesser category than lending—since the borrower already has possession of the item as a trust, and he is allowed to lend the benefits, it follows that he can also deposit the item itself, which is a lower form of disposal. The text concludes that the fatwa is on the permissibility view, as affirmed by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and others. The case from al-Jami' is reinterpreted: it applies only to a time-bound loan that has expired, whereafter the borrower becomes a transgressor if he retains the item; thus his sending it with a stranger is an act of transgression, not a valid deposit. Therefore, it does not refute the general permissibility.

Key Terms
إيداع (ida'): Deposit; placing the item in the custody of another for safekeeping.
وديعة (wadi'ah): The item deposited as a trust.
عارية ('ariyah): Lending; the loan of a physical item for use.
مستعير (musta'ir): Borrower; the recipient of a loaned item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling regarding the borrower depositing the borrowed item with a third party?
A) It is absolutely prohibited.
B) It is permissible, and this is the fatwa.
C) It is prohibited except for a specific time-bound loan.
D) It is disliked (makruh).

Key Takeaway
The borrower may deposit the borrowed item; this is the relied-upon fatwa of the Hanafi school.

Borrower's Options and Liability in a Loan of an Animal
States the borrower's options in using a borrowed animal and liability for deviating after choosing a specific use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the options a borrower has when a loan (ariyah) is unrestricted, and determine when a borrower becomes liable for violating the terms of a restricted loan.

Core Idea
Once the borrower chooses a specific use of the borrowed property, it becomes fixed; changing to a different use incurs liability if it is worse.

When you borrow an animal, you have certain freedoms and responsibilities. This passage clarifies that in an unrestricted loan, you may either ride the animal yourself or give it to someone else to ride, but once you act, that choice becomes the sole permitted use. If the lender restricts the use (e.g., for carrying wheat), you must adhere, and any violation that is more harmful to the animal makes you liable. Understanding these rules protects both parties and ensures fair usage of borrowed property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of loan
Determine whether the loan is unrestricted (no specific use stated) or restricted (purpose and/or time specified). The ruling differs based on this.
If the lender says 'use it however you wish', it's unrestricted. If he says 'for carrying wheat only', it's restricted.
2
Determine the borrower's initial choice
In an unrestricted loan, the borrower has the option to ride or give to ride. Whichever he does first becomes the fixed intended use. In a restricted loan, the borrower must follow the specified use.
Once the borrower acts (e.g., rides), that use is considered as if it were stipulated in the contract.
3
Check for violation
If the borrower later does a different action (e.g., after riding, he gives to ride), he has violated the fixed use. In a restricted loan, check if the violation is to a worse condition.
Violation to a better or similar condition does not incur liability.
4
Assess liability
If the borrower changes after his choice has become fixed, he is liable for any damage. In a restricted loan, liability only arises if the change is to a worse condition.
The liability attaches even if the damage occurs during the second use.
5
Apply the principle
Given a concrete scenario, decide: (1) Was the loan restricted? (2) Did the borrower’s first action fix a use? (3) Did he change? (4) Was the change to a worse condition? Then state the ruling.
Use the examples from the passage: carrying iron instead of wheat is worse, so liable; carrying similar wheat is not worse, so no liability.

Source Text

قوله : ويركب ويُركب ؛ يعني للمستعير أن يركب عليها بنفسه، وإن شاء أركب غيره، لا أنه يركب غيره معه ؛ لأنه صرح في «الهداية) بقوله : حتى لو ركب بنفسه ليس له أن يُركب غيره ؛ لأنه تعيّن ركوبَه ، ولو أركب غيره ليس له أن يركبه بنفسه، حتى لو فعله ضَمِن ؛ لأنه تعين الإركاب. انتهى.
وإليه أشار المصنّف له بقوله : وأياه فعل تعيَّن وضَمِنَ بغيره. انتهى. ووجه جواز الركوب والإركاب أنَّ المعير ما لم يعين المنتفع يكون تعين المنتفع مفوضاً إلى المستعير فإن شاء عين نفسه وركب، وإن شاءَ عيّن غيره وأركبه ، وأيهما عينه تعين، كما إذا
عينه المالك.
(٢) قوله : وأياً فعل ؛ المستعير أو المستأجر من الحمل أو الركوب أو الإركاب تعين ذلك الفعل، فليس له أن يفعل غيره، ضمن كلِّ منهما إن عطبت ؛ لأن ما وقع أولاً تعين مراداً بالعقد، وصار كأنه منصوص عليه.
(۳) قوله : وإن قيد بهما ... الخ ؛ يعني إن المعير إن قيد الإعارة بنوع أو وقت ضَمِنَ المستعير إذا خالف المقيد إذا كان خلافاً إلى شرِّ كما إذا استعار دابة ليحمل عليها حنطة فحمل عليها حطباً أو حديداً مثل وزن الحنطة يضمن ؛ لأنَّ الأوّل يأخذ من ظهر الدابة أكثر، والثاني مما يدق ظهر الدابة فيكون أضر. [٤] قوله : فقط ؛ إنما قيَّد بقوله : فقط احترازاً عن الخلاف إلى خير أو مثل، فإنّه لا يضمن حينئذ، كما إذا استعار دابّة ليحمل عليها مقدار كذا . من الحنطة، فحمل عليها
حنطة أخرى مثلها.
He may ride and may give (another) to ride. And whichever action he does, it becomes fixed, and he is liable for the other. And if he gives absolute freedom of use in time and type, he may use as he wishes at any time he wishes. And if he restricts by them (time and type), he is liable for violating the restriction only if the violation is to a worse (condition). His saying: He may ride and give to ride means the borrower may ride the animal himself, and if he wishes, he may give another to ride, but not that he rides another with him. Because it is stated in al-Hidayah: If he rides himself, he may not give another to ride, because his riding becomes fixed. And if he gives another to ride, he may not ride himself, even if he does so, he is liable, because the giving to ride becomes fixed. End. And the author alluded to this by his saying: 'And whichever action he does, it becomes fixed, and he is liable for the other.' And the reason for the permissibility of riding and giving to ride is that as long as the lender has not specified the beneficiary, the specification of the beneficiary is delegated to the borrower. So if he wishes, he specifies himself and rides; if he wishes, he specifies another and gives him to ride. And whichever he specifies becomes fixed, just as if the owner had specified it. (2) His saying: 'And whichever action he does' refers to the borrower or hirer of carrying, riding, or giving to ride, that action becomes fixed, so he may not do another. Each of them is liable if (the animal) is ruined, because what occurred first becomes fixed as intended by the contract, and becomes as if it was explicitly stipulated. (3) His saying: 'And if he restricts by them...' means that if the lender restricts the loan by type or time, the borrower is liable if he violates the restriction, provided the violation is to a worse condition. For example, if he borrows an animal to carry wheat on it, and he carries firewood or iron of the same weight as the wheat, he is liable, because the former takes more from the back of the animal, and the second damages the back of the animal, thus being more harmful. (4) His saying: 'Only' — he qualified with 'only' to exclude a violation to a better or similar condition, in which case he is not liable. For example, if he borrows an animal to carry a certain amount of wheat, and he carries other wheat similar to it.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage explains the borrower's options when the loan is unrestricted. The borrower may either ride the animal himself or give it to another to ride. However, he cannot do both; once he chooses one option (e.g., riding himself), that becomes the fixed purpose of the loan, and if he later does the other (gives to ride), he becomes liable for any damage. This is because the specific use becomes implied in the contract the moment he acts. The same applies to a hirer regarding carrying, riding, or giving to ride. The second part discusses restrictions: If the lender restricts the use to a specific type (e.g., carrying wheat) or a specific time, the borrower must adhere. If he violates the restriction, he is liable only if the violation results in a worse condition for the animal. For example, carrying iron instead of wheat of the same weight is worse because iron is denser and damages the back more. However, if he carries something equivalent or better (e.g., different wheat of same weight), he is not liable. The key principle is that the borrower's liability arises only from a change to a more harmful use; changes to similar or less harmful uses are permissible.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyana): Becomes fixed/determined as the intended use.
ضمن (damina): He becomes liable (for compensation).
خلاف إلى شر (khilaf ila sharr): Violation to a worse condition (that increases harm to the property).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The borrower is not liable because he had permission.
B) The borrower is liable because his riding became fixed, and he changed to giving to ride.
C) The lender is liable for not specifying the use.
D) The person riding is liable.

Key Takeaway
In a loan of an animal, the borrower's initial action fixes the permitted use; any deviation that is more harmful triggers liability.

Liability for Violating Restrictions on a Borrowed Animal
Commentary on the ruling of riding and making ride in borrowed animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on liability when a borrower violates restrictions on a borrowed animal and distinguish between violations that incur liability and those that do not.

Core Idea
Violation of loan restrictions incurs liability only if the alternative is worse for the item.

A borrowed animal is a trust, but the borrower must adhere to the lender's restrictions. This lesson explores a key Hanafi nuance: once the borrower chooses how to use the animal (e.g., riding or letting another ride), that choice becomes fixed. If the lender specifies a type or time of use, the borrower is liable only if he deviates to a worse alternative. Understanding this rule prevents unjust claims of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the borrower's options
The borrower may either ride the animal himself or let another person ride it. These are the two primary forms of use discussed.
2
Understand the concept of ta'ayyun (determination)
Once the borrower chooses one option (e.g., riding), that action becomes fixed (ta'ayyan). He cannot switch to the other option without the lender's permission; if he does, he is liable for any resulting damage.
This is because the first choice is considered the intended use under the contract.
3
Analyze the impact of lender's restrictions
If the lender specifies a type of use (e.g., carrying wheat) or a time, the borrower must adhere. Violation triggers potential liability.
Restrictions are binding.
4
Apply the 'worse alternative' rule
Liability only arises if the violation is to a worse alternative (e.g., iron instead of wheat). If the alternative is similar (e.g., a different type of wheat same weight) or better, there is no liability.
Worse is measured by harm to the animal, e.g., iron damages the back more.
5
Note exceptions and underlying proof
The rule is grounded in the principle that the borrower's choice becomes as if stipulated. The commentary cites al-Hidayah to support this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويَرْكَبُ ويُرْكِب، وأياً فعل تعين وضَمِنَ بغيره. وإن أطلق الانتفاع في الوقتِ والنوع انتفع ما شاء أي وقت شاء ، وإن قيد بهما ضمن بالخلاف إلى شرّ فقط ( ويَرْكَبُ ويُركب ، وأياً فعل تعين ، وضَمِنَ بغيره. وإن أطلق الانتفاع في الوقتِ والنوع انتفعَ ما شاء أي وقت شاء ، وإن قيد بهما ضمن بالخلاف إلى شر فقط ) قوله : ويركب ويُركب ؛ يعني للمستعير أن يركب عليها بنفسه، وإن شاء أركب غيره، لا أنه يركب غيره معه ؛ لأنه صرح في «الهداية) بقوله : حتى لو ركب بنفسه ليس له أن يُركب غيره ؛ لأنه تعيّن ركوبَه ، ولو أركب غيره ليس له أن يركبه بنفسه، حتى لو فعله ضَمِن ؛ لأنه تعين الإركاب. انتهى. وإليه أشار المصنّف له بقوله : وأياه فعل تعيَّن وضَمِنَ بغيره. انتهى. ووجه جواز الركوب والإركاب أنَّ المعير ما لم يعين المنتفع يكون تعين المنتفع مفوضاً إلى المستعير فإن شاء عين نفسه وركب، وإن شاءَ عيّن غيره وأركبه ، وأيهما عينه تعين، كما إذا عينه المالك. (٢) قوله : وأياً فعل ؛ المستعير أو المستأجر من الحمل أو الركوب أو الإركاب تعين ذلك الفعل، فليس له أن يفعل غيره، ضمن كلِّ منهما إن عطبت ؛ لأن ما وقع أولاً تعين مراداً بالعقد، وصار كأنه منصوص عليه. (۳) قوله : وإن قيد بهما ... الخ ؛ يعني إن المعير إن قيد الإعارة بنوع أو وقت ضَمِنَ المستعير إذا خالف المقيد إذا كان خلافاً إلى شرِّ كما إذا استعار دابة ليحمل عليها حنطة فحمل عليها حطباً أو حديداً مثل وزن الحنطة يضمن ؛ لأنَّ الأوّل يأخذ من ظهر الدابة أكثر، والثاني مما يدق ظهر الدابة فيكون أضر. [٤] قوله : فقط ؛ إنما قيَّد بقوله : فقط احترازاً عن الخلاف إلى خير أو مثل، فإنّه لا يضمن حينئذ، كما إذا استعار دابّة ليحمل عليها مقدار كذا من الحنطة، فحمل عليها حنطة أخرى مثلها.
And he rides and makes ride, and whichever he does becomes determined and he is liable for [doing] other than it. And if he makes unrestricted use in time and type, he may use as he wishes at any time he wishes. And if [the lender] restricts both, he is liable [if he violates] to a worse [alternative] only. He said: 'and he rides and makes ride' meaning the borrower may ride it himself, and if he wishes may make another ride, not that he rides another with him; because it is stated in al-Hidayah: 'Even if he rides himself, he may not make another ride, because his riding became determined, and if he made another ride, he may not himself ride it, even if he does so he is liable because the making-ride became determined.' End. And the author pointed to this by his saying: 'and whichever he does becomes determined and he is liable for other than it.' End. And the reason for permissibility of riding and making-ride is that as long as the lender has not specified the beneficiary, the specification of the beneficiary is delegated to the borrower. If he wishes he specifies himself and rides, and if he wishes he specifies another and makes him ride, and whichever he specifies becomes determined, just as if the lender had specified it. (2) His saying: 'and whichever he does' the borrower or lessee, whether carrying or riding or making-ride, that action becomes determined, so he may not do other than it, each of them is liable if it is destroyed, because what occurred first became intended by the contract and became as if it were stipulated. (3) His saying: 'and if he restricts both...etc.' means if the lender restricts the loan by type or time, the borrower is liable if he violates the restriction if the violation is to a worse [alternative], as if he borrows an animal to carry wheat on it and carries firewood or iron of equal weight of wheat, he is liable because the first takes more from the animal's back, and the second pierces the animal's back and is more harmful. [4] His saying: 'only' he restricted by saying 'only' as a precaution against violation to a better or similar, for then he is not liable, as if he borrows an animal to carry a certain amount of wheat and carries other wheat similar to it.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the law of borrowing (al-ʿāriyah). The commentator explains the rules for the borrower's use of a borrowed animal. First, the borrower has the option to either ride the animal himself or let another person ride it. However, once he chooses one of these actions, that action becomes fixed (taʿayyana), and he cannot switch to the other. If he does switch and the animal is destroyed as a result, he is liable (ḍāmin) for its value. This is because the first action is considered the intended use of the contract. Second, if the lender places no restrictions on the time or type of use, the borrower may use the animal in any manner and at any time he wishes. But if the lender specifies a particular type of use (e.g., carrying wheat) or a specific time, then the borrower must adhere to that restriction. If he violates it, his liability depends on the nature of the violation: if he uses the animal in a way that is worse (sharr) for it, such as carrying iron instead of wheat of equal weight, he is liable because iron damages the animal's back more. However, if he uses it in a way that is equally good (mithl) or better (khayr), he is not liable. For example, if he was restricted to carrying a certain amount of wheat, but carries the same weight of another type of wheat, there is no liability because the animal is not worse off. The commentator cites al-Hidayah to support that once the borrower rides himself, he may not later make another ride, and vice versa. The proof is that the lender's delegation of choice to the borrower gives the borrower the authority to determine the specific use, but once determined, it is as if it were stipulated in the contract.

Key Terms
عارية (ʿāriyah): A contract where the owner of a usable object permits another to benefit from it without compensation.
مستعير (mustaʿīr): The person who borrows the animal; the borrower.
معير (muʿīr): The owner who lends the animal; the lender.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for destruction or damage of the borrowed item due to violation of terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A borrower borrows a horse to carry firewood but instead carries iron of equal weight. The horse dies from the load. Is the borrower liable?
A) Yes, because iron is worse for the horse than firewood.
B) No, because the weight is the same.
C) Yes, because he violated the restriction regardless.
D) No, because the lender should have specified the type of wood.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a borrower is liable for violating a lender's restrictions only if the alternative use is worse for the borrowed item.

Valid Methods of Returning a Borrowed Animal
Commentary on valid delivery methods in loans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the valid methods of returning a borrowed animal to its owner and identify which delivery method discharges the borrower's liability.

Core Idea
Delivering a borrowed animal to the owner's stable or with a household member discharges liability; daily hired workers are not household.

In the Hanafi school, the borrower of an animal remains liable for its safety until it is validly returned to the owner. This lesson examines authentic methods of return recognized by the school, including delivery to the owner's stable or with certain household members. The passage also illustrates the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) overriding qiyas (analogy) in the case of transporting a different type of grain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses when a borrower's liability for a borrowed animal ends after returning it. The key is whether the method of return constitutes valid delivery (taslīm).
Liability in ʿāriyah is a fiduciary duty (amānah).
2
List the valid delivery methods
From the text: (1) returning to the owner's stable, (2) sending with the owner's slave, (3) sending with the owner's hired worker employed by month or year, (4) sending with the owner's hired worker or slave who tends the animal. These are considered household members.
The stable is the customary place for animals.
3
Distinguish daily hired workers
A daily hired worker (ajīr mīyawamah) is not considered part of the owner's household. Delivery to such a person does not discharge the borrower's liability.
Household (ʿiyālah) is the key concept.
4
Understand the istihsan rule
The passage includes a case where the borrower carries barley instead of wheat. By qiyas, changing the type would incur liability. By istihsan, if the substitute is similar or better (barley is lighter), no liability arises.
Istihsan overrides qiyas based on custom and ease.
5
Conclude the ruling
The muftā bihī position: valid delivery is as per custom and household membership. The borrower must ensure the animal reaches the owner or a recognized representative.
Authority: Zahir al-Riwayah and the dominant Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العارية تعريفها وأحكامها وكذا تقييد الإجارة بنوع أو قدر، وردّها إلى اصطبل مالكها ، أو مع عبده، أو أجيره مسانهة، أو مشاهرة، أو مع أجير ربها، أو عبدِهِ يقومُ على دابتِهِ أو لا تسليم التقييد إما أن يكونَ في الوقتِ دون النوع ، أو في النوع دون الوقت، أو فيهما، فإن عَمِلَ على موافقة القيدِ فظاهر وإن خالف، فإن كان الخلاف إلى مثل، أو إلى خير لا يضمن، وإلى شر يضمن، وكذا تقييد الإجارة بنوع أو قدر) : أي إن وافق ، أو خالف إلى مثل ، أو خير لا يضمن، وإلى شر يضمن. [۱] وردها إلى اصطبل مالكها ، أو مع عبده، أو أجيره مسانهة، أو مشاهرة أو مع أجير ربها ، أو عبده يقومُ على دابتِهِ أو لا تسليم) : أي رد الدابة إلى اصطبل مالكها، فهلكت قبل الوصول إلى المالك لا يضمن " ، لأنَّ هذا تسليم، وكذا إن أرسلها المستعير مع عبده إلى المالك، فهلكَتْ قبل الوصول إليه، وكذا إن أرسلها مع أجيره مسانهة أو مشاهرة بخلاف أجيره مياومة ؛ إذ ليس في عياله، فيضمن بالتسليم إليه وكما إذا استعارها ليحمل عليها عشرة أقفزة من الحنطة فحمل عليها عشرة أقفزة من الشعير، فإنّه لا يضمن استحساناً ؛ لأنَّ مثل كيل الحنطة من الشعير أخف على الدابة كما ذكرنا سابقاً، وإن كان القياس الضمان ؛ لأنه خالف في الجنس. هو قوله : وردّها إلى اصطبل ...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ تسليم الدابة إلى مالكها وجوهاً : أحدها: أن يردها إلى اصطبل مالكها ، وهو موضع الدواب، فإنّه تسليم على ما المتعارف. وثانيهما : أن يردّها على يد بعض من في عياله سواء كان عبده أو أجيره مسانهةً أو مشاهرة فإنّها أمانة ، فله أن يحفظها بيد من في عيالة، كما في الوديعة، بخلاف أجير مياومة، فإنّه ليس في عيالة فلو ردّها على يده ضمن. وثالثها: أن يردها على يدِ مَن في عيال مالك الدابة وهو المعير، سواء كان أجيره أو عبده. [٢] قوله : لا يضمن ؛ وهذا استحسان وفي القياس : يضمن ؛ لأنه لم يتحقق ردُّها إلى مالكها ، بل ضيعها، ووجه الاستحسان ما أشار إليه الشارح الله بقوله : لأنّ
The book of ʿāriyah, its definition and rulings. And likewise the restriction of hire by type or amount, and returning it to the stable of its owner, or with his slave, or his hired worker for month or year, or with the owner's hired worker, or his slave who tends to his animal, or no delivery. The restriction is either in time not type, or type not time, or both. If he acts in accordance with the restriction [it is] apparent, and if he violates, if the violation is to similar or better, he is not liable, and to worse, he is liable. And likewise the restriction of hire by type or amount: meaning if he agrees, or violates to similar or better, he is not liable, and to worse, he is liable. [1] His saying: 'and returning it to the stable of its owner, or with his slave, or his hired worker for month or year, or with the owner's hired worker, or his slave who tends to his animal, or no delivery' means: returning the animal to the stable of its owner, if it perishes before reaching the owner, he is not liable because this is delivery. Likewise if the borrower sends it with his slave to the owner, and it perishes before reaching him, [he is not liable]. Likewise if he sends it with his hired worker for month or year, unlike a daily hired worker, because he is not of his household, so he is liable for delivery to him. And as if he borrows it to carry ten qafiz of wheat and carries ten qafiz of barley, he is not liable by istihsan because a similar measure of barley is lighter on the animal as we mentioned earlier, even though by qiyas he would be liable because he changed the type. He said: 'and returning it to the stable...etc.' The summary: delivery of the animal to its owner has several methods: one: to return it to the owner's stable, which is the place of animals, for this is delivery according to custom. Second: to return it by the hand of some of his household, whether his slave or his hired worker for month or year, for it is a trust, so he may keep it with a person of his household, as in deposit, unlike a daily hired worker, for he is not of his household, so if he returns it by his hand, he is liable. Third: to return it by the hand of someone in the household of the owner of the animal, i.e., the lender, whether his hired worker or his slave. [2] His saying: 'he is not liable' this is istihsan, and by qiyas he would be liable because he did not actually return it to its owner, rather he neglected it. The basis of istihsan is that, as the commentator indicated by his saying: 'because...'
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the chapter of ʿāriyah (loans). It discusses two main issues: first, the restriction in hire (ijārah) by type or amount follows the same rule as in ʿāriyah—violation to a worse alternative incurs liability, but to similar or better does not. Second, the valid methods of returning a borrowed animal to its owner. The commentator explains that returning the animal to the owner's stable is considered valid delivery. Similarly, sending it with the owner's slave or with a hired worker who is employed by the year or month (but not a daily hired worker) constitutes valid delivery, because such persons are considered part of the owner's household (ʿiyālah). If the animal perishes after such delivery but before reaching the owner, the borrower is not liable. The daily hired worker is not considered part of the household, so if the borrower sends the animal with him, he remains liable. The commentator also discusses the case where the borrower carries barley instead of wheat: by istihsan (juristic preference), he is not liable because barley is lighter, even though by qiyas (analogy) he would be liable for changing the type. The passage highlights the Hanafi reliance on istihsan over strict qiyas in matters of fiduciary responsibility (amānah). The text also quotes from al-Hidayah to support these rulings.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides strict analogy (qiyas) due to custom, necessity, or other considerations.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning that would impose liability for changing the type, but istihsan overrides it in this case.
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery of the borrowed animal back to the owner or his valid representative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a borrower returns a borrowed horse by handing it over to the owner's daily hired worker, and the horse dies before reaching the owner, who bears the liability?
A) The borrower is liable because a daily hired worker is not considered part of the owner's household.
B) The owner is liable because the animal was returned to his representative.
C) The daily hired worker is liable.
D) Neither is liable as the return was in good faith.

Key Takeaway
Valid delivery of a borrowed animal requires returning it to the owner's stable or household member; otherwise, the borrower remains liable.

Valid Modes of Returning a Borrowed Animal and Liability
Explaining valid modes of returning a borrowed animal and liability conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the valid modes of returning a borrowed animal and APPLY the rule that the borrower is not liable when delivery is made according to custom.

Core Idea
Delivery of a borrowed animal is valid by custom: stable or household members; precious items need direct handover.

When you borrow an animal, you must return it in a way the owner would accept. This lesson explains what counts as valid delivery to avoid being held liable if the animal is lost after you return it. The key is custom ('urf) and whether the recipient is part of the owner's household.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the borrowed item
Is it a non-precious item (like an ordinary animal) or a precious item (like a jewel)? This determines whether leaving it at the owner's house suffices.
Precious items must be handed directly to the owner.
2
Recognize valid delivery modes for non-precious items
Delivery is valid if you place the animal in the owner's stable, or hand it to his slave, or a long-term hired worker (yearly or monthly). These are customary ways of returning.
Custom defines valid delivery.
3
Distinguish household members from outsiders
A daily hired worker is not considered part of the household, so handing the animal to him does not discharge you. You remain liable for loss.
The key is whether the recipient is in the owner's 'iyal (dependents).
4
Apply the istihsan rule for minor violations
If you violate the terms of use (e.g., carry barley instead of wheat) but the change is to something lighter, you are not liable by istihsan, even though qiyas would impose liability.
Istihsan overrides qiyas to avoid hardship.
5
Contrast with wadi'ah and ghasb
For deposits (wadi'ah) and usurped property (ghasb), leaving the item at the owner's house is not sufficient; you must hand it over directly because the owner's implicit consent is lacking.
The borrower is a trustee; the usurper is a wrongdoer.

Source Text

Book of al-Ariyah: Its definition and rulings. Likewise, restricting the hiring (ijarah) by type or amount, and returning it to its owner's stable, or with his slave, or his hired worker on a yearly or monthly basis, or with the owner's hired worker or his slave tending his animal or not — delivery. Restriction either in time without type, or in type without time, or in both. If he acts in accordance with the restriction, it is apparent. If he violates, then if the violation is to something similar or better, he is not liable; if to something worse, he is liable. Likewise, restricting the ijarah by type or amount: i.e., if he conforms, or violates to similar or better, he is not liable; if to worse, he is liable. And returning it to its owner's stable, or with his slave, or his hired worker on a yearly or monthly basis, or with the owner's hired worker or his slave tending his animal or not — delivery: i.e., returning the mount to its owner's stable, and it is destroyed before reaching the owner, he is not liable, because this is delivery. Likewise, if the borrower sends it with his slave to the owner and it is destroyed before reaching him, and likewise if he sends it with his hired worker on a yearly or monthly basis, unlike his daily hired worker, because he is not of his household, so he is liable for delivery to him. And as if he borrowed it to carry ten qafiz of wheat on it but carried ten qafiz of barley on it: then he is not liable by istihsan, because the like of the measure of wheat from barley is lighter on the mount, as we mentioned earlier, even though qiyas would require liability because he violated in type. He said: 'And returning it to the stable... etc.' Summary: The delivery of the mount to its owner has three ways: First, to return it to its owner's stable, which is the place of mounts, and it is delivery according to custom. Second, to return it in the hand of someone of his household, whether his slave or his hired worker on a yearly or monthly basis, for it is a trust, so he may keep it with someone of his household, as in wadi'ah, unlike a daily hired worker, who is not of his household, so if he returns it in his hand he is liable. Third, to return it in the hand of someone in the household of the owner of the mount (the lender), whether his hired worker or his slave. He said: 'He is not liable': this is istihsan, and in qiyas he is liable because he did not actually return it to its owner, but rather lost it. The basis of istihsan is that it is like returning a non-precious borrowed item to its owner's house, unlike returning wadi'ah and ghasb to their owner's houses. Likewise, if he delivers it to the owner's hired worker or his slave, whether he tends the animals or not, and it is destroyed before reaching the owner, he is not liable, and this is the most correct (al-ashah). It was said: he is liable for delivery to his slave who does not tend the animals. So the issue indicates that the borrower does not have the right to deposit (ibda'). Returning a non-precious borrowed item to its owner's house: this is delivery, unlike a precious borrowed item like jewels, which is only returned to the lender. Unlike returning wadi'ah and ghasb to their owner's houses, for that is not delivery, but rather it must be returned to the owner. This delivery is established as delivery according to custom, because returning borrowed items to the owner's house is customary, just as household utensils are borrowed and then returned to the house. He said: 'Likewise, if he delivers it to the owner's hired worker... etc.' i.e., he is not liable in this case either, because this is delivery, for al-ariyah is a trust, so he may keep it with someone of his household, as in wadi'ah as mentioned. He said: 'He is not liable' because the owner is pleased with it; do you not see that if the borrower returns it to the owner, he returns it to his slave. He said: 'This is the most correct' because even if he does not always hand it over, he hands it over sometimes, so the owner's contentment is indicated implicitly. He said: 'Non-precious' — he restricted it to non-precious, because precious items like jewels are not normally delivered except into the hand of their owner. By 'non-precious' is meant what is not of great value, like a jug and the like. He said: 'Unlike returning wadi'ah... etc.' i.e., unlike when the depositor returns the wadi'ah to its owner's house, and the usurper returns the ghasb to its owner's house, that is not delivery legally. He said: 'Rather, it must be returned to the owner' because wadi'ah is only for custody, and the owner did not consent to another's custody of that deposited item, for if he had consented, he would not have deposited it with him. And the usurper is a transgressor by placing his hand on that ghasb, so its removal cannot be except by actual delivery to him.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the rules of returning a borrowed animal ('ariyah). The commentator explains that the borrower is not liable for loss if he returns the animal by customary means: placing it in the owner's stable, or handing it to a member of the owner's household (e.g., his slave or a long-term hired worker). The daily hired worker is not considered part of the household, so delivery to him incurs liability. The passage also discusses cases where the borrower violates the terms of use, such as carrying barley instead of wheat: by istihsan, he is not liable because barley is lighter, but qiyas would impose liability for changing the type. The key principle is that the borrower is a trustee (amin), and delivery must be in a manner that the owner would accept. Precious items must be returned directly to the owner. In contrast, for wadi'ah (deposit) and ghasb (usurpation), leaving the item in the owner's house is not sufficient — actual handover is required because the owner's consent is not implicit.

Key Terms
العَارِيَة (al-ariyah): A loan of a non-consumable item for free use, with the obligation to return it.
التَّسْلِيم (al-taslim): Delivery of the borrowed item back to the owner in a legally recognized manner.
الضَّمَان (al-daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.
الاِسْتِحْسَان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more fitting ruling, often based on custom or necessity.
النَّفِيس (al-nafis): Precious item of significant value, e.g., jewels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following scenarios is the borrower NOT liable for the loss of a borrowed animal?
A) The borrower sends the animal with the owner's daily hired worker, and it is lost before reaching the owner.
B) The borrower sends the animal with the owner's yearly hired worker, and it is lost before reaching the owner.
C) The borrower carries barley instead of wheat as agreed, and the animal is lost.
D) The borrower returns a precious borrowed jewel to the owner's house, and it is lost.

Key Takeaway
The borrower's liability depends on delivering the borrowed item in a manner that is customary and accepted by the owner; non-precious items may be left at the owner's house or with household members, but precious items require direct handover.

Returning a Non-Valuable Borrowed Item to the Owner's House or Employee
Explains ruling on returning non-valuable borrowed items and delivery to employee/slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the return of a non-valuable borrowed item and explain why custom (urf) serves as the proof, while also distinguishing this from valuable items, trusts, and usurped property.

Core Idea
Returning a non-valuable borrowed item to the owner’s house is valid delivery; custom implies consent.

This passage tackles a practical question: how must a borrowed item be returned? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on value and custom. For ordinary items, dropping them off at the owner’s home or handing them to an employee suffices. But for valuable goods, trusts, or stolen property, direct handover is required. The reasoning hinges on customary practice and implied consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its classification
The act is the return of a borrowed item (ʿāriyah). The passage distinguishes between valuable and non-valuable items, and between mere borrowing and other relationships (trust, usurpation).
Non-valuable items are like household tools; valuable are like jewels.
2
State the ruling for non-valuable items
Returning a non-valuable borrowed item to the owner's house is valid delivery. The borrower is not liable if it perishes afterwards. Similarly, delivery to the owner's employee or slave, even one who does not tend animals, does not impose liability.
The relied-upon position (al-asaḥḥ) is no liability. A minority opinion held liability for delivery to a slave not tending animals, but this is rejected.
3
Identify the proof (dalīl)
The proof is custom (urf): it is customary to return borrowed items to the owner's house. Custom implies the owner's consent to such delivery. The passage also notes that borrowing is a trust (amānah), and the borrower may keep it with the owner's household.
The dalīl is aqli (rational) based on custom, not a text.
4
Distinguish from other cases
Valuable items (e.g., jewels) must be returned directly to the lender. Trusts (wadīʿah) and usurped items (maghsūb) also require direct return to the owner, because the owner has not consented to leaving them at his house.
For trusts, the owner chose the trustee specifically; for usurped items, the wrongdoer must personally undo the wrong.
