زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
كرد مستعار غير نفيس إلى دار مالكه ، بخلاف رد الوديعة والمغصوب إلى دار مالكهما
وكذا إن سلمها إلى أجير المالك، أو عبده سواء يقوم على الدواب أو لا، فهلكت قبل الوصول إلى المالك لا يضمن " ، وهو الأصح ، وقيل : يضمن بالتسليم إلى عبده الذي لا يقوم على الدواب، فدلّلت المسألة على أنَّ المستعير لا يَمْلِك الابداع. کرد مستعار غیر نفيس إلى دار مالكه ، فإنّ هذا تسليم بخلاف المستعار النفيس كالجواهر حيث لا يرد إلا إلى المعير، بخلاف ردّ الوديعة والمغصوب إلى دار مالكهما، فإن هذا لا يكون تسليماً ، بل لا بد من الرِّد إلى المالك". هذا التسليم، وتقريره : إنّه تسليم على ما هو المتعارف ؛ لأنَّ ردّ العواري إلى دار المالك معتاد، كما أن آلات البيوت تعار ثم تردُّ إلى الدار.
[١]قوله : وكذا إن سلمها إلى أجير المالك...الخ ؛ يعني وكذا لا يضمن في هذه الصورة ؛ لأنَّ هذا ،تسليم فإنَّ العارية أمانة، فله أن يحفظها بيد من في عياله، كما في الوديعة كما ذكرنا.
6
[٢]قوله : لا يضمن ؛ لأنَّ المالك يرضى به ، ألا ترى أنه لو رده المستعير إلى المالك فهو يرده إلى عبده.
(3) قوله : وهو الأصح ؛ لأنه إن كان لا يدفع إليه دائماً يدفع إليه أحياناً، فرضاء المالك يكون موجودا دلالة.
[٤]قوله : غير نفيس ؛ قيَّد بغير النفيس ؛ لأنَّ النفيس كالجوهر لا يسلّم في العادة إلا إلى يد مالكه ، والمراد بغيرِ النفيس ما لا يكون كثير القيمة، كالكوز ونحوه. [0] قوله : بخلاف رد الوديعة.. الخ ؛ يعني بخلاف ما إذا رد المودع الوديعة إلى دار مالكها، والغاصب المغصوب إلى دار مالكه ، فهو ليس بتسليم شرعا. [٦] قوله : بل لا بد من الردّ إلى المالك ؛ فإنَّ الوديعة لا تكون إلا للحفظ، ولم يرض المالك بحفظ غيره ذلك المودَع ، إذ لو رضي به لَمَا أودعها . عنده، والغاصب متعد بإثباتِ يده في ذلك المغصوب فلا تكون إزالتها إلا بالتسيلم إليه حقيقة.
Essence of the Finality for the Support of the Care: A borrowed item that is not valuable is returned to its owner's house, unlike returning a trust and a usurped item to their owners' houses. Likewise, if he delivers it to the owner's hired worker or his slave, whether he tends to animals or not, and it perishes before reaching the owner, he is not liable, and this is the most correct. And it is said: he is liable for delivering to a slave who does not tend to animals. So the issue indicates that the borrower does not have the right to deposit it. A borrowed non-valuable item to its owner's house: this is delivery, unlike a valuable borrowed item like jewels, which are only returned to the lender, unlike returning a trust and a usurped item to their owners' houses, for this is not considered delivery; rather, it must be returned to the owner. This delivery is affirmed as it is customary; returning borrowed items to the owner's house is customary, just as household tools are borrowed and then returned to the house. [1] His statement: 'Likewise, if he delivers it to the owner's hired worker...' means likewise he is not liable in this case because this is delivery, as the borrowed item is a trust, so he may keep it with someone in his household, as with the trust as we mentioned. [2] His statement: 'He is not liable' because the owner is satisfied with it; do you not see that if the borrower returns it to the owner, he returns it to his slave. (3) His statement: 'This is the most correct' because if he does not give it to him always, he gives it to him sometimes, so the owner's satisfaction is indicated. [4] His statement: 'Not valuable' limits to non-valuable because a valuable item like a jewel is not delivered customarily except to its owner's hand, and by 'non-valuable' is meant what is not of great value, like a pitcher and the like. [0] His statement: 'Unlike returning a trust...' means unlike when the trustee returns the trust to its owner's house, and the usurper the usurped item to its owner's house, it is not delivery legally. [6] His statement: 'Rather, it must be returned to the owner' because a trust is only for safekeeping, and the owner does not consent to someone else keeping that deposited item, for if he consented, he would not have deposited it with him. And the usurper is transgressing by establishing his hand on that usurped item, so its removal is only by actual delivery to him.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn (likely from al-Wiqayah) regarding the return of borrowed items. The author first states that a non-valuable borrowed item (like a ordinary pot) may be returned to the owner's house, and this constitutes valid delivery. This is based on custom: people commonly return borrowed household items to the owner's home. However, this ruling does not apply to trusts (wadi'ah) or usurped items (maghsūb), which must be returned directly to the owner. Next, the passage discusses delivering the borrowed item to the owner's employee or slave. The relied-upon position (al-asaḥḥ) is that the borrower is not liable if the item perishes after such delivery, because the owner implicitly consents to his employee receiving it. A minority view holds the borrower liable if delivered to a slave who does not tend to animals, but this is rejected. The passage also highlights that the borrower does not have the right to entrust the item to someone else (i.e., sub-bailment) unless authorized. The key distinction is between valuable items (like jewels) which require direct handover to the lender, and non-valuable items. For trusts and usurped items, stronger protection is afforded to the owner's property, so mere deposit at the owner's house is insufficient.

Key Terms
عارية (ʿāriyah): Lending of an item for use without compensation; a trust (amana) in the borrower's hands.
ابداع (ībdāʿ): Entrusting a borrowed item to a third party for safekeeping without permission.
نفيس (nafīs): Valuable; of great monetary worth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is returning a non-valuable borrowed item to the owner's house considered valid delivery?
A) Because the Quran commands it.
B) Because it is customary and implies the owner's consent.
C) Because the borrower is free to choose any method.
D) Because the item is not valuable, so the owner does not care.

Key Takeaway
For non-valuable borrowed items, returning them to the owner's house or to his employee is valid delivery based on custom; the borrower is not liable if the item perishes thereafter.

Borrowing Fungible Items: Treated as Loan Unless Specific Use
Ruling with proof: default loan for fungibles, exception for specified use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on borrowing fungible items and identify the exception when it remains a pure borrowing (ʿāriyah).

Core Idea
Borrowing gold, silver, and fungibles is a loan by default because benefit requires consumption; only a specified non-consumptive use preserves ʿāriyah.

This passage from al-Wiqayah clarifies a critical distinction in Hanafi contract law: when you borrow something that is typically consumed (like money or food), the law treats it as a loan, not a simple borrowing. The reason is that the benefit of such items is realized only through consumption, which requires ownership. However, if the lender specifies a use that does not consume the item—such as using coins to decorate a shop—the contract remains a pure borrowing (ʿāriyah). Understanding this principle prevents common confusion in everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is borrowing gold, silver, and fungibles (measured, weighed, counted). The default ruling is that it is a loan (qarḍ).
The matn states this explicitly at the beginning.
2
Locate the illa (effective cause)
The reason given: benefit from these items is only by consumption (istihlak) of their substance. Without consuming, there is generally no benefit.
The text says 'because one cannot benefit from these things except by consumption'.
3
Trace the legal reasoning (qiyas)
Since ʿāriyah is a transfer of benefits only, and here benefits require consumption, ownership must transfer. This leads to either gift or loan; loan is chosen because it is less harmful (requires return of the like).
This is an example of qiyas with istihsan: the illa is khafiyyah.
4
Identify the exception
If the lender specifies a non-consumptive use (e.g., calibrating scales, decorating a shop), the contract remains ʿāriyah; the borrower only has the specified benefit.
The exception is introduced by 'illa idha 'ayyan al-intifa''.
5
Apply the analogy
Compare to borrowing a vessel for adornment or a sword for wearing: the substance is not consumed, so ownership does not transfer. The same applies here.
The commentary explicitly makes this analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العارية تعريفها وأحكامها
وعارية النقدين، والمكيل، والموزون، والمعدود قرض
وعارية النقدين، والمكيل، والموزون، والمعدود (قرض؛ لأنَّهُ لا ينتفع بهذه الأشياء إلا بالاستهلاكِ إلا إذا عين الانتفاع كاستعارة الدراهم ليعير بها الميزان ، أو يُزين الدكان
قوله : لأنه لا ينتفع ... الخ ؛ تقريره : إن الإعارة تمليك المنافع عندنا، كما تقرر فيما تقدم، ولا ينتفع بالنقدين والمكيل والموزون غالباً، إلا باستهلاك أعيانها ، فيقتضي تمليك العين ضرورة ؛ وذلك بالهبة أو القرض.
والقرض أدناهما لكونه متيقناً به، أو لأنه أدناهما ضرراً ؛ لأنه أقل ضرراً على المعطي ؛ لأنه يوجب رد المثل، بخلاف الهبة ، وما هو أقل ضرراً فهو الثابت يقيناً فيثبت. 
قوله : إلا إذا عين ...الخ ؛ استثناء من قوله : قرض، يعني أن كون هذه الأشياء قرضاً إذا أطلق المعير الإعارة، أما إذا عين فيها الانتفاع كما إذا استعار المستعير الدراهم ليعير بها ميزاناً ، أو يزين بها دكاناً لم تكن قرضاً، ولا يكون للمستعير إلا المنفعة المعينة، كما إذا استعار آنية يتجمل بها أو سيفاً محلى يتقلدها، فالانتفاع هاهنا ليس إلا للتجمل أو التقلد، فكذا هذا. 
قوله : يعير الميزان ؛ قال في «المصباح» : عيّرت الدنانير تعييراً : امتحنتها لمعرفة أوزانها، وعايرت المكيال والميزان معايرة وعياراً : امتحنته بغيره لمعرفة صحته. انتهى. وقال في «الصراح»: معايرة رست کردن ترازو وبیمانه. انتهى. فالصواب أن يقال : ليعاير الميزان
قوله : أو يزين الدكّان بأن استعار دراهم كثيرة فوضعها على الدكان حتى يظن الناس غناه فيعاملوا معه. 
قوله : الدكان ؛ معرَّب، ويطلق على الحانوت، وعلى الدكة التي يقعـد عليها، وأما وزنُهُ قال السَّرَقُسطي : النونُ زائدة عند سيبويه، وكذلك قال الأَخفش، وهي مأخوذة من قولهم : أَكَمَةٌ دَكَّاءُ ؛ أي منبسطة، وهذا كما اشتق السلطان من : السلطنة.
Book of Borrowing: Its Definition and Rulings. And the borrowing of the two precious metals (gold and silver), and what is measured by volume, and what is weighed, and what is counted is a loan. And the borrowing of gold and silver, and measured, weighed, counted (is a loan) because one cannot benefit from these things except by consumption, unless the benefit is specified, such as borrowing dirhams to calibrate the scale with them, or to decorate the shop. His statement: 'Because one cannot benefit...' etc. Its explanation: Lending is a transfer of benefits according to us, as established previously, and one cannot benefit from gold and silver and measured and weighed items generally except by consuming their substances, which necessitates transfer of ownership of the substance necessarily; and that is by gift or loan. And the loan is the lesser of the two because it is certain, or because it is less harmful; because it is less harmful to the giver since it requires returning the equivalent, unlike the gift, and what is less harmful is established with certainty, so it is established. His statement: 'Unless he specifies...' etc. is an exception from his statement 'loan', meaning that these things are a loan when the lender gives unconditionally, but if he specifies the benefit in them, such as when the borrower borrows dirhams to calibrate a scale, or to decorate a shop, it is not a loan, and the borrower only has the specified benefit, just as if he borrows a vessel to adorn himself with or a decorated sword to wear, the benefit here is only for adornment or wearing, so likewise this. His statement: 'He calibrates the scale' – al-Misbah said: 'ayyarat al-dananeer ta'yīran: I tested them to know their weights, and 'āyart al-mikyāl wal-mīzān mu'āyaratan wa 'iyāran: I tested them with another to know their correctness. End. And al-Sirah said: 'Mu'āyara is adjusting the scale and measure. End. So the correct wording is 'to calibrate the scale' (ليعاير الميزان). His statement: 'Or decorates the shop' such as borrowing many dirhams and placing them on the shop so that people think he is wealthy and deal with him. His statement: 'al-dukkān' is Arabized, and it is used for the store, and for the bench that one sits on. As for its form, al-Saraqusṭī said: the noon is extra according to Sibawayh, and likewise al-Akhfash said, and it is derived from their saying 'akamah dakkā'' meaning flat, just as 'sultan' is derived from 'saltanah'.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on borrowing fungible items—specifically gold and silver (the two precious metals), and items measured by volume, weight, or count. The matn states that such borrowing is considered a loan (qarḍ), not a pure borrowing (ʿāriyah). The reason is that the typical benefit from these items comes only through consuming them (e.g., spending money, using up food). Since ʿāriyah in the Hanafi school is a transfer of benefits only, and here the benefit necessitates consumption of the substance, it effectively requires transfer of ownership. The two legal options are gift (hiba) or loan (qarḍ). The loan is chosen because it is less harmful to the lender (it requires return of the equivalent) and is more certain. However, there is an exception: if the lender specifies a particular non-consumptive use, such as borrowing dirhams just to calibrate a scale or to decorate a shop, then it remains a pure borrowing (ʿāriyah) and the borrower may only use them for that specified purpose. The commentary emphasizes this exception analogizes to borrowing a vessel for adornment or a sword for wearing. The passage also includes philological notes on the words 'yuʿayyir' (corrected to 'yuʿāyir') and 'al-dukkān'.

Key Terms
نقدين (naqdayn): The two precious metals: gold and silver, used as currency.
قرض (qarḍ): Loan of fungible items where the borrower must return the equivalent; transfer of ownership of the substance.
استهلاك (istihlāk): Consuming the item so that its substance is used up or transformed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, under what condition does borrowing dirhams remain a pure ʿāriyah and not become a loan?
A) When the borrower promises to return them within a day.
B) When the lender specifies a non-consumptive use, like decorating a shop.
C) When the dirhams are old and damaged.
D) When the borrower is wealthy and trustworthy.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the default classification of borrowing fungibles shifts from ʿāriyah to qarḍ when the benefit necessitates consumption; the intention of the parties (specifying a use) can override this default.

‘Āriyah of Fungibles is Qarḍ Unless Use Specified
Ruling and proof for classification of fungible ‘āriyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafī classification of ‘āriyah of fungible items as qarḍ, explain the rational proof, and identify the exception when a non-consumptive use is specified.

Core Idea
Lending fungibles for use is legally a loan (qarḍ) unless the lender specifies a non-consumptive use.

When you lend someone a bag of wheat or a handful of coins, do you waive liability if it gets ruined? The Hanafī school draws a sharp line: if the only way to benefit from the item is to consume it, the contract is automatically treated as a qarḍ (loan of fungibles). This lesson unpacks the reasoning from a key passage in ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āya and the narrow exception that preserves the ‘āriyah classification.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
Identify the baseline: ‘āriyah of fungible items (mithlī) is classified as qarḍ. Note the categories: gold/silver coins (naqdān), measured (makīl), weighed (mawzūn), and counted (ma‘dūd) items.
The ruling applies to all fungibles, not just money.
2
Understand the legal basis
The commentator explains the proof: ‘āriyah in the Hanafī school is tamlīk al-manāfi‘ (ownership of usufruct). Fungibles cannot be used without consuming them, so the contract necessarily transfers ownership of the substance. This can only happen through hibah (gift) or qarḍ (loan). Qarḍ is chosen because it is less harmful to the giver (it requires returning the like).
This is a rational argument (dalīl ‘aqlī) based on the definition of ‘āriyah and the nature of fungibles.
3
Identify the exception
If the lender explicitly specifies a non-consumptive use, the contract remains a true ‘āriyah. Examples include borrowing dirhams to calibrate a scale (mu‘āyara) or to decorate a shop (tazyīn al-dukkān).
In such cases, the borrower only has the right to the specified benefit; if the items are destroyed without negligence, they are not guaranteed.
4
Apply the practical consequence
The key difference is liability: if treated as qarḍ, the borrower guarantees the item (must return the like or its value). If treated as ‘āriyah (with specified non-consumptive use), the item is an amānah (trust) and the guarantor is only liable for negligence.
This affects disputes about loss or damage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the ‘āriyah of gold and silver coins, measured [items], weighed [items], and counted [items] is a qarḍ (loan); because one cannot benefit from these things except by consumption, except if he specifies the benefit, like borrowing dirhams to adjust the scale with them, or to decorate the shop. [1] His statement: 'because one cannot benefit... etc.'; its explanation: Indeed ‘āriyah is ownership of usufructs in our [Hanafi] school, as established previously, and one generally cannot benefit from gold/silver coins, measured, weighed [items] except by consuming their substances, so it necessitates ownership of the substance necessarily; and that is by hibah (gift) or qarḍ. And qarḍ is the lesser of the two because it is more certain, or because it is less harmful; for it is less harmful on the giver because it obligates returning the like, unlike hibah, and what is less harmful is established with certainty, so it is established. [2] His statement: 'except if he specifies... etc.'; an exception from his statement 'qarḍ', meaning that these things are qarḍ when the lender gives an absolute ‘āriyah, but if he specifies the benefit, such as when the borrower borrows dirhams to adjust the scale with them, or to decorate a shop, it is not qarḍ, and the borrower only has the specified benefit, just as if he borrows a vessel to adorn himself with it or a decorated sword to wear, the benefit here is only for adornment or wearing, so similarly this. [3] His statement: 'to adjust the scale'; he said in al-Misbah: 'I adjusted the dinars ta‘yīran: I tested them to know their weights, and I adjusted the measure and scale mu‘āyara and ‘iyār: I tested them with something else to know their correctness.' End. And he said in al-Sirāh: 'Mu‘āyara is to adjust the scale and measure.' End. So the correct expression is 'to adjust the scale'. [4] His statement: 'or to decorate the shop' – such as borrowing many dirhams and placing them on the shop so that people think he is wealthy and deal with him. [PAGE_444] [2] Zubdat al-Nihāya li-‘Umdat al-Ri‘āya [2]-[0] And it is valid to lend land for building and planting, and he has the right to revoke it and oblige [the borrower] to remove them. Commentary
This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āya is a commentary (sharḥ) on the matn of al-Wiqāya dealing with ‘āriyah (lending for use). It states the general rule: lending fungible items—gold, silver, measured, weighed, counted goods—is treated as a qarḍ (loan), not an ‘āriyah. The reason is that benefit from such items normally requires consuming them (e.g., spending gold, eating grain). Since ‘āriyah in the Hanafi school is ownership of usufruct only, and here the usufruct cannot be realized without destroying the substance, the contract necessarily becomes a transfer of ownership of the substance itself. That can be either hibah (gift) or qarḍ. The commentator explains that qarḍ is the more likely because it is less harmful (it requires returning the like) and more certain. However, there is an exception: if the borrower specifies a non-consumptive use—like using dirhams to calibrate a scale or to decorate a shop for appearance—then it remains an ‘āriyah and the borrower is only entitled to that specific benefit. The passage then clarifies the meaning of ‘calibrating the scale’ and ‘decorating the shop’, noting that the latter is a trick to appear wealthy. It also gives the lexicology of ‘dukkān’ (shop/bench). Finally, it states the practical consequence: if the items are treated as qarḍ and are destroyed in the borrower’s hands before use, they are guaranteed (must be replaced); while if they were truly ‘āriyah (amanah), they would not be guaranteed unless negligence occurred.

Key Terms
عارية (‘āriyah): Lending an object for use without charge, where the borrower may use the object but the ownership remains with the lender.
قرض (qarḍ): A loan of fungible items which are consumed and the borrower repays the like in quantity and kind.
استهلاك (istihlāk): Consumption of the very substance of the item (consumptive use).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man borrows a bag of wheat from his neighbor for planting seeds, which will consume the wheat. According to the Hanafī school, this contract is:
A) ‘Āriyah (lending for use)
B) Qarḍ (loan) because wheat is a measurable item and will be consumed
C) Hibah (gift) because the substance is transferred
D) I‘ārah with a specified benefit

Key Takeaway
In Hanafī fiqh, lending fungible items is presumed to be qarḍ (a binding loan of the substance) unless a non-consumptive use is explicitly stipulated.

Lending Land for Building or Planting and Right to Revoke
Ruling that land can be lent for building/planting and the owner can revoke

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lending land for building/planting, explain the lender's right to revoke, and identify the conditions for compensation upon revocation.

Core Idea
Lending land for building/planting is valid but non-binding; the lender can revoke anytime and demand removal, but must compensate if removal harms the land.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on lending land for constructing buildings or planting trees. While such a loan is permissible, it remains a non-binding contract, meaning the lender can reclaim the land at will. However, the law balances the lender's property rights with the borrower's investment, imposing obligations for removal or compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn states that lending land for building or planting is valid (صحيح). This means the contract is effective and legally recognized.
Focus on the word 'صح' (valid) – it immediately establishes permissibility.
2
Understand the Reasoning
The commentator explains that building and planting are 'known benefits' that can be owned through a lease (ijārah). Since ‘āriyah is ownership of usufruct without compensation, granting such benefits is even more appropriate (a fortiori argument).
This is a dalīl ‘aqlī (rational proof) based on analogy from ijārah.
3
Right to Revoke
The lender has the right to revoke the ‘āriyah at any time, even after the borrower has built or planted. This is because the contract is non-binding (ghayr lāzim), like all ‘āriyah contracts in the Hanafī school.
Contrast with binding contracts (lāzim) like sale or lease.
4
Consequences of Revocation
Upon revocation, the lender can oblige the borrower to remove the structures or plants (قلع) to restore the land. However, if removal would damage the land, the lender may instead take the structures/plants at their market value, compensating the borrower.
The exception for harm prevents waste (talaf) of the borrower's property.

Source Text

[2] His statement: 'And it is valid... etc.'; because each of building and planting is a known benefit that can be owned by lease (ijārah), so it is owned by ‘āriyah, rather even more so because it is a donation. Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on ‘āriyah, now addressing the specific case of lending land for building or planting. The matn states that such an ‘āriyah is valid, and the lender has the right to revoke at any time and force the borrower to remove the structures or plants. The commentator explains the reasoning: building and planting are known benefits that can be leased, so they can also be given as a gratuitous ‘āriyah. The contract is non-binding (ghayr lāzim), so the lender can revoke it whenever he wishes. Upon revocation, the lender can oblige removal to restore the land to its original state. However, if removal would damage the land, the lender may instead compensate the borrower by taking the structures/plants at their value. If removal causes no harm, the borrower must remove them; the lender cannot force the borrower to leave them. The text also clarifies that if the items (referring back to the earlier lesson on fungibles) had been truly ‘āriyah (trust), they would not be guaranteed if destroyed, but because they are qarḍ they are guaranteed.

Key Terms
غرس (ghars): Planting a tree or plant; the act of planting.
قلع (qal‘): Removing (a structure or plant) from the land.
غير لازم (ghayr lāzim): Non-binding contract; each party may rescind unilaterally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The lender cannot revoke because the borrower has invested effort
B) The lender may revoke and must allow the trees to remain until harvest
C) The lender may revoke and oblige removal; if removal damages the land, he may take the trees at value
D) The contract automatically becomes a lease

Key Takeaway
Lending land for building or planting is valid but revocable; upon revocation, the lender may demand removal, but if removal harms the land, he may take the improvements at their value.

Ruling on Lending Land for Building and Planting and Revocation
Commentary on ruling of lending land and revocation with compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revoking a loan of land for building/planting, distinguish between absolute and time-bound loans regarding compensation, and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
Lender may revoke a gratuitous land loan at will; compensation for structures hinges on whether the loan was absolute or time-bound.

Lending land for someone to build a house or plant trees is a common act of kindness. But what happens when the lender needs the land back? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer: the lender can revoke the loan at any time, but compensation for the borrower's structures depends on whether the loan had a fixed term. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its rationale, and a key disagreement with Imam Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on Validity
Establish that lending land for building and planting is a valid contract ('ariyah). Note that it is permissible (mubah) and creates a trust at the borrower's hand unless specified as a loan (qard).
The benefit here is a known benefit that can be hired, so it can be lent.
2
Understand the Right to Revoke and Demand Removal
The lender may revoke at any time because 'ariyah is non-binding (ghayr lazim). Upon revocation, the lender can require removal of structures and trees from the land.
If removal harms the land, the lender may instead take the structures for their value.
3
Distinguish Absolute vs Time-Bound for Compensation
For an absolute loan (mutlaq), the lender does not compensate the borrower for any decrease in value due to removal. For a time-bound loan (muwaqqat), if the lender revokes before the term ends, compensation is owed for the loss.
The rationale: in absolute loan, the borrower takes the risk; in time-bound, the lender's promise creates reliance.
4
Analyze the Maliki Dispute
Imam Malik holds that in absolute loans, the lender must leave the structures and compensate the borrower because the borrower is deceived (maghrur). The Hanafi response: the borrower deceived himself in an absolute loan; only in time-bound is the lender a deceiver.
Practical difference: under Maliki, lenders may be less willing to lend land freely.
5
Apply the Tarjih
The Hanafi position (no compensation in absolute loan, compensation in time-bound if revoked early) is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the school, as affirmed by al-Marghinani.
Always verify whether the loan was absolute or time-bound before issuing a fatwa.

Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn about lending land for building and planting. The base ruling states that it is valid to lend land for such purposes, and the lender has the right to revoke the loan and demand removal. The commentary clarifies that unlike a normal loan (ariyah) which is not guaranteed, this is treated as a qard (loan of fungible) so if it perishes before use, the borrower guarantees it. The right to revoke exists because the contract of ariyah is not binding. The lender can require removal of structures and trees, but if removal damages the land, he may take them for their value or negotiate. Two scenarios regarding compensation for damage: if the loan was absolute (no fixed term), the lender does not compensate for decrease in value from removal; if the loan was time-bound and revoked early, he must compensate. The passage then records a dispute with Imam Malik, who holds that in such cases the lender must compensate and leave the structures. The Hanafi response is that the lender is not a deceiver in an absolute loan, so no compensation; but in a time-bound loan, the lender is a deceiver and must compensate.

Key Terms
عارية ('āriyah): A contract of gratuitous loan (not of money) for use of an asset; in this context, land for building/planting.
مطلقة (muṭlaqah): Absolute loan without a fixed term or time limit.
موقتة (muwaqqatah): Loan with a specified time period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always, whenever land is lent for building.
B) Only if the loan was time-bound and revoked before the term ends.
C) Only if the loan was absolute.
D) Never; the borrower bears all loss.

Key Takeaway
The revocability of 'ariyah for land requires careful attention to whether the loan is absolute or time-bound, affecting compensation for structures.

Lending Land for Cultivation: Eviction and Liability
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for revoking a land loan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revoking a loan of land for cultivation, explain the difference between time-limited and absolute loans regarding liability, and evaluate Zufar's dissenting view.

Core Idea
Time-limited land loan creates liability for deception; absolute loan does not — the borrower assumes the risk.

Lending land for cultivation is a common transaction that raises questions about the lender's right to revoke and the borrower's protections. The Hanafi school balances both parties' interests using istihsan to prevent harm and the concept of ghurur to assign liability. This passage from al-Hidayah explains the key rulings and the dispute within the Hanafi school, particularly Zufar's objection to lender liability when a time is set.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on revocation and eviction
The passage states that revoking before the appointed time is disliked (makruh) and that the borrower cannot be evicted until harvest, whether a time was specified or not. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid harm.
Istihsan overrides strict qiyas here to protect the borrower's investment.
2
Distinguish liability based on time specification
If the lender specified a time, he is liable for deception (ghurur) because the borrower relied on that promise. If the loan was absolute (no time), the lender is not liable because the borrower deceived himself by assuming the land would remain available.
The key is the lender's action creating reliance.
3
Analyze the options for improvements
When land is revoked after planting trees or building, the borrower can either demand compensation for the value of his improvements (which then become the lender's) or remove them at his own cost. If removal harms the land, the lender has the choice to pay compensation for the deficiency or the full value.
This balances the rights of lender (original owner) and borrower (improver).
4
Examine Zufar's objection and the Hanafi response
Zufar argues that specifying a time is invalid in loans, so time-limited and absolute loans are identical; thus the lender should not be liable. The majority respond that the stipulation, though not binding as a contract term, creates a moral obligation and induces reliance, making the lender liable for deception.
The majority view is preferred in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is disliked to withdraw before [the appointed time], and if he lends for cultivation, he is not evicted until the harvest, whether [a time] was specified or not. And he is only liable for the deception. In the case of absolute [lending], he [the lender] did not deceive him; rather the borrower deceived himself, relying on the absolute [lending]. And it is disliked to withdraw before [the appointed time], meaning before the time, because it is a breach of promise. (And if he lends for cultivation, he is not evicted until the harvest, whether a time [was specified] or not) And he said in al-Hidayah: And al-Hakim al-Shahid mentioned for him: That the owner of the land is liable to the borrower for the value of his planting and construction, and it becomes his [the lender's], unless the borrower wishes to remove them, and he [the lender] is not liable for their value, so he [the borrower] may do that because it is his property. End. And its summary: That the borrower in this situation has the option: if he wishes, he may make the landowner liable for the value of his planting and construction standing undamaged, and leave it [to the lender], and he owns it by paying the compensation, and if he wishes, he takes his planting and construction, and nothing is owed by the landowner. And it is said: if [the removal] causes harm, the lender is given the choice between compensating for the deficit and compensating the value. And he said in al-Hidayah: They said: If in uprooting there is harm to the land, then the option belongs to the owner of the land, because he is the owner of the original [property] and the borrower is a follower, and the original is given priority. End. 1] His statement: He is only liable... etc.; and Zufar said: He is not liable, because specifying a time and absolute are equal in 'ariyah, due to the invalidity of stipulating terms in loans. Our proof: What the commentator stated: He is only liable for deception. Its summary: That the lender by specifying a time has deceived the borrower, because he explicitly allowed the land to remain in his hand and approved his building or planting for the period he stated. And the deceived party can repel harm from himself by recourse against the deceiver, unlike the case of absolute [lending], because the lender did not deceive him; rather the borrower deceived himself, relying on the absolute, so the harm came from himself, not from the lender. (2) His statement: he is not evicted; meaning the borrowed [land] is not taken away, by istihsan, because harming a believer is prohibited, so it is left in his hand by way of hire at the equivalent rent, until the crop is harvested, so that the benefit of his land is not lost for free. (1) al-Hidayah (3:222). (2) al-Hidayah (3:222). [PAGE_447] Book of 'Ariyah: its definition and rulings 447 Because cultivation has a known end, so in leaving it there is regard for both rights, unlike planting because it has no known end. [1] His statement: Because for cultivation...; meaning because cultivation has a known end, so leaving the land for this period is regard for both rights, as in hire, if the period ends and the crop is not yet ready, unlike planting and building because they have no known limit, so the harm is from both sides as harm of nullifying rights, so the side of the original is favored.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and a ruling statement regarding the loan of land for cultivation. The author first states the ruling: it is disliked (makruh) for the lender to revoke the loan before the agreed time, because it involves breaking a promise. If land is lent for cultivation, the borrower cannot be evicted until the crop is harvested, whether a specific time was stipulated or not. The key principle is istihsan (juristic preference) based on avoiding harm to the borrower. The lender is only liable for damages when he has deceived the borrower by setting a time limit; if the loan was absolute (no time limit), the borrower takes the risk. The text then quotes al-Hidayah, explaining that if the lender revokes after the borrower has planted trees or built structures, the borrower has a choice: either to demand compensation for the value of his improvements and leave them, or to remove them at his own expense. However, if removal would harm the land, the lender has the option to either pay compensation for the deficiency or the full value. Zufar al-Hadhdha'i disagrees with the liability ruling, arguing that time-limited and absolute loans are alike because stipulating terms is invalid in loans. The Hanafi response is that the lender is liable because he deceived the borrower by promising the land for a specific period; the borrower relied on that promise. The passage concludes by explaining the reason for not evicting the borrower: cultivation has a known end (harvest time), so leaving the land until then respects both parties' rights, whereas planting and building have no known end, so the original owner's right is given priority.

Key Terms
العارية (al-‘āriyah): A loan of a non-fungible item (e.g., land) for use without charge.
الغرور (al-ghurūr): Deception or inducing someone to act based on a false impression; here, the lender's promise of a time limit.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; here, preferring to leave the borrower in possession until harvest to avoid harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority, why is the lender liable for damages if he revokes a land loan that had a specified time?
A) Because the contract is binding and revocation breaches it.
B) Because he deceived the borrower by promising the land for that period.
C) Because the borrower has no other place to farm.
D) Because the lender demanded rent after promising free use.

Key Takeaway
In land loans for cultivation, the borrower cannot be evicted until harvest, and the lender is liable only if he deceived the borrower by specifying a time.

Wording in Land Loan Contracts: The Preference for 'It'am' over 'I'arah'
Specifies correct wording for written contracts of land loan for cultivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on using 'it'am' in written contracts for land lent for farming and apply the underlying principle to other similar cases.

Core Idea
For land lent for farming, write 'at'amta' (you fed me), not 'a'arta' (you lent me), to avoid ambiguity.

When land is lent for cultivation, the borrower often writes a contract. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the wording should be precise to reflect the purpose. This lesson examines the linguistic reasoning behind preferring the term 'it'am' (feeding) over 'i'arah' (lending), and why a house does not require the same distinction.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the exact wording recommended by Abu Hanifa for a written contract when land is lent for cultivation.
The passage says: 'يكتب لفظ الإطعام' (he writes the expression it'am).
2
Understand the reasoning
Why is 'it'am' better than 'i'arah'? Because 'it'am' is specific to cultivation, while 'i'arah' is general and could also mean building or other uses.
The commentator explains: 'لفظ الإطعام أدل على الزراعة من الإعارة'.
3
Contrast with a house
Why does the same rule not apply to a house? Because a house is only lent for dwelling, so 'i'arah' alone suffices to convey the purpose.
The passage says: 'بخلاف الدار؛ لأنها لا تعار إلا للسكنى'.
4
Extract the principle
What general contract principle is demonstrated? Use terminology that is specific to the intended use to prevent disputes.
This reflects the broader Hanafi concern for avoiding ambiguity in contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ويكتب المعـار قد أطعمتني أرضك لا أعرتني إذا أعيرت للزراعة)، إذا أعيرت الأرض للزراعة، فأراد المستعير أن يكتب كتابا فعند أبي حنيفة يُكتبُ لفظ الإطعام؛ لأنه أدل على الزراعة، فإنّ إعارة الأرض، قد يكون للبناء والغرس  (۳) قوله : لأنه أدلّ ... الخ ; تقريره : إنَّ لفظ الإطعام أدلّ على الزراعة من الإعارة ; لكونه مختصاً بالزراعة، والإعارة قد تكون للزراعة وقد تكون للبناء ونحوه ، كنصب الخيمة، فتكونُ الكتابة باللفظ المخصوص بالمراد أولى، بخلاف الدار ; لأنها لا تعارُ إلا للسكنى والغرض يصير معلوماً بقوله : أعرتني.
(And the borrower writes: 'You have fed me your land' not 'You have lent me [it]' when [the land] is lent for cultivation). When the land is lent for cultivation, and the borrower wants to write a document, according to Abu Hanifa the expression 'it'am' (feeding) is written because it is more indicative of cultivation. Indeed, lending (i'arah) of land could be for building and planting. [PAGE_447] (3) His statement: Because it is more indicative...; its restatement: The word 'it'am' is more indicative of cultivation than 'i'arah' because it is specific to cultivation, while 'i'arah' could be for cultivation or building and the like, such as pitching a tent. So writing with the expression specific to the intended purpose is better. Unlike a house, because it is only lent for dwelling, and the purpose becomes known by saying 'you lent me'.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the proper terminology to use in written contracts when land is lent for farming. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the borrower should write 'You have fed me (at'amta) your land' rather than 'You have lent me (a'arta) your land'. The reason is that the word 'it'am' (feeding/giving for cultivation) is more specific to farming, while 'i'arah' (lending) is general and could also apply to building, planting trees, or other uses. By using 'it'am', the contract clearly expresses the purpose of cultivation. This is recommended to avoid ambiguity. The commentator notes that this specificity is not needed for a house, since a house is only lent for dwelling, so saying 'a'arta' (you lent) suffices to convey the purpose.

Key Terms
الإطعام (al-iṭ‘ām): Literally 'feeding'; here used to mean giving land for cultivation, as a specific term for lending land for farming.

Key Takeaway

Wording in Land Loan Documents: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Dispute on whether to use 'you fed me' or 'you lent me' in a land loan document

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the wording of a land loan document and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
In land loan documents, Abu Hanifa prefers the specific term 'iṭʿām' over the generic 'iʿārah' to avoid ambiguity, but the Sahibayn prioritize the established contract term.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the precise wording to use when drafting a document for lending land for agriculture. While the two disciples (Sahibayn) prefer the standard term for loan (i'arah), Abu Hanifa argues for a term specific to cultivation (it'am) because it avoids doubt and protects both parties' rights. The commentary explains the reasoning, particularly regarding the known end of cultivation versus indefinite planting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the act is lending land for agriculture, and the dispute is about the wording in the document: 'أطعمتني' (you fed me) vs. 'أعرتني' (you lent me).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the borrower should write 'أطعمتني' because this term is specific to cultivation. His proof: cultivation has a known end (harvest time), so the land is returned after that period, respecting both parties' rights. In contrast, planting trees (ghars) has no known end, causing harm.
The commentary also mentions that 'iṭʿām' is more indicative of cultivation than 'iʿārah'.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the borrower should write 'أعرتني' because it is the established term for the contract of loan (i'arah). They analogize to lending a house, where only 'i'arah' is written, not 'suknah' (dwelling).
4
Analyze the key difference
The dispute is between specificity (Abu Hanifa) and general established usage (Sahibayn). Abu Hanifa emphasizes the known end of agriculture, while the Sahibayn prioritize consistency with the contract term.
The text does not resolve the dispute; both are presented as valid.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the actual wording in the loan document. Different wording may affect interpretation if a dispute arises about the purpose of the loan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكتب المعار قد أطعمتني أرضك لا أعرتني إذا أعيرت للزراعة لان للزرع نهاية معلومة ، ففي التركِ مراعاة الحقين بخلاف الغرس إذ ليس لهم نهاية معلومة.
[۱] قوله : لأن للزرع ; يعني لأن للزرع نهاية معلومة ، فترك الأرض إلى هذه المدّة مراعاة للحقين، كما في الإجارة ، إذا انقضت المدَّة والزرعُ لم يدرك، بخلافِ الغرس والبناء ; لأنه ليس له حد معلوم، فيكون الضرر من الجانبين ضرر إبطال حق، فيترجح جانب الأصل.

وعندهما يكتب لفظة الإعارة.

And the borrower should write: 'You have fed me your land' not 'You have lent me' when the land is lent for cultivation, because for cultivation there is a known end, so leaving the land for this period observes both rights, unlike planting (gharas) which has no known end.
[1] His statement: 'because for cultivation' means because cultivation has a known end, so leaving the land for this period observes both rights, as in leasing, when the term expires and the crop has not matured, unlike planting and building because they have no fixed limit, so the harm to both sides is harm of invalidating a right, thus the side of the original owner is preferred.
[3] His statement: 'because it is more indicative' etc.; its exposition: the word 'feeding' (iṭʿām) is more indicative of cultivation than 'lending' (iʿārah) because it is specific to cultivation, whereas lending can be for cultivation or for building etc., like pitching a tent, so writing with the term specific to the intended purpose is better, unlike a house because it is only lent for dwelling and the purpose becomes known by saying 'you lent me'.
And according to them (the two disciples), he writes the word 'lending' (iʿārah).
[1] His statement: 'and according to them he writes the word lending' because the word lending is established for the contract of lending, and writing with the established term is better as in lending a house, where one does not write 'dwelling'.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the wording of a document when land is lent for agriculture. Abu Hanifa holds that the borrower should write 'you have fed me your land' (أطعمتني) rather than 'you have lent me' (أعرتني) because the term 'feeding' is specific to cultivation and leaves no ambiguity. The proof is that cultivation has a known time limit, so the land will be returned after harvest, unlike planting trees or building, which have indefinite ends and thus cause harm. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the borrower should write 'you have lent me' (أعرتني) because that is the term for the contract itself, and writing with the official contract term is preferable, analogous to lending a house where only 'lending' is written without specifying 'dwelling'. The commentary from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains the reasoning behind each position.

Key Terms
إطعام (iṭʿām): Literally 'feeding'; used metaphorically for lending land for agriculture.
إعارة (iʿārah): Loan contract; the generic term for lending an item for use.
غرس (ghars): Planting (trees) – contrasted with زرع (crops) because trees have no fixed harvest time.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, there is an internal dispute on whether to use the specific term 'iṭʿām' or the general term 'iʿārah' when drafting a land loan document, with Abu Hanifa favoring specificity and the Sahibayn favoring the established contract term.

Documenting the Loan Contract with the Correct Term
Ruling on documentation terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the term to be used in documenting a contract of 'ariyah and explain the reasoning based on the principle of using the designated term.

Core Idea
Use the specific legal term 'i'arah' for the loan contract – it is more appropriate.

When drafting a contract for loan of use ('ariyah), which word should you write? A simple but important choice: writing the designated legal term 'i'arah' is recommended over alternatives like 'sakinah'. This lesson examines the ruling from the Hanafi school and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions of Abu Hanifa), one writes the word 'i'arah' in the contract for 'ariyah. Identify who holds this view and the level of obligation (recommended, not obligatory).
The phrase 'عندهما' attributes this to the Sahibayn.
2
Understand the Reasoning
The reason given is that the word 'i'arah' is the designated term (موضوع) for this contract. Writing with the designated term is 'more appropriate' (أولى). Recognize that this is based on linguistic precision and legal convention.
The term 'أولى' indicates preference, not obligation.
3
Apply the Analogy
The passage draws an analogy with the loan of a house: one writes 'i'arah', not 'sakinah' (dwelling). Understand that 'sakinah' is a description, not the contractual term.
This reinforces that the designated term should be used.
4
Distinguish from Other Views
Note that the ruling is attributed specifically to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Consider whether Abu Hanifa himself held a different view; the text does not specify, but the expression 'عندهما' often implies a contrast with the Imam's position.
This is a point of intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on documentation.
5
Extract the Fiqh Principle
The broader principle is that in legal documents, one should use the technical terms designated by the Shari'ah for each contract, as they carry precise legal meanings.
This principle ensures clarity and avoids ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : وعندهما يكتب لفظ الإعارة ؛ فإن لفظ الإعارة موضوع لعقد الإعارة، والكتابة بالموضوع أولى كما في إعارة الدار، حيث لا يكتب السكني.
[1] His saying: 'And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), one writes the word i'arah.' Indeed, the word i'arah is designated for the contract of i'arah, and writing with the designated term is more appropriate, just as in the loan of a house, where one does not write sakinah (right of dwelling).
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Al-Hidayah. It states that according to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the two companions of Abu Hanifa), the contract document for 'ariyah (loan of use) should explicitly write the word 'i'arah (loan) rather than other terms like 'sakinah' (dwelling). The reasoning is that the word 'i'arah is the specific legal term for this contract, and using the designated term is more appropriate. An analogy is drawn with the loan of a house, where one writes 'i'arah and not 'sakinah'. This is a straightforward ruling on documentary drafting.

Key Terms
إعارة (i'arah): The act of loaning a benefit; the specific term used for the contract of 'ariyah.
عارية (ariyah): Loan of usufruct without compensation; a contract granting the use of an object.
سكنى (sakinah): Right of dwelling; a term used for the usufruct of a house, but not the proper term for a loan contract.

Linguistic and Legal Definition of Hiba
Linguistic analysis and definition of the chapter on gifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hiba linguistically and legally according to the Hanafi school and distinguish it from ariyah.

Core Idea
Hiba transfers ownership of the substance; ariyah only transfers usufruct — one is compound, the other simple.

Scholars begin the chapter on gifts (hiba) by clarifying its relationship to the previous chapter on loans (ariyah). This sets the stage for a precise legal definition. Understanding the linguistic roots of 'hiba' helps us appreciate the nuanced legal transfer it entails. In this lesson, we will unpack the definition from classical lexicons and the Hidayah commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the connection with the previous chapter
The commentator explains that ariyah (loan) involves ownership of usufruct without compensation, while hiba involves ownership of the substance without compensation. Ariyah is like a simple (mufrad), hiba like a compound (murakkab) because it includes both substance and benefit.
This structural analogy (simple vs. compound) is a common analytical tool in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Extract the linguistic definition
According to 'al-Nihayah', hiba in language is 'an expression for delivering something to another in a way that benefits him.' This includes both property and non-property. The verb 'wahab' is used in the Quran with the preposition 'li'.
The definition focuses on the act of giving with benefit, not necessarily ownership transfer in language.
3
Note the grammatical dispute
Classical linguists like Ibn al-Qutiyyah say 'wahab' is transitive only with 'li', not directly to two objects. Jurists use 'wahabtuka malan' by analogy with 'a'ta'. This debate shows how jurists may extend linguistic usage for legal precision.
The Hanafi school follows the juristic usage, accepting the ditransitive construction.
4
Synthesize the legal definition
From the linguistic basis and the context of the chapter, the legal (shar‘i) definition of hiba is: a gratuitous transfer of ownership of a specific substance ('ayn) from one person to another, without compensation. This excludes loans of usufruct (ariyah) and sales.
This definition is foundational for understanding the rules of gifts in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كتاب الهبة ؛ وجه المناسبة بينها وبين ما قبلها ظاهر؛ لأنَّ ما قبلها تمليك المنفعة بلا عوض، وهي تمليك العين بلا عوض؛ ولأن العارية كالمفرد، والهبة كالمركب؛ لأن فيها تمليك العين مع المنفعة، والمفرد مقدم على المركب طبعاً، والهبة بالكسر : بخشيدن. كذا في «الصراح». يقال : وهبت لزيد ما لا أهبه له هبة أعطيته بلا عوض، يتعدى إلى المفعول باللام في التنزيل : يَهَبُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ إِنَاثًا وَيَهَبُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ الذُّكُورَ، ووَهَباً - بفتح الهاء وسكونها - وموهباً وموهبة بكسرهما. قال ابن القوطية والسِّرَقُسطي والمُطَرِّزي وجماعة : ولا يتعدى إلى الأول بنفسه، ولا يقال : وهبتك مالاً، والفقهاء يقولونه، وقد يُجعل له وجه، وهو أن تضمن وهب معنى أعطى، فيتعدّى بنفسه إلى مفعولين. ومن كلامهم : وهبني الله له فلا؛ أي جعلني، لكن لم يسمع في كلام فصيح، وزید موهوب له، والمال موهوب، وأنّهبتُ الهبة قبلتها، واستوهبتها سألتها، وتواهبوا : وهب بعضهم لبعض. كذا في «المصباح»). وقال في «النهاية»: إنها في اللغة: عبارة عن إيصال الشيء إلى الغير بما ينفعه، قال الله : فَهَبْ لِي مِن لَّدُنكَ وَلِيًّا . انتهى. أي سواء كان مالاً أو غيره، وقال الفهستَانِيُّ : ويتعدّى إما باللام نحو : وهبته له، وحكى أبو عمرو : وهبتك، كما في «القاموس». وقالوا : بحذف اللام منه، وأمّا بمن نحو : وهبتُهُ منك على ما جاء به من أحاديث كثيرة في «الصحيح». كما في دقائق النَّوَوِي»، فظنَّ من المُطَرِّزي أنه خطأ...
His saying: The chapter of Hiba; the connection between it and what precedes is apparent, because what precedes is ownership of usufruct without compensation, and this is ownership of the substance without compensation. And because the ariyah is like a simple, and hiba is like a compound, because in it there is ownership of the substance along with the usufruct, and the simple is naturally prior to the compound. And hiba (with kasrah) means 'bakhshidan' (giving) in Persian; so in 'al-Sirah'. It is said: 'Wahabtu li Zayd malan — I gave to Zayd property — a hiba: I gave it without compensation.' It is transitive to the object with lam in the Quran: 'He grants to whom He wills females and grants to whom He wills males.' And wahab (with fatha on ha and sukun) and mawhab and mawhibah (with kasrah). Ibn al-Qutiyyah, al-Siraqusti, al-Mutarrizi and others said: It is not transitive to the first object by itself, and it is not said 'Wahabtuka malan' (I gave you property), but the jurists say it, and it can be justified by implying that wahab has the meaning of a'ta (gave), thus it becomes transitive to two objects. And from their speech: 'Wahabani Allah lahu fala' — meaning 'made me', but it is not heard in eloquent speech. Zayd is mawhub lahu, and the property is mawhub. Anhabtu al-hibah: I accepted it; istawhabtuhu: I asked for it; tawahabu: they gave to each other. So in 'al-Misbah'. And in 'al-Nihayah': It is in language: an expression for delivering something to another in a way that benefits him. Allah said: 'So grant me from Yourself a guardian.' End. That is, whether it is property or otherwise. And al-Fahistani said: It is transitive either with lam, as 'wahabtuhu lahu', and Abu Amr narrated 'wahabtuka' as in al-Qamus. And they said: with dropping of lam from it, and also with min, as 'wahabtuhu minka' based on many hadiths in al-Sahih, as in Daqa'iq al-Nawawi, so al-Mutarrizi thought it was an error...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Hiba (gift) with a linguistic and legal analysis. The commentator first explains the connection between the previous chapter (on ariyah, loan of usufruct) and this one: the previous deals with ownership of benefit (manfa'ah) without compensation, while hiba is ownership of the substance (ayn) without compensation. The commentator then notes that ariyah is like a simple entity (mufrad) because it involves only benefit, whereas hiba is like a compound (murakkab) because it includes both substance and benefit. The discussion then moves to the linguistic aspects of the word 'hiba'. The commentator defines it as giving without compensation, referencing Quranic usage. There is a grammatical discussion about whether the verb 'wahab' is transitive with lam (to) or can be ditransitive without lam. Some linguists object to 'wahabtuka malan', but jurists use it, possibly by analogy with 'a'ta' (give). The passage also provides derived forms and other usages from classical Arabic.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): In language: delivering something to another in a way that benefits him; in law: transfer of ownership of a substance without compensation.
تمليك العين (tamlik al-'ayn): Transfer of ownership of the physical object itself.
تمليك المنفعة (tamlik al-manfa'ah): Transfer of ownership of the usufruct/benefit of an object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the linguistic definition in 'al-Nihayah', what is hiba?
A) Ownership of usufruct without compensation
B) Ownership of the substance with compensation
C) Delivering something to another in a way that benefits him
D) A contract of exchange

Key Takeaway
Hiba is the transfer of ownership of a substance without compensation, distinct from ariyah which only transfers usufruct.

Definition and Proofs of Hiba in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining hiba and establishing its legitimacy from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hiba, identify its proofs from Quran and Sunnah, and distinguish it from other contracts using the definitional components.

Core Idea
Hiba is immediate ownership transfer without compensation; its essence distinguishes it from sale, lease, and charity.

In this lesson, we examine the classical Hanafi definition of hiba (gift) and the evidence establishing its legitimacy. The text not only defines the contract but also delineates it from other transactions by dissecting each element of the definition. Understanding this definition is crucial for correctly applying the conditions and rulings that follow.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct the definition
Break down the matn's definition 'tamlik 'ayn bi-la 'iwad' into its three components: tamlik (transfer of ownership), 'ayn (tangible asset), and bi-la 'iwad (without compensation). Understand what each term excludes.
This definition is the foundation for distinguishing hiba from deposits, leases, and sales.
2
Identify the proofs of legitimacy
Locate the three proofs cited: Quran (4:4), hadith from Bukhari (accepting gifts), and hadith from Muwatta (exchange gifts to increase love). Also note the claim of ijma'.
The proofs establish that hiba is a recommended act with basis in revelation and communal consensus.
3
List the conditions and pillars
Extract the conditions for the giver (sanity, puberty, ownership) and the gifted item (possessed, undivided, distinct, free). Note the two pillars: offer and acceptance.
Conditions must be present at the time of the contract; their absence renders the gift invalid.
4
Apply the definition to exclude other contracts
Work through the commentator's method: 'tamlik' excludes deposits; 'ayn' excludes leases and loans; 'bila 'iwad' excludes sales. Practice with each example.
This shows how the definition functions as a precise legal instrument.
5
Resolve the definitional objection
Consider the objection that the definition also covers bequest (wasiyyah) and charity (sadaqah). The commentator offers two solutions: interpreting 'tamlik' as immediate, or restricting the definition to absolute hiba.
This step sharpens your ability to handle counterarguments in fiqh reasoning.

وهي عقد مشروع، ودليل مشروعيتها : قوله : فَإِن طِبْنَ لَكُمْ عَن شَيْءٍ مِنْهُ نَفْسًا فَكُلُوهُ ... وما رواه البخاري في (صحيحه) من حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه إنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : لو دعيت إلى ذراع أو كراع لأجبت ، ولو أهدي إلي ذراع أو كراع لقبلت . وما روى مالك في «الموطأ» مرسلاً عن عطاء بن عبد الله رضي الله عنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : تصافحوا يذهب الغل، وتهادوا تحابوا، يذهب الشحناء . وعلى تلك المشروعية انعقد الإجماع، وكيف لا ؟ وهي من صفات الكمال، فإنَّ الله تعالى وصف بها نفسه بقوله : أَمْ عِندَهُمْ خَزَانُ رَحْمَةِ رَبِّكَ الْعَزِيزِ الْوَهَّابِ . والبشر إذا باشرها فقد اكتسب من أشرف الصفات لما فيها من استعمال الكرم وإزالة الخس وشح النفس، وإدخال السرور في قلب الموهوب له، وإيراث المودة والمحبة ، وقال : وَمَن يُوقَ شُحَ نَفْسِهِ فَأُوْلَئِكَ هُمُ الْمُفْلِحُونَ ، فهي مندوبة، وقبولها سنة إلا لعارض ، كما إذا علم أنه مال حرام، أو أنه يمتن عليه بما أهداه إليه. وسببها : إرادة الخير للواهب، دنيوي كعوض محبة وحسن ثناء، وأخروي وهو الثواب، ذكر في «النهاية : قال الإمام أبو منصور : يجب على المؤمن أن يُعَلِّم ولده (١) النساء : ٤ . (۲) في (صحيح البخاري»(۲ : ۹۰۸)، وغيرها. (۳) «الموطأ» (۲ : ۹۰۸) ، و (سنن البيهقي الكبير (٦ : ١٦٩)، وغيرها. (٤) ص : ٩ . (٥) الحشر : ۹. كتاب الهبة تعريفها وأحكامها [۱] ٤٥١ تمليك عين بلا عوض (هي تمليك عين بلا عوض الجود والإحسان، كما يجب عليه أن يُعَلِّمه التوحيد والإيمان ؛ إذ حبُّ الدينار رأس كل خطيئة. انتهى. وشرائط صحتها في الواهب : العقل، والبلوغ، والملك، فلا تصح هبة المجنون والصغير والعبد، ولو مكاتباً ، أو أم ولد، أو مُدبّراً، أو مُبعضاً، وغير المالك. وشرائط صحتها في الموهوب : أن يكون مقبوضاً غير مشاع، مميزاً غير مشغول، كما سيأتيك تفصيلُهُ إن شاء الله تعالى. وركنها الإيجاب والقبول، وسيأتيك تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى. وحكمها : ثبوت الملكِ في العين الموهوبة غير لازم ، حتى يصح الرجوعُ والفسخ، وعدم صحة خيار الشرط فيها ، وإنّها لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة. [١) قوله : هي تمليك عين بلا عوض ؛ فالتمليك إشارة إلى أن الهبة لا تصح إلا تمن يقع عنه التمليك، وهو الحر العاقل البالغ المالك، فلا تصح من القن والمكاتب والمدبر وأم الولد والمجنون والصغير وغير الم المالك، والمراد بالعين : عين المال لا العين المطلق بقرينة التمليك المضاف إليه ؛ لأنَّ العينَ الذي ليس بمال لا يفيد الملك. فبقوله: تمليك ؛ خرج الوديعة، فإنّها أمانةٌ تركت للحفظ، فلا يملكها المودع. وبقوله : عين ؛ خرجت الإجارة والإعارة لكونها تمليك منفعة. وبقوله : بلا عوض ، خرج البيع ؛ لكونها تمليك عين بعوض. ويرد على هذا التعريف أنه صادق على الوصية ؛ لأنها أيضاً تمليك عين بلا عوض، وإن كان بعد الموت، وعلى الصدقة، ولا يصدق على الهبة بعوض، فينتقض طرداً وعكساً، فاحتيج إلى تكلف، وهو أنَّ المراد بالتمليك هو التمليك حالاً بقرينة أنَّ قول الواهب : وهبتُ ؛ لإنشاء الهبة حالاً ، كبعت. وأن المراد بقوله : بلا عوض بلا شرط عوض، لا أن عدم العوض شرط فيه ؛ لينتقض التعريف بالهبة بشرط العوض، لكن الأولى أن يقال : هذا التعريف للهبة المطلقة لا لمطلق الهبة، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار المرادِ المذكور، فإنَّ فيه حذف ما ليس له قرينة صريحة، ولإخراج الصدقة.
It is a legitimate contract. The proof of its legitimacy is His saying: 'But if they give up willingly to you anything of it...' and what al-Bukhari narrated in his Sahih from Abu Huraira that the Prophet said: 'If I were invited to a foreleg or a shank I would accept, and if a foreleg or a shank were gifted to me I would accept.' And what Malik narrated in al-Muwatta' as a mursal from 'Ata' ibn 'Abdullah that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Shake hands, rancor disappears; exchange gifts, you love each other, hatred disappears.' And upon that legitimacy consensus has been reached. How could it not be? It is among the attributes of perfection, for Allah describes Himself with it in His saying: 'Or do they have the treasures of the mercy of your Lord, the Almighty, the Bestower?' And when a human performs it, he acquires the noblest attributes because it involves generosity, removal of miserliness, bringing joy to the recipient, and engendering love. And He said: 'And whoever is protected from the stinginess of his soul, it is those who are successful.' So it is recommended (mandub), and accepting it is sunnah except for a hindrance, such as knowing it is unlawful wealth or that the giver will reproach him for it. Its cause is the desire for good for the giver, worldly like reciprocation of love and praise, and otherworldly which is reward. Mentioned in al-Nihayah: Imam Abu Mansur said: It is obligatory for a believer to teach his child that love of the dinar is the root of every sin. End. Its conditions of validity regarding the giver: sanity, puberty, and ownership. So the gift of an insane person, a minor, a slave—even if a mukatab, umm walad, mudabbar, muba'd, or a non-owner—is not valid. Conditions of validity regarding the gifted item: that it be possessed, non-partitioned (ghayr musha'), distinct, and not occupied, as will come in detail if Allah wills. Its pillar is offer and acceptance, and its verification will come if Allah wills. Its ruling: ownership of the gifted item is established but not binding, so revocation and annulment are valid; option of condition is not valid in it; and it is not invalidated by corrupt conditions. [1] His saying: 'It is the transfer of ownership of a tangible thing without compensation.' So 'tamlik' indicates that hiba is only valid from one who can effect transfer—the free, sane, adult owner—so it is not valid from a slave, mukatab, mudabbar, umm walad, insane, minor, or non-owner. By 'ayn' is meant the capital asset, not absolute 'ayn, due to the context of tamlik; because a non-property 'ayn does not confer ownership. By 'tamlik' emerges wadi'ah (deposit), for it is a trust left for safekeeping; the depositee does not own it. By 'ayn' emerge ijarah (lease) and i'arah (loan) as they are transfer of usufruct. By 'bila 'iwad' emerges sale because it is transfer of property for compensation. It is objected that this definition applies to wasiyyah (bequest) because it is also transfer of property without compensation, albeit after death, and to sadaqah, and it does not apply to hiba with compensation, so it is invalid both by inclusion and exclusion. Thus a complication is needed: that by 'tamlik' is meant immediate transfer, indicated by the giver's saying 'I give' as an immediate declaration like 'I sell,' and by 'bila 'iwad' is meant without a condition of compensation, not that absolute absence of compensation is a condition, otherwise the definition would be invalidated by hiba with conditional compensation. But it is better to say: this definition is for absolute hiba not for hiba in general, so there is no need to assume the mentioned meaning, for it involves omitting what lacks a clear context, and to exclude sadaqah.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the legal definition of hiba (gift) in the Hanafi school. The author begins by stating that hiba is a legitimate contract and provides proofs from the Quran (Surah al-Nisa 4:4), the Sunnah (hadith about accepting gifts and the hadith on exchanging gifts to increase love), and consensus (ijma'). The ruling is that hiba is mandub (recommended) and accepting it is sunnah, unless there is a reason not to, such as if the gift is from unlawful wealth or if acceptance would lead to reproach. The cause (sabab) of hiba is the desire for good, whether worldly (like reciprocation) or otherworldly (reward). The author then explains the conditions for validity: the giver must be sane, adult, and owner (so gifts from an insane person, minor, or slave are invalid); the gifted item must be possessed, undivided, distinct, and free from encumbrances. The pillars are offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). The ruling (hukm) is that ownership is established but not binding, allowing revocation. The commentary then analyzes the matn definition 'tamlik 'ayn bi-la 'iwad' (transfer of ownership of a tangible thing without compensation), showing how each part excludes other contracts: 'tamlik' excludes deposit, 'ayn' excludes leases and loans (usufruct), and 'bila 'iwad' excludes sale. The commentator addresses an objection that the definition also applies to bequest and charity, and resolves it by specifying that hiba is immediate transfer and the absence of compensation is without anticipation of return, but prefers to say that the definition is for absolute hiba (not hiba with reward or charity).

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A contract transferring ownership of a tangible asset immediately without compensation, as defined by the matn 'tamlik 'ayn bi-la 'iwad'.
تمليك عين بلا عوض (tamlik 'ayn bi-la 'iwad): Transfer of ownership of a specific tangible thing without any compensation, the technical definition of hiba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition, what distinguishes hiba from a sale?
A) Hiba transfers ownership of a tangible thing without compensation, while sale transfers ownership with compensation.
B) Hiba requires a specific verbal formula, while sale does not.
C) Hiba is only valid for family members, while sale is for everyone.
D) Hiba can be revoked easily, while sale cannot.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of absolute hiba as immediate transfer of a tangible asset without any condition of compensation is the cornerstone for distinguishing it from other contracts and applying its specific rulings.

Definition and Conditions of Hiba (Gift)
Defining hiba and explaining its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hiba, identify its essential conditions for validity, and explain why the definition excludes sale, deposit, and other contracts.

Core Idea
Hiba is a donation of property without compensation; delivery and capacity are essential for validity.

This lesson unpacks the formal Hanafi definition of hiba (gift) and its conditions. The passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah dissects each term of the definition and clarifies the requirements for donor and gifted object, including the important condition of delivery (qabd).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
The definition is 'tamlik ‘ayn bi-lā ‘iwaḍ' (transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation). Locate this in the text.
2
Unpack each term
Discuss the implications: 'tamlik' restricts to capable persons, ‘ayn' means property (not usufruct), 'bi-lā ‘iwaḍ' distinguishes from sale.
3
List the conditions for donor and gift
Donor must be sane, adult, owner. Gift must be delivered (maqbūḍ), not an undivided share, distinguishable, and not encumbered.
4
Understand the ruling (ḥukm)
Ownership transfers but is ghayr lāzim (not binding); revocation is possible under certain conditions.
5
Address objections to the definition
The definition seems to include bequest (waṣiyyah) and charity (ṣadaqah). The commentator resolves by restricting to 'absolute gift' (al-hiba al-muṭlaqah) or by interpreting 'immediate transfer' and 'without condition of compensation'.
