وتصح بوهبت، ونحلت، وأعطيتك
وتصح بوهبت، ونحلت، وأعطيتك
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
قال البرْجَنْدِي : ويمكن أن يقال : هي خارجة، بقوله: بلا عوض، إذ عوضُ المتصدق هو الثواب غالباً، وهو عوض. انتهى.
أقول : فيه ما فيه ؛ فإنَّ سبب الهبة هو إرادة الخير للواهب، سواء كان دنيوياً كحسن ثناء مثلاً، أو أخروياً وهو الثواب كما عرفت سابقاً، نعم يمكن الرجوع إلى القول بالتغاير الاعتباري، وبقي هاهنا إشكال قوي، وهو أنّ هبة الطاعاتِ هبة صحيحة عند أهل السنة. كما صرحوا به، ولا يصدق عليه تمليك عين، فتدبر.
[1] قوله : وتصح بوهبت.... الخ ؛ إنّما تصح الهبة بقوله : وهبت ؛ لأنه صريح فيه، وبقوله : نحلت ؛ لأنه مستعمل فيه مجازاً، وكذا بقوله : أعطيتك، فإنّه أيضاً مستعمل في الهبة مجازاً، يقال : أعطاك الله، ووهبك الله بمعنى واحد. وبقوله : أطعمتك هذا الطعام لما ذكره الشارح، فإن الإطعام ... الخ، ولعلَّ في إطلاق قوله : وتصح بوهبت... الخ ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّ الهبة تصح وتقع بهذه الأقوال وإن صدرت هذه الأقوال عن القائل مزاحاً نقله في «البحر»(۱) عن «الخلاصة». هبني وقال المقدسي : الذي في «الخلاصة»: إنّه طلب الهبة مزاحاً لا جداً فوهبه جداً وسَلَّم صحت الهبة ؛ لأنَّ الواهب غيرُ مازح، وقد قبل الموهوب له قبولاً صحيحاً. انتهى. وما نقله صاحب «التنوير» عن «الخزانة» مستدلاً به على ما في «متنه»(٢) لا يفيده. فإنه نحو ما في «الخلاصة»، وعبارتها : لو قال هذا الشيء على وجه المزاح، فقال : وهب وسلم جاز. انتهى. وكذا ما في «القُهُسْتانِي (٣) لا يفيده، ونصه : ويدخلُ فيه ما يكون على وجه المزاح، فلو قال : هب لي كذا، فقال: وهبت، وقال الآخر : قبلتُ وسَلَّم إليه جاز. انتهى. فالحاصل أن التسليم قرينة في المزاح، وهو معتبر في تمام الهبة، فالتسليم قرينة على أن الهبة صدرت من الواهب جداً لا مزاحاً، وعن ابن المبارك : أنه مر على قوم
Book of Hiba: Its definition and rulings. Ownership of an asset without compensation (it is the transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation, generosity and benevolence, as it is obligatory upon him to teach him tawheed and faith; for love of the dinar is the head of every sin. End.) And its conditions of validity regarding the donor: sanity, majority, and ownership, so the gift of the insane, minor, and slave is invalid, even if the slave is a mukatab, or umm walad, or mudabbar, or part-free, and the non-owner. And its conditions of validity regarding the gifted object: that it be received, not undivided, distinguished, not occupied, as its detail will come to you if Allah wills. And its pillar is offer and acceptance, and its investigation will come to you if Allah wills. And its ruling: the establishment of ownership in the gifted asset is not binding, so revocation and rescission are valid, and the option of condition is not valid in it, and it is not invalidated by invalid conditions. [1) His statement: It is the transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation; so 'transfer' indicates that a gift is only valid from the one from whom transfer occurs, and he is the free, sane, adult, owner, so it is invalid from the slave, mukatab, mudabbar, umm walad, insane, minor, and non-owner. And by 'asset' is meant property, not the absolute 'ayn, due to the context of 'transfer' added to it; because the 'ayn that is not property does not confer ownership. So by his saying 'transfer' exits wadi'ah (deposit), for it is a trust left for preservation, so the depositee does not own it. And by his saying 'asset' exits ijarah (hire) and i'arah (loan) because they are transfer of usufruct. And by his saying 'without compensation' exits sale, because it is transfer of an asset for compensation. And an objection is raised against this definition that it applies to wasiyyah (bequest), because it is also transfer of an asset without compensation, even if after death, and to sadaqah (charity), and it does not apply to a gift with compensation, so it is invalid both ways (inclusion and exclusion). So a forced interpretation is needed, which is that what is meant by 'transfer' is immediate transfer, due to the context that the donor's statement 'I gave' is for instant creation of gift, like 'I sold'. And that what is meant by 'without compensation' is without condition of compensation, not that absence of compensation is a condition in it, to avoid the definition being invalidated by a gift with a condition of compensation. But the better view is to say: This definition is for the absolute gift (al-hiba al-mutlaqah) not for gift in general, so there is no need to consider the mentioned interpretation, because it involves omission without a clear context, and to exclude sadaqah. And it is valid by 'I gave' and 'I granted' and 'I gave you'. Commentary from al-Barjandi: It can be said that it (sadaqah) is excluded by his saying 'without compensation', because the compensation of the one who gives charity is reward (thawab) usually, and that is a compensation. End. I say: There is something in it; because the cause of hiba is the desire for good for the donor, whether worldly like good reputation, or otherworldly which is reward as you have known previously. Indeed, one can return to the view of conceptual distinction. And there remains here a strong problem: that the gift of acts of obedience (hiba al-ta'at) is a valid gift according to Ahl al-Sunnah, as they have stated, and it does not apply to it 'transfer of an asset', so ponder. [1) His statement: 'And it is valid by 'I gave'... etc.'; indeed, the gift is valid by his saying 'wahaba' because it is explicit in it, and by his saying 'nahaltu' because it is used metaphorically, and similarly by his saying 'a'daytuka', for it is also used metaphorically for gift. It is said: 'A'taka Allah' and 'Wahabaka Allah' with the same meaning. And by his saying 'I fed you this food' as mentioned by the commentator, for feeding... etc. And perhaps in the absolute statement of his saying 'And it is valid by 'I gave' ... etc.' is an indication that the gift is valid and occurs by these statements even if these statements are made by the speaker in jest, as transmitted in 'al-Bahr' from 'al-Khulasah'. 'Hibni' and al-Maqdisi said: What is in 'al-Khulasah' is that he asked for a gift in jest not seriously, then he (the donor) gave it to him seriously and handed over, the gift is valid because the donor is not joking, and the donee accepted a valid acceptance. End. And what the author of 'al-Tanwir' transmitted from 'al-Khazinah' as evidence for what is in his matn does not support it. For it is similar to what is in 'al-Khulasah', and its expression: If he said this thing in jest, and said: 'I gave' and handed over, it is permissible. End. And similarly what is in 'al-Qahistani' does not support it, and its text: And it includes what is in jest, so if he said: 'Grant me such-and-such', and he said: 'I granted', and the other said: 'I accept', and handed over to him, it is permissible. End. So the conclusion is that delivery (taslim) is an indication in jest, and it is considered in the completion of the gift, so delivery is an indication that the gift came from the donor seriously not jokingly. And from Ibn al-Mubarak: that he passed by a people...
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah opens the chapter of Hiba (gift) with a formal definition, conditions, and rulings. The author first defines hiba as 'transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation' (tamlik 'ayn bi-la 'iwad). The commentator then unpacks each term: 'tamlik' restricts the gift to those capable of transferring ownership—free, sane, adult, owner—thus excluding slaves, minors, and the insane. '.Ayn' is specified as property, not just any object, so non-property cannot be gifted. 'Bi-la 'iwad' distinguishes hiba from sale. The commentator notes objections to the definition: it also covers bequest (wasiyya) and charity (sadaqa), and excludes gifts with compensation. He resolves this by interpreting 'tamlik' as immediate and 'bi-la 'iwad' as without a condition of compensation, but prefers to restrict the definition to 'absolute gift' (al-hiba al-mutlaqah). Next, the passage lists conditions for validity: for the donor, sanity, majority, and ownership; for the gift, that it be delivered (maqbud), not an undivided share (ghayr musha'), distinguishable (mumayyaz), and unoccupied (ghayr mashghul). The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul). The ruling (hukm) is that ownership is established but not binding (ghayr lazim), allowing revocation. The text then addresses valid expressions for the gift: 'wahaba', 'nahala', and 'a'ta' are explicit or metaphorical. The commentator discusses the effect of jest (mizah): a gift uttered in jest can be valid if the donor actually delivers (taslim), as delivery indicates seriousness. He cites authorities like al-Khulasah and al-Qahistani to show that delivery overrides the initial jest. The passage ends with a reference to Ibn al-Mubarak, likely illustrating a similar scenario.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): The transfer of ownership of a specific asset without compensation, as defined in the matn.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; the act by which the donor gives ownership to the donee.
عين (‘ayn): A specific asset or property, not including usufruct or non-property.
عوض (‘iwad): Compensation; in hiba, the absence of compensation is essential.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, which of the following is a condition for the validity of a gift?
A) The gift must be delivered (maqbūḍ)
B) The gift must be of high value
C) The donor must be a male
D) The gift must be witnessed by two men

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hiba is precise: it requires immediate transfer of ownership, no compensation in fact or condition, and delivery for completion.

Valid Expressions and the Effect of Jest in Hiba
Discussing valid expressions and the effect of jest on hiba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid verbal formulas for hiba and explain the Hanafi rule that delivery (taslim) overrides a jestful utterance, validating the gift.

Core Idea
A gift spoken in jest becomes valid if the donor actually delivers the item—delivery proves sincerity.

This lesson examines the words that can create a valid gift and the intriguing case of a gift made in jest. The Hanafi jurists discuss whether a humorous offer can be binding; the answer hinges on the act of delivery (taslim), which transforms jest into a serious transaction.

Guided Path
1
List the valid expressions
The words 'wahaba' (I gave), 'nahala' (I granted), and 'a'ta' (I gave) are valid. 'At'amtu' (I fed) is also valid for food.
'Wahaba' is explicit (ṣarīḥ); 'nahala' and 'a'ta' are metaphorical (majāz) but commonly used.
2
Understand the effect of jest
If the words are spoken in jest, the gift is not valid merely by utterance. However, if the donor actually delivers (taslim) the item, that delivery is a qarīnah (indication) of seriousness, making the gift valid.
3
Analyze the scenario from al-Khulāṣah
A person says 'hibnī' (grant me) in jest; the donor says 'wahaba' and delivers. The gift is valid because the donor's delivery shows he was not joking.
4
Identify the key principle
Delivery (taslim) is a qarīnah that the gift was intended seriously, overriding the initial jest.
5
Supporting citations
The passage quotes al-Khulāṣah, al-Tanwīr, al-Qahistānī, and Ibn al-Mubārak to reinforce the rule.

كتاب الهبة تعريفها وأحكامها
تمليك عين بلا عوض (هي تمليك عين بلا عوض الجود والإحسان، كما يجب عليه أن يُعَلِّمه التوحيد والإيمان ؛ إذ حبُّ الدينار رأس كل خطيئة. انتهى.
وشرائط صحتها في الواهب : العقل، والبلوغ، والملك، فلا تصح هبة المجنون والصغير والعبد، ولو مكاتباً، أو أم ولد، أو مُدبّراً، أو مُبعضاً، وغير المالك. وشرائط صحتها في الموهوب : أن يكون مقبوضاً غير مشاع، مميزاً غير مشغول، كما سيأتيك تفصيلُهُ إن شاء الله تعالى.
وركنها الإيجاب والقبول، وسيأتيك تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى. وحكمها : ثبوت الملكِ في العين الموهوبة غير لازم، حتى يصح الرجوعُ والفسخ، وعدم صحة خيار الشرط فيها، وإنّها لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة. [١) قوله : هي تمليك عين بلا عوض ؛ فالتمليك إشارة إلى أن الهبة لا تصح إلا ممن يقع عنه التمليك، وهو الحر العاقل البالغ المالك، فلا تصح من القن والمكاتب والمدبر وأم الولد والمجنون والصغير وغير المالك، والمراد بالعين : عين المال لا العين المطلق بقرينة التمليك المضاف إليه ؛ لأنَّ العينَ الذي ليس بمال لا يفيد الملك. فبقوله: تمليك ؛ خرج الوديعة، فإنّها أمانةٌ تركت للحفظ، فلا يملكها المودع. وبقوله : عين ؛ خرجت الإجارة والإعارة لكونها تمليك منفعة. وبقوله : بلا عوض، خرج البيع ؛ لكونها تمليك عين بعوض. ويرد على هذا التعريف أنه صادق على الوصية ؛ لأنها أيضاً تمليك عين بلا عوض، وإن كان بعد الموت، وعلى الصدقة، ولا يصدق على الهبة بعوض، فينتقض طرداً وعكساً، فاحتيج إلى تكلف، وهو أنَّ المراد بالتمليك هو التمليك حالاً بقرينة أنَّ قول الواهب : وهبتُ ؛ لإنشاء الهبة حالاً، كبعت. وأن المراد بقوله : بلا عوض بلا شرط عوض، لا أن عدم العوض شرط فيه ؛ لينتقض التعريف بالهبة بشرط العوض، لكن الأولى أن يقال : هذا التعريف للهبة المطلقة لا لمطلق الهبة، فلا حاجة إلى اعتبار المرادِ المذكور، فإنَّ فيه حذف ما ليس له قرينة صريحة، ولإخراج الصدقة.

وتصح بوهبت، ونحلت، وأعطيتك

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah opens the chapter of Hiba (gift) with a formal definition, conditions, and rulings. The author first defines hiba as 'transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation' (tamlik 'ayn bi-la 'iwad). The commentator then unpacks each term: 'tamlik' restricts the gift to those capable of transferring ownership—free, sane, adult, owner—thus excluding slaves, minors, and the insane. '.Ayn' is specified as property, not just any object, so non-property cannot be gifted. 'Bi-la 'iwad' distinguishes hiba from sale. The commentator notes objections to the definition: it also covers bequest (wasiyya) and charity (sadaqa), and excludes gifts with compensation. He resolves this by interpreting 'tamlik' as immediate and 'bi-la 'iwad' as without a condition of compensation, but prefers to restrict the definition to 'absolute gift' (al-hiba al-mutlaqah). Next, the passage lists conditions for validity: for the donor, sanity, majority, and ownership; for the gift, that it be delivered (maqbud), not an undivided share (ghayr musha'), distinguishable (mumayyaz), and unoccupied (ghayr mashghul). The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul). The ruling (hukm) is that ownership is established but not binding (ghayr lazim), allowing revocation. The text then addresses valid expressions for the gift: 'wahaba', 'nahala', and 'a'ta' are explicit or metaphorical. The commentator discusses the effect of jest (mizah): a gift uttered in jest can be valid if the donor actually delivers (taslim), as delivery indicates seriousness. He cites authorities like al-Khulasah and al-Qahistani to show that delivery overrides the initial jest. The passage ends with a reference to Ibn al-Mubarak, likely illustrating a similar scenario.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): The transfer of ownership of a specific asset without compensation, as defined in the matn.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; the act by which the donor gives ownership to the donee.
عين (‘ayn): A specific asset or property, not including usufruct or non-property.
عوض (‘iwad): Compensation; in hiba, the absence of compensation is essential.

Checkpoint
What is the decisive factor that makes a gift valid when it was initially uttered in jest?
A) The donee's acceptance
B) The presence of witnesses
C) The delivery (taslīm) of the gifted item
D) A written contract

Key Takeaway
Delivery (taslīm) can override a jestful utterance and validate a gift, as it serves as an objective sign of serious intention.

Valid Expressions for Hibah and the Effect of Jest
Valid expressions for hibah and effect of jest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the valid expressions for hibah and explain why a gift made in jest is valid if delivery occurs.

Core Idea
A hibah is valid with any explicit or customary expression, and even jest is cured by delivery.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies which words make a valid gift and what happens if the giver was joking. The key is that delivery (taslim) reveals the true intention. Understanding this protects against a common misconception that jest voids a gift entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Valid Expressions
The text lists 'wahaba', 'nahala', 'a'tayta', and mentions 'at'amtu' (I fed). Determine why these are valid: some are explicit (sarih) and others are metaphorical (majaz) but commonly used for gifting.
Metaphorical expressions are valid if customarily understood as a gift.
2
Analyze the Role of Jest
If a person utters a gift in jest, the ruling is that the gift is valid if delivery (taslim) occurs. Delivery serves as a qarinah (indication) that the speaker was serious.
The text cites sources like al-Bahr and al-Khulasa to support this.
3
Examine the Ibn al-Mubarak Story
Ibn al-Mubarak asked for a musical instrument in jest, was given it, then broke it. The gift was valid, but he was liable for compensation according to Abu Hanifa because breaking musical instruments is an actionable wrong.
This illustrates that jest does not invalidate the gift, but other rulings apply separately.
4
Distinguish Jest from Serious Intent
The conclusion is that delivery (taslim) is a clear indication that the gift was intended seriously. Without delivery, a jest may be invalid, but with it, the gift is complete.
This principle protects transactions from ambiguity.

And it is valid with 'I gifted', 'I donated', and 'I gave you'. ... The summary: after they agreed on the pillar of offer, they differed on the pillar of acceptance. The author of al-Tuhfah said: its pillars are offer and acceptance, and al-Quduri and the author of al-Hidayah and al-Kafi chose that. Its reasoning: hibah is a contract, and every contract is concluded by offer and acceptance. But it is objected that the major premise is denied because a gratuitous contract is completed by mere offer and does not require acceptance, like sadaqah, as stated in al-Hidayah and others, so likewise hibah.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the matn 'al-Wiqayah'. It begins by affirming that hibah (gift) is valid with the words 'wahaba' (given as a gift), 'nahala' (donated), and 'a'tayta' (gave). These words are either explicit or metaphorically used for hibah. The text then discusses that even if uttered in jest, if delivery (taslim) occurs, the hibah is valid because delivery indicates seriousness. A story of Ibn al-Mubarak is cited where he took a musical instrument from people in jest and broke it, but because he had said 'give it to me' and they delivered it, the gift was valid; however, he was liable for breaking it according to Abu Hanifa because breaking musical instruments requires compensation. The passage then shifts to the debate on whether acceptance (qabul) is a pillar (rukn) in hibah. The commentator notes that the phrase 'wa tasihhu bi wahabtu' indicates that acceptance is not a pillar, as some Hanafi sources state. However, other authorities like al-Kafi and al-Tuhfah consider acceptance a pillar because hibah is a contract. The commentator favors the view that acceptance is not required, drawing an analogy to sadaqah (charity), which is completed by offer alone. This is the preferred (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): A gift; a gratuitous transfer of ownership without compensation.
مزاح (mazah): Jest; a statement made in play, which may affect the validity of a contract if not followed by delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The donee's verbal acceptance
B) The presence of witnesses
C) Delivery (qabd) of the gifted item
D) A written contract

Key Takeaway
A hibah uttered in jest is valid if delivery occurs; delivery reveals the true intention and completes the gift.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Acceptance as a Pillar of Hibah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on acceptance as a pillar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether acceptance (qabul) is a pillar of hibah and identify the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Acceptance is not a pillar of hibah according to the majority; gratuitous contracts are completed by offer alone.

This passage presents a significant internal debate within the Hanafi school: is acceptance (qabul) a rukn (pillar) of hibah? The text cites authorities on both sides and then provides the preferred view. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to navigate ikhtilaf within a single madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The debate: is acceptance (qabul) a pillar (rukn) of the hibah contract, or is the offer (ijab) sufficient?
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
2
State the Opposing View
Some Hanafi scholars (al-Quduri, author of al-Hidayah, al-Kafi) hold that acceptance is a pillar because hibah is a contract and every contract requires offer and acceptance.
They base this on the general rule for contracts.
3
State the Majority View
Imam Abu Hanifa and the majority of the Hanafi school hold that acceptance is not a pillar. Their evidence: hibah is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'), similar to sadaqah, which is completed by offer alone.
They deny the major premise that all contracts require acceptance.
4
Analyze the Analogy to Sadaqah
The Hanafi position uses qiyas: sadaqah is a gratuitous transfer that does not require acceptance; similarly, hibah being gratuitous does not require it.
This analogy is the core of the dalil.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if a donor says 'I gift you this' and the donee does not verbally accept, but delivery occurs, the gift is valid according to the majority view.
The minority view would consider the gift invalid until acceptance.

Source Text

Validity of a Gift Made in Jest (Hibat al-Mazih)
Incident proving validity of joking gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a gift (hibah) given in jest is valid according to the Hanafi school, and explain the proof from the incident of the tanbur.

Core Idea
A joking gift is valid because ownership transfers by offer and delivery, regardless of intent.

In this lesson, we examine an interesting incident from al-Khaniyyah where a shaykh asked for a tanbur as a gift and then broke it. The donors felt deceived, but the shaykh's action serves as a proof that a gift made in jest (hibat al-mazih) is valid. This ruling is a departure from the general principle that contracts require serious intent, but Hanafi law upholds it based on the external act.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue
Identify the legal question: Is a gift made in jest (hibat al-mazih) valid? The giver lacked serious intent but performed the offer and delivery.
This is a specific sub-issue within hibah.
2
Understand the Incident
Read the incident: A shaykh asked a group for their tanbur as a gift. They gave it to him. He immediately broke it. They complained of being deceived. The shaykh argued that the gift was valid, so he broke his own property.
The tanbur is a stringed musical instrument.
3
Analyze the Legal Implication
The shaykh's argument implies that the gift was valid despite his joking intention. The donors' complaint of deception does not invalidate the transfer because offer (by the donors) and delivery (qabd) occurred.
In Hanafi law, the pillars of hibah are ijab (offer) and qabd (delivery).
4
Connect to Abu Hanifa's View
The commentator notes that this ruling is a precaution against Abu Hanifa's view that breaking musical instruments (like the tanbur) requires compensation. Since the gift was valid, the shaykh owned it and broke his own property, thus no compensation.
Abu Hanifa's view is mentioned to show the practical benefit of the ruling.
5
Conclude the Ruling
The incident is evidence that a gift from a joking person (mazih) is valid. The Hanafi school holds that jest does not invalidate hibah, contrary to some other schools that require serious intent.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يضربون بالطنبور ، فقال لهم : هبوا هذا منّي فدفعوه إليه، فضرب به الأرض فكسره ، فقالوا : يا شيخ خدعتنا. انتهى. ذلك قال في «المنح») : وذكر هذه الواقعة في «الخانية»: ثم قال : وإنما قال لهم. احترازاً من قول أبي حنيفة الله ، فإن عنده كسر الملاهي يوجب الضمان، وهذا دليل على جواز هبة المازح. انتهى.
They were beating the tanbur, so he said to them: 'Gift this to me.' So they gave it to him, he struck it on the ground and broke it. They said: 'O shaykh, you have deceived us.' End. That is what was said in 'al-Munh': And this incident was mentioned in 'al-Khaniyyah'. Then he said: And he said to them only as a precaution against the saying of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. For according to him, breaking musical instruments necessitates compensation, and this is evidence for the validity of a joking gift. End.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents an incident from the Khaniyyah as evidence for the validity of a gift made in jest. The shaykh asked for a tanbur as a gift, then immediately broke it. The donors felt deceived, but the shaykh argued that the gift was valid because it was given. The commentator notes that this ruling is a precaution against Abu Hanifa's view that breaking musical instruments requires compensation. Here, since the gift was valid, ownership transferred, and the shaykh broke his own property, so no liability. This proves that a joking gift is valid. The key point is that the shaykh intended to educate them, and the gift was executed with offer and delivery. The ruling is that a gift from a joking person (mazih) is valid, contrary to the principle that joking in contracts may invalidate them. However, in hiba, jest does not prevent validity.

Key Terms
هبة المازح (hibat al-mazih): A gift given in jest; it is valid in Hanafi law despite lack of serious intent.
طنبور (tanbur): A stringed musical instrument mentioned in the incident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the shaykh's action is a precaution against Abu Hanifa's view?
A) Abu Hanifa said that joking gifts are invalid.
B) Abu Hanifa said that breaking musical instruments requires compensation, so the shaykh wanted to avoid liability.
C) Abu Hanifa said that a gift is only valid if the giver is serious.
D) Abu Hanifa said that the donors could revoke the gift.

Key Takeaway
A gift (hibah) given in jest is valid in the Hanafi school because the external act of offer and delivery transfers ownership; intent is not required.

Acceptance as Pillar of Hibah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on acceptance as pillar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on acceptance in hibah, identify the proofs for each, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) regarding oaths.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, acceptance is not a pillar of hibah; the offer alone concludes the gift.

The question of whether acceptance (qabul) is a pillar (rukn) of the gift contract (hibah) is not merely theoretical—it affects when a gift becomes binding and how oaths are fulfilled. This lesson unpacks the key proofs and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine exactly what is being disputed: is acceptance a pillar of hibah or not? Note that all agree offer (ijab) is a pillar.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; the Shafi'i view is mentioned only in passing.
2
Analyze the proofs for each view
Examine the dalil for the view that acceptance is not a pillar (based on analogy to sadaqah and gift of debt). Then examine the opposing view that acceptance is a pillar (based on analogy to sale and the nature of contracts).
The major premise 'all contracts require acceptance' is challenged.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence appears in oaths: if someone swears not to give a gift and then makes an offer without acceptance, does he break his oath? Both views lead to the same result here because the offer itself manifests generosity.
Even those who require acceptance for the contract still say the oath is broken because the purpose of the oath is the manifestation of generosity.
4
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that acceptance is not a pillar, as chosen by the author of al-Hidayah and others. Note that delivery (qabd) remains a condition for ownership to be established.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قوله : وتصح بوهبت ; دلالة على أنَّ القبول ليس بركن. كما أشار إليه في «الخلاصة» وغيرها، وذكر في الكِرْمَانِي : أن الإيجاب في الهبة عقد تام، وفي «المبسوط» : إنَّ القبض كالقبول في البيع ; ولذا لو وهب الدين من الغريم لم يفتقر إلى القبول. كما في «الكبرى». لكن في «الكافي» و«التحفة»: إنّه ركن، وذكر في الكِرْمَانِي»: إنّها تفتقر إلى الإيجاب ; لأن ملك الإنسان لم ينتقل إلى الغير بدونِ تمليكه وإلى القبول; لأنه إلزام الملك على الغير، وإنما يحنث إذا حلف أن لا يهب فوهب، ولم يقبل; لأنَّ الغرض عدم إظهار الجود، وقد وجد الإظهار. ذكره القُهُسْتَانِيُّ في «شرح النقاية (٢). والحاصل : إنّهم بعدما اتفقوا على ركنيّة الإيجاب اختلفوا في ركنية القبول، فقال صاحب «التحفة»: إنّ ركنها الإيجاب والقبول، واختاره القُدُوري (۳)، وصاحب «الهداية»(4)، و«الكافي» حيث قال : تصح بالإيجاب والقبول. انتهى. ووجهه : إنَّ الهبة عقد، وكل عقد ينعقد بالإيجاب والقبول، وفيه : إنّ الكبرى ممنوعة ; لأنَّ عقد التبرع يتم بمجرَّدِ الإيجاب ولا يفتقر إلى القبول، كالصدقة. كما صرح به في «الهداية»(٥) ، وغيرها، فكذلك الهبة أيضاً. وقال شيخ الإسلام خُواهَر زَادَه : إن الركن مجرد الإيجاب، قال في «الفتاوى العالمكيرية»: وأما ركنها فقول الواهب وهبت ; لأنه تمليك ، وإنّه يتم بالمالك وحدّد، والقبض شرط ثبوت الملك للموهوب له، حتى لو حلف لا يهب فوهب ولم يقبل الآخر حنث. كذا في «محيط السرخسي». انتهى. وقال في غاية «البيان»: ولهذا قال علماؤنا : إذا حلف لا يهب فوهب ولم يقبل يحنث في يمينه. انتهى. وفيه إنَّ الحنث ; لأنه إنما منع نفسه عما في وسعه، وقبول الغير ليس في وسعه، فافهم، هذا ما وعدته سابقاً، وهاهنا أبحاث نفيسة تركناها لغرابة المقام، ومحلها مبسوطات الأعلام.
And in His statement: 'and it is valid by 'I gave as a gift'' is an indication that acceptance is not a pillar. As indicated in al-Khulasa and others. And in al-Karmani it is mentioned that the offer in hibah is a complete contract. And in al-Mabsut: that the taking of possession is like acceptance in sale; therefore, if one gifts a debt to the debtor, it does not require acceptance. As in al-Kubra. However, in al-Kafi and al-Tuhfah, it is a pillar. And in al-Karmani it is mentioned that it requires the offer because one's ownership does not transfer to another without his transfer of ownership, and [requires] acceptance because it is imposing ownership on another. And he only breaks his oath if he swears not to give and gave, and the other did not accept; because the purpose is not to manifest generosity, and the manifestation has occurred. Mentioned by al-Quhistani in Sharh al-Nuqayah. The conclusion: After they agreed on the pillar being the offer, they differed on the pillar being acceptance. So the author of al-Tuhfah said: its pillars are offer and acceptance, and this was chosen by al-Quduri, the author of al-Hidayah, and al-Kafi where he said: it is valid by offer and acceptance. End. And its justification: hibah is a contract, and every contract is concluded by offer and acceptance. But there is [the objection]: The major premise is rejected because a gratuitous contract is completed by mere offer and does not require acceptance, like charity. As clarified in al-Hidayah and others, so too is hibah. And Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzadah said: the pillar is merely the offer. He said in al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyyah: as for its pillar, it is the words of the donor 'I gifted' because it is a transfer of ownership, and it is completed by the owner alone, and taking possession is a condition for the establishment of ownership for the donee, such that if he swears not to give and gave, and the other did not accept, he breaks his oath. So in Muhit al-Sarakhsi. End. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: for this reason our scholars said: if he swears not to give and gave and the other did not accept, he breaks his oath. End. The breaking of the oath is because he only prevented himself from what was within his capacity, and the other's acceptance is not within his capacity. So understand. This is what I promised earlier, and here are valuable investigations that we left due to the unfamiliarity of the subject, and their place is in the detailed works of the great scholars.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute over whether acceptance (qabul) is a pillar (rukn) of the gift contract (hibah). The discussion is triggered by the statement in the matn that the gift is valid by the utterance 'wahabtu' (I gifted), which some take to imply that acceptance is not required. The commentary reviews various positions: (1) Some say acceptance is not a pillar; the offer alone suffices because hibah is a gratuitous contract (like sadaqah). (2) Others, like the authors of al-Kafi and al-Tuhfah, hold that acceptance is a pillar, analogizing hibah to sale (bay'). (3) A middle view by Shaykh al-Islam Khwaharzadah says the pillar is the offer only, but delivery (qabd) is a condition for ownership to be established. The practical consequence is seen in oaths: if a person swears not to give a gift and then makes an offer that is not accepted, he still breaks his oath according to all, because the offer itself constitutes the gift (it is the manifestation of generosity). The text concludes by stating that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that acceptance is not a pillar, as per the Hidayah and others. The detailed investigations are left for the large commentaries.

Key Terms
ركن القبول (rukn al-qabul): The element of acceptance in the contract of gift; its necessity is disputed among Hanafis.
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The offer made by the donor, e.g., uttering 'wahabtu'.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession; it is a condition for the transfer of ownership, not a pillar of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the pillar of hibah?
A) Offer only.
B) Offer and acceptance.
C) Offer and delivery.
D) Acceptance only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon view is that acceptance is not a pillar of hibah; the gift is concluded by offer alone, while delivery is a condition for ownership transfer.

Distinction between Hibah and 'Ariyah in the Phrase 'At'amtuka'
Explains when 'at'amtuka' indicates hiba vs. ariyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the phrase 'I fed you' is interpreted as a gift (hibah) for consumables and a loan ('ariyah) for non-consumables, based on customary usage.

Core Idea
The object of 'feeding' determines whether it is a gift or a loan: for food it is hibah, for land it is 'ariyah.

This passage addresses a common scenario in Hanafi fiqh: when someone says 'I fed you' (at'amtuka), does it transfer ownership or just usufruct? The answer depends on the object. For food, which is consumed, the word customarily implies a gift (hibah). For land, which is not eaten, it implies a loan for use ('ariyah) of its produce. The commentary also notes a nuance: if the speaker explicitly says 'take possession' (faqbidhu), it is clearly hibah; otherwise, some scholars consider it ambiguous, though the relied-upon view is the default interpretation based on custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object
Determine what the phrase 'at'amtuka' is attributed to: food (ma'kul al-'ayn) or something else like land.
The object determines the legal interpretation.
2
Apply the default ruling for food
If the object is food, the word 'feeding' customarily implies transfer of ownership (hibah). This is the default.
Even if the speaker did not add 'faqbidhu', the ruling is still hibah according to the relied-upon view.
3
Apply the default ruling for land
If the object is land, which is not consumed, 'feeding' customarily refers to the usufruct (like crops), so it is 'ariyah (loan for use).
Interpreting it as hibah would be a non-customary metaphor and is not adopted.
4
Consider the nuance from al-Muhit al-Burhani
Some classical texts note that without 'faqbidhu', the phrase 'I fed you this food' could be ambiguous between hibah and 'ariyah. However, the mufta bihi (operative view) is hibah.
This nuance shows the depth of Hanafi legal analysis but does not change the relied-upon ruling.
5
Appreciate the role of custom ('urf)
The ruling is based on customary usage. The speech of a rational person must be interpreted according to what is customary, not every possible literal meaning.
Custom is a recognized source (dalil) in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1]قوله : فإنّ الإطعام ... الخ ; يعني إنّ الإطعام إذا نسب إلى ما يؤكل عينه يراد به التمليك بغير عوض، وهو الهبة ، وإذا نسب الأرض ، وعينها لا تؤكل، يكون المراد به ما يستعمل منها ، فأمكن ذلك بالعارية فيكون عارية. [١] قوله : كان هبة ; فيه أنه يخالف ما في «المحيط البرهاني»(۱) نقلاً عن «الأصل» من أنه لو قال : أطعمتك هذا الطعام فإن قال : فاقبضه فهو هبة، وإن لم يقل : فاقبضه، فهو يحتمل الهبة والعارية. [٢]قوله : : كان عارية ; وإن أمكن أن يراد بالإطعام المضاف إلى مثل الأرض تمليك العين مجازاً، لكنَّ هذا التجوز ليس بمتعارف ، وإنّما المتعارف أن يراد إطعام الغلة على طريق ذكر المحل وإرادة الحال، وكلامُ العاقل إنما يجب حمله على المتعارفِ لا على كلّ ما احتمله اللفظ. كذا في «النتائج») [ ]
[1] His saying: 'For feeding...' etc.; meaning that if 'feeding' is attributed to something whose substance is eaten, the intended meaning is transfer of ownership without compensation, which is hibah. And if attributed to land, whose substance is not eaten, the intended meaning is the usufruct derived from it, so that can be through 'ariyah (loan), thus it is 'ariyah. [1] His saying: 'it is hibah'; there is in it that it contradicts what is in al-Muhit al-Burhani, quoting from al-Asl, that if he says: 'I fed you this food', if he says: 'take possession of it', then it is hibah; and if he does not say: 'take possession of it', then it is ambiguous between hibah and 'ariyah. [2] His saying: 'it is 'ariyah'; even if it is possible that by 'feeding' attributed to land the transfer of ownership of the substance is intended metaphorically, however this metaphorical usage is not customary; rather the customary is that the feeding of produce is intended by way of mentioning the place and intending the usufruct. And the speech of a rational person must be interpreted according to the customary, not according to every possible meaning of the word. Thus in al-Nata'ij.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage comments on the maxim that if a person says 'I fed you this food' (at'amtuka hadha al-ta'am), it is a gift (hibah) because the food is edible and the word 'feeding' implies transfer of ownership. But if he says 'I fed you this land' (at'amtuka hadha al-ard), it is a loan for use ('ariyah) because land is not eaten; rather, the usufruct (like crops) is intended. The commentary notes a nuance from al-Muhit al-Burhani: if the speaker adds 'take possession of it', then it is clearly a gift; otherwise, it could be ambiguous. However, the relied-upon position is that it is a gift for food and a loan for land, based on customary usage. The passage emphasizes that speech must be interpreted according to what is customarily understood ('urf), not every possible literal meaning.

Key Terms
الإطعام (al-it'am): The utterance 'I fed you' which, depending on the object, is interpreted as a gift or a loan.
العارية (al-'ariyah): A loan for use, transfer of usufruct without compensation, as opposed to hiba which transfers ownership.
المتعارف (al-muta'arif): That which is customary; the norm in speech and practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a gift (hibah).
B) It is a loan ('ariyah).

D) It is a sale.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the phrase 'I fed you' is interpreted according to customary usage: for consumables it is a gift, for non-consumables it is a loan.

Validity of Hibah using 'Ja'altu hadha laka'
Explains that 'ja'altu' indicates transfer of ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the phrase 'ja'altu hadha laka' constitutes a valid hibah because the lam indicates tamlik, and identify the implicit qiyas used.

Core Idea
The preposition 'lam' in 'laka' transforms an ambiguous verb into a clear transfer of ownership.

In this passage, the commentator explains that a gift need not be expressed with the specific word 'hibah' or 'wahabtu'. The phrase 'Ja'altu hadha laka' (I made this for you) is equally valid because the preposition 'laka' conveys ownership. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and analogical reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the main ruling: the gift is valid when using the phrase 'ja'altu hadha laka'.
The ruling is specific to this phrasing; other phrases may be assessed similarly.
2
Linguistic basis
Recognize that the preposition 'lam' in 'laka' indicates transfer of ownership (tamlik).
This is a standard Hanafi principle: al-lam li al-tamlik.
3
Implicit qiyas
Understand the analogy: the same phrase used in a sale (with compensation) effects ownership; therefore, it also effects ownership in a gift (without compensation).
This analogy strengthens the validity by showing consistency across contracts.
4
Apply the principle
Conclude that any phrase that clearly indicates transfer of ownership, especially through the lam, can serve as a valid offer of hibah.
Delivery of possession is still required for completion, but the offer itself is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳] قوله : وجعلت هذا لك ; أي وتصح الهبة بقوله : جعلت هذا الشيء لك ; لأنَّ اللام للتمليك ، فصار كأنه قال : ملكتك هذا الشيء، ألا ترى أن ذلك لو كانَ بعوض كان تمليكاً، فكذا بغير عوض.
[3] His saying: 'And I made this for you'; meaning that the gift is valid by his saying: 'I made this thing for you'; because the lam (preposition 'for') indicates transfer of ownership, so it is as if he said: 'I have given you ownership of this thing.' Do you not see that if it was for compensation, it would be a transfer of ownership; so too without compensation.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the phrase 'Ja'altu hadha laka' (I made this for you) is a valid expression for making a gift. The key is the preposition 'lam' (laka) which indicates ownership. Thus, the utterance is equivalent to saying 'I have transferred ownership of this to you.' The analogy is that if such a phrase is used in a sale (with compensation), it effects ownership; similarly, in a gift (without compensation), it also effects ownership. This shows that the verb 'ja'ala' (to make) combined with 'lam' can serve as an offer of hibah, even though it is less direct than 'wahabtu'.

Key Terms
اللام للتمليك (al-lam li al-tamlik): The preposition 'lam' used to indicate transfer of ownership, e.g., 'laka' means 'to you as ownership'.
جعلت (ja'altu): Used as a verb to create a legal transfer, similar to 'wahaba' (to gift).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the preposition 'lam' indicates transfer of ownership.
C) Because it is followed by delivery.
D) Because it is in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'lam' in an utterance transforms it into a clear transfer of ownership, making the gift valid.

Interpreting 'It'ām' in Contracts: Gift or Loan?
Explains when the word 'feeding' implies gift or loan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that custom ('urf) governs the interpretation of ambiguous terms in contracts, specifically to distinguish between gift and loan when the word 'feeding' is used.

Core Idea
The word 'feeding' means gift for food, but loan for land — custom dictates meaning, not literal possibility.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya explains the Hanafi interpretation of the term 'it'ām' (feeding) when used in contracts. The legal effect hinges on what the feeding is attributed to: food implies a gift, land implies a loan. The commentary also introduces a nuance from al-Muhit al-Burhani and ultimately grounds the ruling in the principle of customary usage ('urf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its attribution
The key term is 'it'ām' (feeding). The first step is to determine what it is attributed to: food or land. This attribution changes the legal classification.
The passage gives two clear cases.
2
Apply the general rule for food
If attributed to food, 'it'ām' means a gift (hiba) because the substance is consumable. The owner transfers ownership without compensation.
This is the default, but see nuance in step 3.
3
Consider the nuance for food with missing command
From al-Muhit al-Burhani: if the speaker says 'I fed you this food' without commanding 'take possession', the word can be interpreted as either gift or loan. This shows that even for food, context matters.
This nuance highlights the need for additional indications.
4
Apply the rule for land using custom
For land, 'it'ām' cannot mean ownership of the substance because land is not eaten. Customarily, it means granting the use of the land or its produce. Thus it is a loan ('āriyah).
The commentary explicitly rejects a metaphorical interpretation that would make it a gift, because custom does not support it.
5
Derive the governing principle
The overarching rule is that a rational person's speech must be interpreted according to customary usage, not every literal possibility. This Hanafi usul principle (al-‘urf muḥakkam) resolves the ambiguity.
This principle is cited from al-Nata'ij.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ الإطعام إذا نسب إلى الطعام كان هبة، وإذا نسب إلى الأرض كان عارية
[1] قوله: فإنَّ الإطعام... الخ؛ يعني إنَّ الإطعام إذا نسب إلى ما يؤكل عينه يراد به التمليك بغير عوض، وهو الهبة، وإذا نسب الأرض، وعينها لا تؤكل، يكون المراد به ما يستعمل منها، فأمكن ذلك بالعارية فيكون عارية.
[١] قوله: كان هبة؛ فيه أنه يخالف ما في «المحيط البرهاني» نقلاً عن «الأصل» من أنه لو قال: أطعمتك هذا الطعام فإن قال: فاقبضه فهو هبة، وإن لم يقل: فاقبضه، فهو يحتمل الهبة والعارية.
[٢]قوله: كان عارية؛ وإن أمكن أن يراد بالإطعام المضاف إلى مثل الأرض تمليك العين مجازاً، لكنَّ هذا التجوز ليس بمتعارف، وإنّما المتعارف أن يراد إطعام الغلة على طريق ذكر المحل وإرادة الحال، وكلامُ العاقل إنما يجب حمله على المتعارفِ لا على كلّ ما احتمله اللفظ. كذا في «النتائج».
For 'feeding' when attributed to food, it is a gift, and when attributed to land, it is a loan. [1] His statement: 'For feeding...' etc.; meaning that 'feeding' when attributed to an edible substance means ownership without compensation, and that is a gift. When attributed to land, whose substance is not eaten, the intended meaning is its use, so that is possible through a loan, so it is a loan. [1] His statement: 'it is a gift'; there is that it contradicts what is in al-Muhit al-Burhani, transmitted from al-Asl, that if he said 'I have fed you this food' and then said 'take possession of it', it is a gift; if he did not say 'take possession of it', it can be interpreted as a gift or a loan. [2] His statement: 'it is a loan'; although it is possible that 'feeding' when attributed to something like land could metaphorically mean ownership of the substance, this metaphor is not customary. Rather, the customary meaning is feeding the produce by way of mentioning the location and intending the produce, and the speech of a rational person must be interpreted according to custom, not according to every possibility of the wording. Thus in al-Nata'ij.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage defines the legal implications of the word 'it'ām' (feeding) when used in contracts. The author states a general rule: if 'it'ām' is attributed to food, it is a gift (hiba), but if attributed to land, it is a loan (āriyah). The commentary expands on this by explaining the reasoning: food can be owned and consumed, so 'feeding' means transferring ownership; land cannot be consumed as a substance, so 'feeding' means granting the use of the land, which is a loan. However, a detail from al-Muhit al-Burhani qualifies the first part: if someone says 'I fed you this food' without commanding 'take possession', it is ambiguous between gift and loan. The commentary resolves this by appealing to customary usage: in common parlance, 'feeding' attributed to land means feeding its produce (like fruit or grain), not the land itself. Custom ('urf) dictates interpretation, not every literal possibility. This is a Hanafi principle that custom determines meaning in ambiguous contracts.

Key Terms
الإطعام (al-it'ām): The act of feeding, used in contractual context to mean giving food or the right to use land.
الهبة (al-hibah): Gift: transfer of ownership without compensation, complete with offer and acceptance (and possession according to Hanafis).
العارية (al-'āriyah): Loan: transfer of the right to use an object without compensation, the object itself remains owned by the lender.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi law, if someone says to another 'I have fed you this land,' what does it legally mean?
A) It is a gift of the land itself.
B) It is a loan ('āriyah) of the land's use.
C) It is invalid because land cannot be eaten.
D) It is a sale of the land's produce.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ambiguous terms in contracts are interpreted according to custom, not literal possibility.

Validity of Gift by Saying 'Ja'altu Hādhā Laka'
Confirms that saying 'Ja'altu hādhā laka' is a valid gift offer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the rukn of a gift and explain why the formula 'Ja'altu hādhā laka' is valid.

Core Idea
A gift is valid by the donor's offer alone; acceptance is not a pillar.

This passage from Sharh al-Wiqāyah addresses the validity of a specific wording for a gift: 'Ja'altu hādhā laka' (I have made this for you). The Hanafi school holds that the essential pillar (rukn) of a gift is only the offer (ījāb) by the donor. Acceptance by the donee is not required for the contract's existence, though possession is a condition for the establishment of ownership. The commentary cites the example of an oath to illustrate that the offer alone completes the gift contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What legal act is being discussed? Saying 'Ja'altu hādhā laka' (I have made this for you). According to the passage, this constitutes a valid gift (sahih).
The ruling is that the gift is valid (sahih), not that ownership is immediately transferred.
2
Locate the rukn of a gift
The passage states that the rukn is simply the offer (ījāb). The donor's words indicating transfer of ownership are sufficient. Acceptance (qabūl) is not part of the rukn.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position; other schools consider acceptance a pillar.
3
Understand the role of possession
Possession (qabḍ) is a condition for the establishment of ownership (thubūt al-milk) for the donee. Without possession, the donee does not become owner, but the gift contract is still concluded.
This means ownership is not immediate upon offer; it depends on the donee taking possession.
4
Apply the oath example
If someone swears not to give a gift, and then says 'Ja'altu hādhā laka' but the donee does not accept, the donor breaks his oath. This shows the gift is concluded by the offer alone, as the donor has done what is within his control.
The oath is concerned with the donor's action, not the donee's response.

Source Text

(And I have made this for you) [3] And Shaykh al-Islam Khwāhar Zādah said: The rukn (pillar) is simply the offer. [ ] His statement: 'And I have made this for you'; i.e., a gift is valid by his saying 'I have made this thing for you' because the lām (preposition) indicates ownership, so it is as if he said 'I have made you the owner of this thing.' Do you not see that if that were with compensation, it would be a transfer of ownership, so similarly without compensation.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage confirms that the formula 'Ja'altu hādhā laka' (I have made this for you) is a valid expression for a gift (hiba) in Hanafi law. The commentary begins by quoting Shaykh al-Islam Khwāhar Zādah, who states that the only pillar (rukn) of a gift is the offer (ījāb) – i.e., the donor's words indicating transfer of ownership. The donor's declaration alone is sufficient to conclude the contract; acceptance (qabūl) by the donee is not a pillar. This is emphasized by the example of an oath: if someone swears not to give a gift, and he says 'I have made this for you' but the donee does not accept, he still breaks his oath because the gift contract is complete with the offer. The reason is that the oath binds him to what is within his control (making the offer), not the donee's acceptance. The commentary then directly addresses the matn: the word 'ja'ala' (make) with the possessive lām effects a transfer of ownership, analogous to saying 'malkatuka' (I have made you owner). The Hanafi position is that any wording that clearly indicates transfer of ownership suffices for a gift, even without the explicit word 'wahaba' (I give).

Key Terms
الركن (al-rukn): The essential component of a contract without which the contract does not exist; here, the offer (ījāb) alone is the rukn.
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): The offer: the words of the donor that indicate transfer of ownership, such as 'I have gifted' or 'I have made for you'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The gift is invalid because acceptance is a pillar.
B) The gift is valid and the donee becomes owner immediately.
C) The gift is valid but ownership requires acceptance.
D) The gift is valid but ownership requires possession.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the rukn of a gift is only the offer; acceptance is not required for the contract but possession is a condition for ownership.

Ruling of 'Umrā and Distinction from Suknā
Establishes that 'umrā is a valid gift and distinguishes it from suknā as a loan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'umrā, explain why a void condition does not invalidate the gift, and distinguish 'umrā from suknā based on the wording used.

Core Idea
'Umrā is a valid gift; the condition of return after death is void. Suknā is a loan, not ownership.

In this passage, the author establishes the Hanafi position on 'umrā (life grant) using a hadith of the Prophet ﷺ. The commentary explains that the condition of return is void because gifts are not invalidated by void conditions. The passage also distinguishes 'umrā from suknā (dwelling grant), clarifying that the use of the word 'suknā' indicates a loan of usufruct, not a transfer of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is 'umrā? A gift for life with a condition that the property returns to the grantor after the grantee's death. The ruling: the gift is valid (sahih) and the condition is void.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Whoever grants a life grant, it belongs to the grantee during his life and to his heirs after him.' This is the primary evidence.
Hadith transmitted by all major collectors except Bukhārī.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit wording 'فهي للمعمر له' indicates that the granted property becomes the property of the grantee; 'ولورثته من بعده' confirms that ownership passes to his heirs, directly contradicting the condition of return.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle on void conditions
The condition is fāsid (void) because it contradicts the essence of a gift (immediate ownership). According to Hanafi law, a gift is not invalidated by a void condition; the contract stands and the condition is ignored.
Contrast with schools that uphold the condition.
5
Distinguish 'umrā from suknā
If the grantor uses the word 'suknā' (dwelling), it is a definitive grant of usufruct only, making it a loan ('āriyah), not a gift of ownership. The word 'suknā' is decisive in limiting the right to use.
Thus, the phrasing matters: 'umrā = ownership; suknā = loan.

Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage deals with the legal classification of 'umrā (life grant) and suknā (dwelling grant). The matn provides a hadith that 'umrā is a valid gift that passes to the grantee's heirs; it is not a conditional grant that reverts to the grantor. The commentary explains that the expressions 'a'martuka' and 'ja'altu laka 'umrā' are valid forms of gift. The reasoning is that the word 'umrā implies immediate transfer of ownership (tamlik bi al-ḥāl), and any stipulation of return after the grantee's death is a void condition (sharṭ fāsid). According to the Hanafi principle, a gift is not invalidated by void conditions; the gift remains valid and the condition is disregarded. The hadith is cited as evidence. The passage then distinguishes between 'umrā and suknā: if someone says 'My house is for you as a life grant for dwelling' (using the word suknā), the word suknā is decisive in granting only the right to use (manfa'ah), so it is a loan ('āriyah), not a gift of ownership. The commentary notes that the hadith is reported by all major collectors except Bukhārī. The authority weight comes from the hadith and the Hanafi principle on void conditions.

Key Terms
العمرى (al-'umrā): A life grant: a gift of property for the duration of the grantee's life, with a void condition of return to the grantor after death.
السكنى (al-suknā): A dwelling grant: a grant of the right to live in a property, which is a loan of use ('āriyah) not ownership.
الشرط الفاسد (al-sharṭ al-fāsid): A void condition in a contract that is not contrary to the contract's essence but is legally invalid; here, the condition of return after death in a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The grantee only has the right to live in the house for his life; after his death it returns to the grantor.
B) It is a valid gift; the grantee owns the house and his heirs inherit it after him.
C) The gift is invalid because it contains a condition.
D) It is a loan ('āriyah) because of the word 'life grant'.

Key Takeaway
A life grant ('umrā) in the Hanafi school is a valid gift; any condition of return is void, and the property passes to the grantee's heirs. The word 'suknā' indicates a loan, not ownership.

The Hanafi Ruling on 'Umra: A Valid Gift with a Void Condition
Legal ruling on 'umra with proof and linguistic distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'umra (life grant) and explain why the condition of return after the donee's death is void, while distinguishing it from a mere loan (sukna).

Core Idea
'Umra is an immediate gift; the donee owns it and it passes to heirs; the condition of return is void.

The classical Hanafi texts address a common transaction: a person says to another, 'I grant you this property for your life' (a'martuka). Is this a gift, a loan, or something else? The answer hinges on the precise wording and a Prophetic hadith. In this lesson, we unpack the ruling, the proof, and a critical distinction that changes the legal nature of the transfer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Valid Expressions for 'Umra
The passage explains that the expressions 'a'martuka' and 'ja'altuha laka 'umra' are valid formulations for a gift. Note that both imply immediate transfer of ownership (tamlik) of the corpus.
These phrases contrast with other expressions that may limit the transfer to usufruct.
2
Examine the Prophetic Hadith as the Primary Proof
The hadith 'Whoever grants a life grant, it belongs to the grantee during his life and to his heirs after him' is the core evidence. Understand that the hadith explicitly attributes ownership to the donee and his heirs, not the donor.
The hadith is one of the proofs for the Hanafi position; it is narrated by Muslim, Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, and Nasa'i.
3
Analyze the Void Condition of Return
If the donor stipulates that the property returns to him after the donee's death, that condition is void (batil) because it contradicts the essence of a gift. The gift itself remains valid.
The principle: a gift is not invalidated by a void condition (shart fasid) unless the condition itself is the basis of the contract.
4
Distinguish 'Umra from Sukna (Loan for Dwelling)
When the donor adds the word 'sukna' (dwelling), the transaction becomes a loan ('ariya) because 'sukna' is definitive for usufruct, overriding the ambiguous 'laka' which could imply ownership.
This distinction shows the importance of precise wording in Islamic transactions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1] قوله: أو أعمرتكه، وجعلته لك عمري؛ أي وتصح الهبة بهذين القولين لما ذكره الشارح من الحديث؛ ولأن معنى العمري هو التمليك للحال، واشتراط الاسترداد بعد موت المعمر له فصح التمليك وبطل الشرط؛ لأنَّ الهبة لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة. قال في «الكفاية»: أعمـرتك هذا الشيء؛ أي جعلت لك هذا الشيء مدة عمرك، فإذا مت أنت، فهو لي، يقال: أعمره الدار، قال له: هي لك عمرك، ومنه: أمسكوا عليكم أموالكم لا تعمروها، فمن أعمر شيئاً فهو له، ومنه العمرى. انتهى. وقد مر ذكره فتذكر. And 'I grant you life ownership (a'martuka) and I make it for you as a life grant (ja'altuha laka 'umra). The Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever grants a life grant ('umra), it belongs to the grantee during his life and to his heirs after him.' Contrary to if he says: 'My house is for you as a life grant for dwelling (sukna).' For his saying 'sukna' makes it a loan ('ariya). As in al-Kifayah. [4] His saying: 'makes it a loan'; because the word 'sukna' is definitive in granting usufruct, and the particle of ownership in 'laka' is ambiguous, and the definitive overrides the ambiguous. It is possible that his saying 'sukna' comes as an explanation, so it specifies grant of usufruct, thus making it a loan.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the validity of 'umra (life grant) as a gift. The commentator explains that the words 'a'martuka' and 'ja'altuha laka 'umra' are valid formulations for a gift because they imply immediate transfer of ownership (tamlik) of the corpus. The condition that the property returns to the donor upon the donee's death is void (batil) because it contradicts the nature of the gift. The hadith of the Prophet ﷺ confirms that the property becomes the donee's and passes to his heirs. The key distinction is that if the donor adds the word 'sukna' (dwelling), the expression becomes ambiguous: 'sukna' is definitive for usufruct (manfa'a), while 'laka' can imply either ownership of the corpus or usufruct. Since the definitive term ('sukna') governs the ambiguous, the transaction becomes a loan ('ariya) rather than a gift. The commentator cites al-Kifayah to clarify the linguistic and legal connotations of 'umra. The ruling is part of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
العمرى (al-'umra): A type of gift where the donor says 'this is for you for your life' implying ownership during the donee's life with condition of reversion after death.
عارية ('ariya): A loan of a tangible object for usufruct without transfer of ownership; the object must be returned.
سكنى (sukna): Dwelling; a grant of the right to inhabit a house without transfer of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The transaction becomes a valid gift of the corpus with a void condition.
B) The transaction becomes a loan ('ariya) because 'sukna' is definitive for usufruct.
C) The transaction becomes invalid because of ambiguity.
D) The transaction remains a gift but the donee only has a right to dwell, not own.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a life grant ('umra) is an immediate, irrevocable gift of the corpus; only if the word 'sukna' is used does it become a non-ownership loan.

Valid Phrasings for a Gift: Distinguishing Hibah from Ariyah
Defines valid gift phrasings and classifies them as hibah or ariyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify ambiguous phrasings (e.g., 'I mounted you on this animal', 'I clothed you this garment', 'my house is a gift for you to dwell in') as a valid gift (hibah) or a loan (ariyah) based on the donor's intention and customary usage.

Core Idea
Intention and custom decide whether 'dwelling' or 'mounting' gives ownership or just usufruct.

The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah refines the understanding of gift phrasings. Words like 'mount', 'clothe', and 'dwell' are ambiguous; their legal classification depends on whether the donor intends transfer of ownership or merely usufruct. This lesson teaches you to analyze such phrasings using linguistic and customary evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrasing
Look at the words used to express the transfer. Common examples include 'I mounted you', 'I clothed you', 'my house for you to dwell'. Note the literal meaning and possible interpretations.
2
Determine literal meaning and customary usage
Check the linguistic meaning (haqiqa lughawiyya) and whether custom (urf) has given it a secondary meaning implying ownership. For example, 'clothing' in custom implies ownership, as shown in the Quranic verse on expiation (5:89).
Custom may override literal meaning.
3
Examine the donor's intention (niyyah)
The validity of the gift depends on the donor's intention. If the donor intends a gift, the phrasing is valid as a gift; otherwise it remains a loan. The phrasing 'I mounted you' is a linguistic metaphor but a customary reality when intended as gift.
Niyyah distinguishes gift from loan.
4
Classify the transaction
Based on the above, classify the transaction as hibah if ownership is intended and the wording is appropriate, or ariyah if only usufruct is intended. For example, 'dwelling as a gift' is a gift of usufruct (ariyah) because 'dwelling' specifies the usufruct.
5
Apply the rule to other phrasings
The author lists various combinations: 'dwelling as a gift', 'grant as dwelling', etc. Use the same methodology: check the specification (tamyiz) and intention to determine if it's a gift of property or a loan.
The phrasing 'or a loan as a gift as a loan' is a complex case.

Source Text

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
وحملتك على هذه الدابة بنيتها وكسوتُك هذا الثوب، وداري لك هبة تسكنها، وفي هبة سكنى (وحملتك على هذه الدابة بنيتها، وكسوتُك هذا الثوب، وداري لك هبة تسكنها، فإنَّ قوله: تسكنها ليس تميزاً، بل هو مشورة) (وفي هبة سكنَى): أي داري لك هبة سكني، فقوله: سكنى تمييز فيكون تفسيراً لما قبله، فيكون عارية. قوله: لك يحتمله والمحكم يكون قاضياً على المحتمل ويمكن أن قوله: سكني، خرج مخرج التفسير فتعين تمليك المنفعة، فيجعله عارية.
[١] قوله: وحملتك... الخ؛ أي وتصح الهبة بقوله: حملتك على هذه الدابة إذا نوى به الهبة، فإن لم ينوها به لا؛ لأنَّ الحمل في اللغة هو الإركاب، فيكون عارية، لكنَّه يحتمل الهبة عرفاً، فإنّه يقال: حمل الأمير فلاناً على فرس، ويراد به التمليك، فيحمل عليه عند نيّته، وكان هذا الحمل مجازا لغويا، وحقيقة عرفية. [٢]قوله: وكسوتك هذا الثوب؛ أي وتصح الهبة بهذا القول؛ لأنَّ الكسوة يراد به التمليك، ألا ترى إلى قوله في كفارة اليمين: أَوْ كَسَوْتُهُمْ، والكفارة لا تتعدى بالمنافع، وكذا يقال: كسى فلان فلاناً إذا ملكه لا إذا أعاره. [٣] قوله: هبة؛ ليس بقيد بل لو قال: داري لك عمرى تسكنها، كان كذلك. نص عليه في «النهاية». [٤] قوله: ليس تميزاً؛ وتفسيراً فإنَّ الفعل لا يصلح تفسيراً للاسم، فقد أشار عليه في ملكه بأن يسكنها، فإن شاءَ قبل مشورته، وإن شاء لم يقبل. [٥] قوله: مشورة؛ بتسكين الشين وفتح الواو، وبضم الشين وسكون الواو، بمعنى الشورى: وهي استخراج رأي على غالب الظن. [٦] قوله: داري لك هبة؛ فنصب الهبة على الحال أو التمييز، لما في قوله: داري لك من الإبهام.

أو سكنى هبة، أو نحلى سكنى، أو سكنى صدقة، أو صدقة عارية، أو عارية هبة عارية، وتتم بالقبض الكامل. أو سكنى هبة): أي داري لك بطريق السكنى حال كون السكنى هبة: أي موهوبة، (أو نحلى سكنى): النّحلى اسم من النحلة: أي الإعطاء تقديره نحلتها نحلة، ثمَّ قولهم: سكنى؛ تمييزاً، أو سكنى صدقة): أي داري لك بطريق السكنى حال كون السكنى صدقة، (أو صدقة عارية): أي داري لك صدقة حال كونها بطريق العارية، فعارية تمييز فُهِمَ منه المنفعة، أو عارية هبة عارية): أي داري لك بطريق العارية حال كونها هبة، فلما قال: عارية فهم منها المنفعة، فمعناه حال كون المنافع موهوبة لك.
(وتتم بالقبض الكامل) [1] قوله: وتتم بالقبض الكامل؛ لأنَّ القبض لا بُدَّ منه لثبوتِ الملك، وقال مالك: يثبت الملك فيه قبل القبض بمجرد الإيجاب والقبول، وبـه قـال أبـو ثـوره والشافعي له في القديم؛ لأنَّ الهبة إزالة ملك بغير عوض، فلا يعتبر فيها القبض، كالوصية والوقف؛ لأنها عقد ينقل الملك فلا يتوقف على القبض كالبيع. ولنا: ما رواه مالك في «الموطأ» في «كتاب القضاء»: عن ابن شهاب عن عروة بن الزبير عن عائشة رضي الله عنها زوج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إنها قالت: «إن أبا بكر الصديق كان نحلها جذ عشرين وسقا من ماله بالغابة، فلما حضرته الوفاة قال: والله يا بنية ما من الناس أحد أحب إلي غنى بعدي منك، ولا أعزُّ علي فقراً بعدي منك، وإني كنت نحلتك جذ عشرين وسقا، فلو كنت جددته كان لك، وإنما هو اليوم مال وارث، وإنما هما أخواك وأختاك، فاقتسموه على كتاب الله. قالت عائشة رضي الله عنها فقلت يا أبت والله لو كان كذا وكذا لتركته، إنما هي أسماء فمن الأخرى، قال: ذو بطن ابنة خارجة أراها وجارية... الحديث، وما روى عبد الرزاق في مصنفه عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه قال: «لا يحل إلا لمن حازه فقبضه».
Cream of the Conclusion for the Support of the Caretaking. And [if one says] 'I mounted you on this animal intending [it as a gift]' and 'I clothed you this garment' and 'my house is a gift for you to dwell in', and regarding 'a gift of dwelling': 'my house is a gift of dwelling for you', so his statement 'dwelling' is a specification, thus it explains the preceding, so it becomes a loan. His statement 'for you' is probable, and the definitive is decisive over the probable, and it is possible that his statement 'dwelling' comes as explanation, so it determines the transfer of usufruct, making it a loan.
[1] His statement 'I mounted you...' etc.: meaning the gift is valid with his statement 'I mounted you on this animal' if he intends gift thereby; if he does not intend it, then no, because mounting in language means riding, so it is a loan, but it may bear the meaning of gift customarily, for it is said 'the prince mounted so-and-so on a horse' intending transfer of ownership, so it is interpreted accordingly when he intends it, and this mounting is a linguistic metaphor and a customary reality. [2] His statement 'I clothed you this garment': meaning the gift is valid with this statement, because clothing is intended for transfer of ownership; do you not see His statement in the expiation of an oath: 'or clothe them', and expiation does not extend to usufructs, and similarly it is said 'so-and-so clothed so-and-so' when he gave him ownership, not when he lent him. [3] His statement 'gift' is not a condition, rather if he said 'my house is a lifelong gift for you to dwell in' it is likewise. This is stated in al-Nihayah. [4] His statement 'it is not a specification' but an advice, because an action cannot explain a noun, so he advised him in his property to dwell in it; if he wishes he accepts his advice, and if he wishes he does not accept. [5] His statement 'advice' with sukoon on the sheen and fatha on the waw, and with dammah on the sheen and sukoon on the waw, meaning consultation: which is extracting an opinion based on predominant probability. [6] His statement 'my house is a gift for you' so the accusative of 'gift' is as a circumstantial accusative or specification, due to the ambiguity in his statement 'my house is for you'.

Or 'a dwelling as a gift', or 'a grant as a dwelling', or 'a dwelling as charity', or 'charity as a loan', or 'a loan as a gift as a loan', and it is completed by full possession. Or 'a dwelling as a gift': meaning my house is for you by way of dwelling while the dwelling is a gift: i.e., given, (or 'a grant as a dwelling'): al-nihla is a noun from giving: i.e., giving, its estimation is 'I gave her a gift', then their statement 'dwelling' as specification, or 'a dwelling as charity': meaning my house is for you by way of dwelling while the dwelling is charity, (or 'charity as a loan'): meaning my house is for you as charity while it is by way of loan, so 'loan' is a specification from which the usufruct is understood, or 'a loan as a gift as a loan': meaning my house is for you by way of loan while it is a gift, so when he said 'loan' the usufruct is understood from it, so its meaning is while the usufructs are given to you. (And it is completed by full possession) [1] His statement 'and it is completed by full possession': because possession is necessary for the establishment of ownership. Malik said: ownership is established before possession by mere offer and acceptance, and Abu Thawr and al-Shafi'i in his old position also said this, because a gift is removal of ownership without compensation, so possession is not considered in it, like a bequest and an endowment, because it is a contract that transfers ownership, so it does not depend on possession like a sale. And for us: what Malik narrated in al-Muwatta in the Book of Judgments from Ibn Shihab from Urwa ibn al-Zubayr from A'isha, the wife of the Prophet (peace be upon him), that she said: 'Abu Bakr al-Siddiq gave her a gift of twenty wasqs of dates from his property in al-Ghabah. When his death approached, he said: By Allah, O my daughter, there is no one among people I love to be rich after me more than you, and no one I dislike to be poor after me more than you. I gave you a gift of twenty wasqs, so if you had taken possession of it, it would have been yours. Today it is the property of the heir, and they are your two brothers and two sisters, so divide it according to the Book of Allah.' A'isha said: I said, O father, by Allah, even if it were such and such, I would have left it; they are only Asma and the other one... the hadith. And what Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his Musannaf from Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'It is not lawful except for one who takes possession of it.'
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a super-commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, dealing with the definition and rulings of gifts (hibah). The author begins by discussing various phrasings that may indicate a gift or a loan (ariyah). He explains that saying 'I mounted you on this animal intending it' can be a valid gift if the donor intends it as such, because although the literal meaning is riding (a loan), customarily it can imply transfer of ownership. Similarly, 'I clothed you this garment' is valid as a gift because clothing usually implies ownership, as seen in the Quranic verse on expiation of oaths (5:89). The author then focuses on the phrase 'my house is a gift for you to dwell in'. He clarifies that the word 'dwell' here is not a specification but advice; if the donor intends a loan, it is a loan, but if advice, the recipient may accept or reject. He distinguishes between 'gift of dwelling' where 'dwelling' is a specification making it a loan of usufruct, and other phrasings like 'dwelling as a gift', 'grant as dwelling', etc., all of which are analyzed to determine whether they transfer ownership of the property or just the usufruct. The key principle is that the intention and the wording determine whether it is a gift or a loan.

The passage then moves to the condition of possession (qabd) for a gift. The author states that a gift is completed only by full possession. He reports that Imam Malik, Abu Thawr, and al-Shafi'i in his old opinion held that ownership transfers immediately by offer and acceptance without possession, analogizing it to a bequest or endowment. The Hanafi position (the author's school) requires possession, and the proof is the hadith of Aisha where Abu Bakr said that if she had taken possession (jadadtihi) it would have been hers, indicating that without possession the gift does not transfer ownership. Also, a narration from Umar emphasizes that a gift is not lawful until the recipient takes possession. Thus, in the Hanafi school, a gift is not binding and ownership does not transfer until the gift is physically taken into the recipient's possession or control (qabd kamil). This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift: a contract that transfers ownership of a property without compensation, completed by offer, acceptance, and possession.
العارية (al-ariyah): A loan: a contract that transfers the right to use (usufruct) of a property without compensation, not ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is classified as a loan (ariyah) rather than a gift (hibah)?
A) 'I mounted you on this animal' with intention of gift.
B) 'I clothed you this garment.'
C) 'My house is a gift for you to dwell in.'
D) 'I gift you my car.'

Key Takeaway
The nature of a transfer depends on wording and intention; 'dwelling' specifies usufruct, making it a loan.

The Condition of Possession in Gifts: Hanafi Position and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Articulates the requirement of full possession for gift completion and discusses the inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires full possession (qabd kamil) for the completion of a gift and evaluate the opposing proofs of Malik, Abu Thawr, and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A gift is not complete until the recipient takes possession; without it, ownership does not transfer.

The passage now turns to a crucial condition: possession (qabd). The Hanafi school insists on full possession for the gift to be binding. This lesson examines the evidence from the hadith of Aisha and Umar, and contrasts it with the views of Malik and al-Shafi'i, who argue that offer and acceptance alone transfer ownership.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule
The gift is completed only by full possession (qabd kamil). Without it, the gift is not binding and ownership does not transfer.
2
Identify the opposing view
Malik, Abu Thawr, and al-Shafi'i (old position) hold that ownership transfers immediately by offer and acceptance, without possession. They analogize to bequest and endowment.
3
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
The hadith of Aisha: Abu Bakr said 'if you had taken possession of it (jadadtihi), it would have been yours.' This implies that without possession, she did not own it. Also, Umar's statement: 'It is not lawful except for one who takes possession.'
These are ahad hadiths.
4
Evaluate the opposing analogy
Opponents argue that a gift is like a bequest or endowment, which transfer ownership without possession. The Hanafi response: bequest and endowment are death transfers or charitable trusts; they are not analogous because gift is an inter vivos transfer without compensation.
5
Determine the practical consequences
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the donor dies before possession, the gift is void in Hanafi, but valid in Maliki; the heirs cannot claim it in Maliki because ownership already passed. In Hanafi, the property returns to the donor's estate.
This affects inheritance.
