زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
وحملتك على هذه الدابة بنيتها وكسوتُك هذا الثوب، وداري لك هبة تسكنها، وفي هبة سكنى (وحملتك على هذه الدابة بنيتها، وكسوتُك هذا الثوب، وداري لك هبة تسكنها، فإنَّ قوله: تسكنها ليس تميزاً، بل هو مشورة) (وفي هبة سكنَى): أي داري لك هبة سكني، فقوله: سكنى تمييز فيكون تفسيراً لما قبله، فيكون عارية. قوله: لك يحتمله والمحكم يكون قاضياً على المحتمل ويمكن أن قوله: سكني، خرج مخرج التفسير فتعين تمليك المنفعة، فيجعله عارية.
[١] قوله: وحملتك... الخ؛ أي وتصح الهبة بقوله: حملتك على هذه الدابة إذا نوى به الهبة، فإن لم ينوها به لا؛ لأنَّ الحمل في اللغة هو الإركاب، فيكون عارية، لكنَّه يحتمل الهبة عرفاً، فإنّه يقال: حمل الأمير فلاناً على فرس، ويراد به التمليك، فيحمل عليه عند نيّته، وكان هذا الحمل مجازا لغويا، وحقيقة عرفية. [٢]قوله: وكسوتك هذا الثوب؛ أي وتصح الهبة بهذا القول؛ لأنَّ الكسوة يراد به التمليك، ألا ترى إلى قوله في كفارة اليمين: أَوْ كَسَوْتُهُمْ، والكفارة لا تتعدى بالمنافع، وكذا يقال: كسى فلان فلاناً إذا ملكه لا إذا أعاره. [٣] قوله: هبة؛ ليس بقيد بل لو قال: داري لك عمرى تسكنها، كان كذلك. نص عليه في «النهاية». [٤] قوله: ليس تميزاً؛ وتفسيراً فإنَّ الفعل لا يصلح تفسيراً للاسم، فقد أشار عليه في ملكه بأن يسكنها، فإن شاءَ قبل مشورته، وإن شاء لم يقبل. [٥] قوله: مشورة؛ بتسكين الشين وفتح الواو، وبضم الشين وسكون الواو، بمعنى الشورى: وهي استخراج رأي على غالب الظن. [٦] قوله: داري لك هبة؛ فنصب الهبة على الحال أو التمييز، لما في قوله: داري لك من الإبهام.

أو سكنى هبة، أو نحلى سكنى، أو سكنى صدقة، أو صدقة عارية، أو عارية هبة عارية، وتتم بالقبض الكامل. أو سكنى هبة): أي داري لك بطريق السكنى حال كون السكنى هبة: أي موهوبة، (أو نحلى سكنى): النّحلى اسم من النحلة: أي الإعطاء تقديره نحلتها نحلة، ثمَّ قولهم: سكنى؛ تمييزاً، أو سكنى صدقة): أي داري لك بطريق السكنى حال كون السكنى صدقة، (أو صدقة عارية): أي داري لك صدقة حال كونها بطريق العارية، فعارية تمييز فُهِمَ منه المنفعة، أو عارية هبة عارية): أي داري لك بطريق العارية حال كونها هبة، فلما قال: عارية فهم منها المنفعة، فمعناه حال كون المنافع موهوبة لك.
(وتتم بالقبض الكامل) [1] قوله: وتتم بالقبض الكامل؛ لأنَّ القبض لا بُدَّ منه لثبوتِ الملك، وقال مالك: يثبت الملك فيه قبل القبض بمجرد الإيجاب والقبول، وبـه قـال أبـو ثـوره والشافعي له في القديم؛ لأنَّ الهبة إزالة ملك بغير عوض، فلا يعتبر فيها القبض، كالوصية والوقف؛ لأنها عقد ينقل الملك فلا يتوقف على القبض كالبيع. ولنا: ما رواه مالك في «الموطأ» في «كتاب القضاء»: عن ابن شهاب عن عروة بن الزبير عن عائشة رضي الله عنها زوج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إنها قالت: «إن أبا بكر الصديق كان نحلها جذ عشرين وسقا من ماله بالغابة، فلما حضرته الوفاة قال: والله يا بنية ما من الناس أحد أحب إلي غنى بعدي منك، ولا أعزُّ علي فقراً بعدي منك، وإني كنت نحلتك جذ عشرين وسقا، فلو كنت جددته كان لك، وإنما هو اليوم مال وارث، وإنما هما أخواك وأختاك، فاقتسموه على كتاب الله. قالت عائشة رضي الله عنها فقلت يا أبت والله لو كان كذا وكذا لتركته، إنما هي أسماء فمن الأخرى، قال: ذو بطن ابنة خارجة أراها وجارية... الحديث، وما روى عبد الرزاق في مصنفه عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه قال: «لا يحل إلا لمن حازه فقبضه».
Cream of the Conclusion for the Support of the Caretaking. And [if one says] 'I mounted you on this animal intending [it as a gift]' and 'I clothed you this garment' and 'my house is a gift for you to dwell in', and regarding 'a gift of dwelling': 'my house is a gift of dwelling for you', so his statement 'dwelling' is a specification, thus it explains the preceding, so it becomes a loan. His statement 'for you' is probable, and the definitive is decisive over the probable, and it is possible that his statement 'dwelling' comes as explanation, so it determines the transfer of usufruct, making it a loan.
[1] His statement 'I mounted you...' etc.: meaning the gift is valid with his statement 'I mounted you on this animal' if he intends gift thereby; if he does not intend it, then no, because mounting in language means riding, so it is a loan, but it may bear the meaning of gift customarily, for it is said 'the prince mounted so-and-so on a horse' intending transfer of ownership, so it is interpreted accordingly when he intends it, and this mounting is a linguistic metaphor and a customary reality. [2] His statement 'I clothed you this garment': meaning the gift is valid with this statement, because clothing is intended for transfer of ownership; do you not see His statement in the expiation of an oath: 'or clothe them', and expiation does not extend to usufructs, and similarly it is said 'so-and-so clothed so-and-so' when he gave him ownership, not when he lent him. [3] His statement 'gift' is not a condition, rather if he said 'my house is a lifelong gift for you to dwell in' it is likewise. This is stated in al-Nihayah. [4] His statement 'it is not a specification' but an advice, because an action cannot explain a noun, so he advised him in his property to dwell in it; if he wishes he accepts his advice, and if he wishes he does not accept. [5] His statement 'advice' with sukoon on the sheen and fatha on the waw, and with dammah on the sheen and sukoon on the waw, meaning consultation: which is extracting an opinion based on predominant probability. [6] His statement 'my house is a gift for you' so the accusative of 'gift' is as a circumstantial accusative or specification, due to the ambiguity in his statement 'my house is for you'.

Or 'a dwelling as a gift', or 'a grant as a dwelling', or 'a dwelling as charity', or 'charity as a loan', or 'a loan as a gift as a loan', and it is completed by full possession. Or 'a dwelling as a gift': meaning my house is for you by way of dwelling while the dwelling is a gift: i.e., given, (or 'a grant as a dwelling'): al-nihla is a noun from giving: i.e., giving, its estimation is 'I gave her a gift', then their statement 'dwelling' as specification, or 'a dwelling as charity': meaning my house is for you by way of dwelling while the dwelling is charity, (or 'charity as a loan'): meaning my house is for you as charity while it is by way of loan, so 'loan' is a specification from which the usufruct is understood, or 'a loan as a gift as a loan': meaning my house is for you by way of loan while it is a gift, so when he said 'loan' the usufruct is understood from it, so its meaning is while the usufructs are given to you. (And it is completed by full possession) [1] His statement 'and it is completed by full possession': because possession is necessary for the establishment of ownership. Malik said: ownership is established before possession by mere offer and acceptance, and Abu Thawr and al-Shafi'i in his old position also said this, because a gift is removal of ownership without compensation, so possession is not considered in it, like a bequest and an endowment, because it is a contract that transfers ownership, so it does not depend on possession like a sale. And for us: what Malik narrated in al-Muwatta in the Book of Judgments from Ibn Shihab from Urwa ibn al-Zubayr from A'isha, the wife of the Prophet (peace be upon him), that she said: 'Abu Bakr al-Siddiq gave her a gift of twenty wasqs of dates from his property in al-Ghabah. When his death approached, he said: By Allah, O my daughter, there is no one among people I love to be rich after me more than you, and no one I dislike to be poor after me more than you. I gave you a gift of twenty wasqs, so if you had taken possession of it, it would have been yours. Today it is the property of the heir, and they are your two brothers and two sisters, so divide it according to the Book of Allah.' A'isha said: I said, O father, by Allah, even if it were such and such, I would have left it; they are only Asma and the other one... the hadith. And what Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his Musannaf from Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'It is not lawful except for one who takes possession of it.'

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a super-commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, dealing with the definition and rulings of gifts (hibah). The author begins by discussing various phrasings that may indicate a gift or a loan (ariyah). He explains that saying 'I mounted you on this animal intending it' can be a valid gift if the donor intends it as such, because although the literal meaning is riding (a loan), customarily it can imply transfer of ownership. Similarly, 'I clothed you this garment' is valid as a gift because clothing usually implies ownership, as seen in the Quranic verse on expiation of oaths (5:89). The author then focuses on the phrase 'my house is a gift for you to dwell in'. He clarifies that the word 'dwell' here is not a specification but advice; if the donor intends a loan, it is a loan, but if advice, the recipient may accept or reject. He distinguishes between 'gift of dwelling' where 'dwelling' is a specification making it a loan of usufruct, and other phrasings like 'dwelling as a gift', 'grant as dwelling', etc., all of which are analyzed to determine whether they transfer ownership of the property or just the usufruct. The key principle is that the intention and the wording determine whether it is a gift or a loan.

The passage then moves to the condition of possession (qabd) for a gift. The author states that a gift is completed only by full possession. He reports that Imam Malik, Abu Thawr, and al-Shafi'i in his old opinion held that ownership transfers immediately by offer and acceptance without possession, analogizing it to a bequest or endowment. The Hanafi position (the author's school) requires possession, and the proof is the hadith of Aisha where Abu Bakr said that if she had taken possession (jadadtihi) it would have been hers, indicating that without possession the gift does not transfer ownership. Also, a narration from Umar emphasizes that a gift is not lawful until the recipient takes possession. Thus, in the Hanafi school, a gift is not binding and ownership does not transfer until the gift is physically taken into the recipient's possession or control (qabd kamil). This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
القبض الكامل (al-qabd al-kamil): Full possession: actual taking control of the gifted item, necessary for completion of the gift in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence used by the Hanafi school to require possession for a gift?
A) Analogy to sale.
B) Hadith of Aisha about Abu Bakr's gift.
C) General consensus.
D) Quranic verse on almsgiving.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a gift is not binding without full possession; this distinguishes it from schools that transfer ownership by mere offer and acceptance.

Parsing Compound Gift Expressions: Sukna Hiba and Beyond
Explaining five compound gift expressions and their parsing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse compound gift expressions in Hanafi contracts and determine which term indicates the gifted item vs. the mode of transfer.

Core Idea
In a compound gift expression, the first noun often specifies the benefit; the second determines the legal category.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains how Hanafi jurists interpret five compound expressions combining terms like 'sukna', 'nuhla', 'sadaqa', 'ariya' with 'hiba'. Each combination creates a specific legal meaning. Understanding this parsing is essential for drafting clear gift contracts and avoiding ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two nouns in each compound expression
List the pairs: sukna+hiba, nuhla+sukna, sukna+sadaqa, sadaqa+ariya, ariya+hiba. Note that in Arabic the word order is first noun (specifier) then second noun (qualifier), but the legal analysis determines the relationship.
The first noun is often the benefit being transferred; the second noun casts it as a specific contract type.
2
Determine which noun is the gifted item (al-mawhub) and which is the mode (tamyiz)
Apply the author's explanations: In 'sukna hiba', the residence itself is the gift (the gifted item is sukna). In 'nuhla sukna', the grant (nuhla) is the gift and sukna is the specification (tamyiz). In 'sadaqa ariya', the charity (sadaqa) is the gift and ariya is the mode. In 'ariya hiba', the loan (ariya) indicates the benefit and hiba indicates that the benefits are gifted.
The text uses 'تمييزاً' (specification) to indicate that the second noun clarifies the type of gift.
3
Apply the principle: the tamyiz explains the mode of the gift
For each case, state what is actually transferred. For 'sukna hiba', the right of residence is transferred as a gift. For 'nuhla sukna', the residence is the subject of a grant. For 'sukna sadaqa', the residence is given as charity. For 'sadaqa ariya', the benefit is given as charity in the form of a loan. For 'ariya hiba', the benefits are gifted.
The mode affects whether ownership of the benefit is transferred (hiba) or only permission to use (ariya).
4
Summarize the legal effect of each compound
Create a concise summary: In 'sukna hiba', only usufruct is gifted; in 'nuhla sukna', residence is the subject of a gratuitous grant; in 'sukna sadaqa', residence is charity; in 'sadaqa ariya', charity but the mode is a loan so no ownership of the benefit; in 'ariya hiba', the benefits are gifted which implies transfer of ownership of the benefit.
This parsing prevents confusion between transfer of ownership of the benefit (hiba) and mere permission (ariya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو سكنى هبة، أو نحلى سكنى، أو سكنى صدقة، أو صدقة عارية، أو عارية هبة. قوله: أو سكنى هبة: أي داري لك بطريق السكنى حال كون السكنى هبة: أي موهوبة، أو نحلى سكنى: النّحلى اسم من النحلة: أي الإعطاء تقديره نحلتها نحلة، ثم قولهم: سكنى؛ تمييزاً، أو سكنى صدقة: أي داري لك بطريق السكنى حال كون السكنى صدقة، أو صدقة عارية: أي داري لك صدقة حال كونها بطريق العارية، فعارية تمييز فُهِمَ منه المنفعة، أو عارية هبة عارية: أي داري لك بطريق العارية حال كونها هبة، فلما قال: عارية فهم منها المنفعة، فمعناه حال كون المنافع موهوبة لك.
Or 'residence as a gift', or 'grant of residence', or 'residence as charity', or 'charity as a loan', or 'loan as a gift'. His saying: 'or residence as a gift': meaning my house is yours by way of residence while the residence is a gift: i.e., gifted. 'Or grant of residence': 'nuhla' is derived from 'nahl' meaning giving; its interpretation is 'I grant you a grant'; then they say 'residence' as a specification. 'Or residence as charity': meaning my house is yours by way of residence while the residence is charity. 'Or charity as a loan': meaning my house is charity for you while it is by way of loan, so 'loan' is a specification from which benefit is understood. 'Or loan as a gift': meaning my house is yours by way of loan while it is a gift; when he said 'loan', benefit is understood from it, so its meaning is that the benefits are gifted to you.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage begins by listing several compound expressions used in contracts related to gifts (hiba). Each expression combines a type of benefit (residence, charity, loan) with the word 'gift' or 'grant'. The author then explains each phrase to clarify the precise meaning. For instance, 'sukna hiba' (residence as a gift) means the actual right of residence is the gifted item, not the house itself. In 'nuhla sukna' (grant of residence), 'nuhla' explicitly indicates a grant, and 'sukna' specifies the subject. When 'sadaqa' (charity) is combined with 'ariya' (loan), the charity takes effect but the mode is a loan, so only the benefit is transferred. Finally, 'ariya hiba' (loan as a gift) makes it clear that the benefits (manafi) are gifted. The key is identifying which term carries the actual transfer of ownership (hiba, nuhla, sadaqa) and which specifies the type of benefit (sukna, ariya). This careful parsing is typical of Hanafi legal analysis to avoid ambiguity in contracts.

Key Terms
سكنى (sukna): The right to reside in a property; a benefit (manfa'ah) that can be gifted.
نحلى (nuhla): A grant or gift, from the root 'nahl' meaning giving without compensation.
عارية (ariya): A loan of a benefit (usufruct) without transfer of ownership; the tamyiz (specifier) here indicates the mode.
تمييز (tamyiz): Specification; a grammatical term used here to indicate that the noun clarifies the mode or type of gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The house itself
B) The right of residence (sukna)
C) Both the house and the right of residence

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of compound gift expressions in Hanafi fiqh depends on correctly identifying which term is the gifted item and which specifies the mode of transfer.

Gift Completed Only with Complete Possession (Qabd Kamil)
Ruling that gift ownership requires complete possession, with proof and intra-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that ownership of a gift does not transfer without complete possession (qabd kamil), explain the proofs from the actions of Abu Bakr and Umar, and distinguish between the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on what constitutes possession.

Core Idea
A gift does not transfer ownership until the donee takes complete possession (qabd kamil); mere offer and acceptance is insufficient.

This lesson explores why the Hanafi school insists that a gift (hiba) is not fully effective until the donee physically takes possession of the item. We will examine the hadith of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, the saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab, the rational argument based on the gratuitous nature of gifts, and the nuanced conditions for 'complete possession'. The lesson also addresses the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether mere vacating (takhliya) constitutes possession, and the distinction between a gifted item that occupies the donor's property versus one that is occupied by it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage begins with 'وتتم بالقبض الكامل' (and it is completed with complete possession). Understand that possession (qabd) is a condition (shart) for ownership to transfer in a gift.
The condition is not for the validity of the contract itself, but for the transfer of ownership.
2
Examine the opposing view and its proof
Malik, Abu Thawr, and al-Shafi'i (old view) hold that ownership transfers upon offer and acceptance without possession, by analogy to bequest (wasiyya) and endowment (waqf). Understand the analogy and why the Hanafi school rejects it.
The analogy fails because bequest and endowment involve death or separation of ownership; gift is a contract among the living.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil naqli)
The hadith of Abu Bakr and Aisha shows that Abu Bakr considered the gift of twenty wasqs incomplete because Aisha had not taken possession before his death. Also the saying of Umar: 'لا يحل إلا لمن حازه فقبضه'. These prove that possession is required.
These are actions and statements of the Companions, which the Hanafi school considers strong proof.
4
Understand the rational argument (dalil aqli)
A gift is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'); if ownership were established immediately, the donor could be compelled to deliver, contradicting the voluntary nature. Also, ownership without the ability to dispose is useless.
This argument distinguishes gift from sale, where delivery is obligatory.
5
Apply the conditions of complete possession
Complete possession (qabd kamil) requires that the donee can fully take control without impediment from the donor's property. The passage distinguishes between the gifted item occupying the donor's property (shaghl – valid) and the gifted item being occupied by the donor's property (mashghul – invalid). Also, intra-Hanafi: Abu Yusuf requires actual seizure (hiyaza); Muhammad deems takhliya (vacating) sufficient.
Examples: giving a sack containing food (invalid) vs. giving food in a sack (valid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتتم بالقبض الكامل. قوله : وتتم بالقبض الكامل؛ لأنَّ القبض لا بُدَّ منه لثبوتِ الملك، وقال مالك : يثبت الملك فيه قبل القبض بمجرد الإيجاب والقبول ، وبه قال أبو ثور والشافعي له في القديم ; لأنَّ الهبة إزالة ملك بغير عوض ، فلا يعتبر فيها القبض ، كالوصية والوقف ; لأنها عقد ينقل الملك فلا يتوقف على القبض كالبيع. ولنا : ما رواه مالك في «الموطأ» في «كتاب القضاء»: عن ابن شهاب عن عروة بن الزبير عن عائشة رضي الله عنها زوج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إنها قالت : «إن أبا بكر الصديق كان نحلها جاد عشرين وسقا من ماله بالغابة، فلما حضرته الوفاة قال : والله يا بنية ما من الناس أحد أحب إلي غنى بعدي منك ، ولا أعزُّ علي فقراً بعدي منك، وإني كنت نحلتك جاد عشرين وسقا، فلو كنت جددته كان لك ، وإنما هو اليوم مال وارث، وإنما هما أخواك وأختاك، فاقتسموه على كتاب الله. قالت عائشة رضي الله عنها فقلت يا أبت والله لو كان كذا وكذا لتركته ، إنما هي أسماء فمن الأخرى ، قال : ذو بطن ابنة خارجة أراها وجارية... الحديث ، وما روى عبد الرزاق في مصنفه عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه قال : «لا يحل إلا لمن حازه فقبضه». [1] أي تتم الهبة بالقبض الكامل الممكن في الموهوب للموهوب له ، فالقبض الكامل في المنقول ما يناسبه، وفي العقار ما يُناسبه. ولأنَّ الهبة عقد تبرع ، ولو ثبت الملك فيه بلا قبض لتوجهت المطالبة على المتبرع بالتسليم، كما في البيع، فيؤدّي إلى إيجاب ما تبرع به ، وهو خلاف موضوع التبرعات ، بخلاف المعاوضات، فإن قيل : الملك يقع على وجه لا يوجب التسليم، أجيب بأنه لا فائدة فيه حينئذ، إذ فائدة الملك التمكن من التصرف. [1]قوله : بالقبض ; أي الحيازة ; وهي أن يصير الشيء في حيز القابض، فظهر أنَّ التخلية أي التمكن من الحيازة لم يكن قبضاً، وهذا عند أبي يوسف رح خلافاً لمحمد رح. [2] قوله : بالقبض الكامل ; ولو كان الموهوب شاغلاً لملك الواهب لا مشغولاً به، قال في «الفصول العمادية» في «فتاوى ظهير الدين» له : هبة الشاغل تجوز، وهبة المشغول لا تجوز. والأصل في جنس هذه المسائل : إنَّ اشتغال الموهوب بملك الواهب يمنع تمام الهبة ; لأنَّ القبض شرط ، وأمَّا اشتغالُ ملك الواهب بالموهوب فلا يمنع تمامَ الهبة ، مثاله : وهب جراباً فيه طعام لا يجوز، ولو وهب طعاماً في جراب جاز، وعلى هذا نظائره. وفي «الزيادات» المنسوب إلى القاضي أبي جعفر : لـو وهـب دابة مُسَرَّجَةً وسَلَّمَها كذلك لم يجز الهبة، وبمثله لو وهب اللجامَ والسرج دون الدابة وسلّمها كذلك جاز ; لأنَّ الدابة تصير مشغولة بالسرج واللجام ، والسرج واللجامُ لا يصير مشغولاً بالدابة، وعلى هذا الرهن. واشتغال الموهوب بملك غير الواهب، هل يمنع تمام الهبة؟ ذكر صاحب «المحيط» : في «الباب الأول من هبة» «الزيادات»: إنّه لا يمنع ، فإنه قال : لو أعار داراً من إنسان ثمَّ إنَّ المستعير غصب متاعاً ووضعه في الدار، ثم وهب المعير الدار من المستعير صحتِ الهبة في الدار. وكذلك لو أنَّ المعير هو الذي غصب المتاع ووضعه في الدار ، ثمّ وهب المعير من
And it (the gift) is completed with complete possession. His saying: 'And it is completed with complete possession' because possession is indispensable for the establishment of ownership. Malik said: Ownership is established in it before possession by mere offer and acceptance, and Abu Thawr and al-Shafi'i in his old view said the same; because the gift is a removal of ownership without compensation, so possession is not considered in it, like a bequest and an endowment; because it is a contract that transfers ownership, so it does not depend on possession like a sale. Our proof: what Malik narrated in al-Muwatta in the Book of Judgement from Ibn Shihab from Urwa ibn al-Zubayr from Aisha, the wife of the Prophet (peace be upon him), that she said: 'Abu Bakr al-Siddiq had granted me twenty wasqs of his property in al-Ghaba. When death approached, he said: By Allah, my daughter, there is no one after me I would rather see rich than you, and no one after me I find harder to see poor than you. I had granted you twenty wasqs. If you had taken possession of it, it would be yours. Today it is the property of the heirs. Your two brothers and two sisters, so divide it according to the Book of Allah.' Aisha said: I said, O father, by Allah, even if it were such and such, I would have left it. (She said): It was only Asma, so who is the other? He said: Dhul Batn, the daughter of Kharidja, I think... the hadith. And what Abd al-Razzaq narrated in his Musannaf from Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'It is not permissible except for one who has taken it in possession and seized it.' [1] That is, the gift is completed with the complete possession possible in the gifted item for the donee. Complete possession in movables is what befits them, and in immovables what befits them. And because the gift is a gratuitous contract; if ownership were established in it without possession, then the giver would be required to deliver, as in a sale, leading to the enforcement of what he donated, which contradicts the nature of gratuitous transfers, unlike commutative contracts. If it is said: Ownership occurs in a manner that does not require delivery, the answer is that there is no benefit in it then, because the benefit of ownership is the ability to dispose. [1] His saying: 'with possession' means seizure, meaning that the thing comes into the possession of the seizer. It becomes clear that vacating (takhliya), i.e., enabling seizure, is not possession. This is the view of Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him), contrary to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). [2] His saying: 'with complete possession' applies if the gifted item is occupied (shaghl) in the owner's property, not occupied by it. He said in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya in Fatawa Zahir al-Din: The gift of something that occupies (the donor's property) is valid, but the gift of something that is occupied (by the donor's property) is not valid. The principle in these matters is: The occupation of the gifted item by the owner's property prevents the completion of the gift, because possession is a condition; but the occupation of the owner's property by the gifted item does not prevent the completion of the gift. Example: If he gives a sack containing food, it is not valid; if he gives food in a sack, it is valid. And similar cases follow. In al-Ziyadat attributed to Qadi Abu Ja'far: If he gives a saddled horse and delivers it as such, the gift is not valid. Similarly, if he gives the bridle and saddle without the horse and delivers them as such, it is valid, because the horse becomes occupied by the saddle and bridle, but the saddle and bridle do not become occupied by the horse. The same applies to mortgage. As for the gifted item being occupied by the property of someone other than the donor, does it prevent the completion of the gift? The author of al-Muhit mentioned in the first chapter of Gift in al-Ziyadat that it does not prevent it. He said: If someone lends a house to a person, then the borrower wrongfully takes goods and places them in the house, then the lender gives the house as a gift to the borrower, the gift of the house is valid. Similarly, if the lender is the one who wrongfully took the goods and placed them in the house, then the lender gives (the house as a gift) to the borrower...
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage establishes a core Hanafi ruling: a gift (hiba) is not complete and ownership does not transfer until the donee takes full possession (qabd kamil) of the gifted item. The matn states 'وتتم بالقبض الكامل' (and it is completed with complete possession). The commentator explains that possession is a necessary condition for ownership to be established. This is in opposition to Imam Malik and the old view of al-Shafi'i, who held that ownership transfers immediately upon offer and acceptance, analogizing gift to bequest and endowment. The Hanafi proof comes from the practice of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq: he had granted Aisha twenty wasqs of dates, but because she had not taken possession before his death, he considered it part of the estate, not her property. Also, the saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab that something is only lawful for the one who has taken it in possession. The commentator then provides a rational argument: a gift is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'); if ownership were established without possession, the donor could be compelled to deliver, which would negate the voluntary nature of gifts. If it is argued that ownership could be established without requiring delivery, the answer is that such ownership would be useless because the owner could not dispose of it. The passage then clarifies the definition of qabd: it is actual seizure (hiyaza), not mere takhliya (vacating the property). This is the position of Abu Yusuf, while Muhammad considers takhliya sufficient for possession. Finally, the commentator discusses the condition of 'complete possession' in relation to whether the gifted item is occupied by the donor's property. If the item is 'occupied' (mashghul) by the donor's property, the gift is invalid because possession cannot be fully taken; but if the item is 'occupying' (shaghl) the donor's property, it is valid. Examples include a sack with food (invalid if the sack is given with food inside) and a saddled horse (invalid if the saddle is in the way). However, if the gifted item is occupied by a third party's property, it does not prevent the gift's validity, as shown in the example of a house with goods placed by a borrower.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabd): Physical possession or seizure of the gifted item; actual control, not merely enabling the donee to take it.
التخلية (al-takhliya): Vacating or leaving the property empty so that the donee can take possession; not considered qabd by Abu Yusuf.
شاغل / مشغول (shaghl / mashghul): Shaghl: the gifted item occupies the donor's property (e.g., food in a sack). Mashghul: the gift is occupied by the donor's property (e.g., sack containing food). The gift is valid only in the first case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when does ownership of a gifted item transfer to the donee?
A) Immediately upon offer and acceptance
B) Only after the donee takes complete possession (qabd kamil)
C) After a judicial decree
D) Only after the donor's death

Key Takeaway
A gift does not transfer ownership until the donee takes complete possession (qabd kamil); this is proven by the practice of the Companions and the nature of gratuitous contracts.

Completion of Gift by Possession: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Explaining the requirement of full possession for gift validity and the internal Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires physical possession (qabd) for gift completion and distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on takhliya.

Core Idea
A gift is completed only by actual possession, not mere enabling, per the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This lesson examines the requirement of possession (qabd) for the validity of a gift (hiba). The author argues that without possession, the gratuitous nature of a gift would be compromised. We then explore an internal Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad over whether enabling the donee (takhliya) is sufficient or whether actual seizure is needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The passage states that a gift is completed by full possession. Identify the act (hiba) and the condition (qabd) that completes it.
The condition is possession, not just offer and acceptance.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Trace the analogy with sale: if ownership were established without possession, the donor would be obligated to deliver, contradicting the gratuitous nature of gifts.
This qiyas shows why possession is necessary to preserve the nature of gift.
3
Define the core dispute
What is qabd? Abu Yusuf says actual physical seizure; Imam Muhammad says takhliya (enabling) is sufficient. The passage clarifies that takhliya is not possession according to Abu Yusuf.
Both views are within the Hanafi school.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Yusuf, requiring actual seizure. This is the opinion adopted in Zahir al-Riwayah and preferred by later scholars.
For practical fatwa, actual possession is required.

Source Text

أي تتم الهبة بالقبض الكامل الممكن في الموهوب للموهوب لـه ، فالقبض الكامل في المنقول ما يناسبه، وفي العقار ما يُناسبه ولأنَّ الهبة عقد تبرع ، ولو ثبت الملك فيه بلا قبض لتوجهت المطالبة على المتبرع بالتسليم، كما في البيع، فيؤدّي إلى إيجاب ما تبرع به ، وهو خلاف موضوع التبرعات ، بخلاف المعاوضات، فإن قيل : الملك يقع على وجه لا يوجب التسليم، أجيب بأنه لا فائدة فيه حينئذ، إذ فائدة الملك التمكن من التصرف.
[1]قوله : بالقبض ؛ أي الحيازة ؛ وهي أن يصير الشيء في حيز القابض، فظهر أنَّ التخلية أي التمكن من الحيازة لم يكن قبضاً، وهذا عند أبي يوسف رح خلافاً لمحمد رح.
Meaning: The gift is completed by full possession that is possible for the gifted object by the donee. Full possession of movable property is what is suitable for it, and of immovable property is what is suitable for it. And because the gift is a gratuitous contract; if ownership were established without possession, then the donor would be demanded to deliver, as in sale, which would necessitate making the gratuitous act obligatory, contrary to the nature of gratuitous contracts. If it is said: 'Ownership occurs in a manner that does not obligate delivery,' the answer is that then there is no benefit in it, for the benefit of ownership is the ability to dispose. [1] His saying: 'by possession' means physical seizure, which is that the thing enters the sphere of the taker. Thus it becomes clear that takhliya, i.e., enabling seizure, is not possession. This according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, contrary to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the requirement of possession (qabd) for the completion of a gift (hiba). The author explains that a gift is a voluntary contract; if ownership were transferred without possession, the donor could be compelled to deliver the object, which contradicts the voluntary nature of gifts. This is unlike a sale, where delivery is obligatory. The text also distinguishes between movable and immovable property regarding what constitutes 'full possession'. Then the commentator notes a difference between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad: according to Abu Yusuf, merely enabling possession (takhliya) is not sufficient; actual physical seizure is required. Imam Muhammad holds that takhliya itself is possession. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Yusuf, as found in the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabd): Physical seizure or taking possession of the gifted object, bringing it into the sphere of the donee.
التخلية (al-takhliya): Enabling the donee to take possession, i.e., removing obstacles and making the object available.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what constitutes possession (qabd) for a gift?
A) The donor's offer and the donee's acceptance
B) Takhliya, i.e., enabling the donee to take possession
C) Actual physical seizure of the gifted object
D) Written documentation

Key Takeaway
A gift is not legally binding in the Hanafi school until the donee takes actual physical possession of the gifted object.
